We have already spoken in the First Chapter about every disease in which it is appropriate to use fire cautery and burning medicine, their indications, causes, instruments, and shapes of cauteries.
And I set forth there chapters from head to foot.
And I will proceed in this chapter along the same way, so that the seeker may find what he is looking for.
And before I begin this, O sons, it is necessary that you learn that what is in this chapter about cautery is even more terrifying than what is in the first chapter.
And therefore it is necessary that caution be even greater in this, since in the operation which is in this chapter, there often occurs evacuation with blood, with which life stands at the opening of a vein, or incision over an abscess, or opening of an exit, or cure of a wound, or extraction of an arrow, or incision over a stone, and similar things: from all of these trembling and fear arise, and many of them result in death.
And I warn you not to fall into that about which there is hesitation, for sometimes people with various conditions come to you in this art. Among them is he who becomes angry with his illness and finds death light: because of the vehemence of what he has found from his illness and the length of his wickedness. And with the illness, there is fear that signifies death.
Among them is he who offers his wealth and shares it with you, hoping for health, but his illness is deadly.
Therefore, do not consent to him who comes to you from among them whose way is like this. And let your caution be greater than your greed and desire for gain. And do not presume upon anything from him unless after certain knowledge which proves among you that he has achieved worthy success.
And use in the care of all your patients prognosis and indication about that towards which health is suitably directed.
In that there is for you assistance towards acquiring fame and glory, and remembrance and praise.
May God inspire you, O sons, with His understanding, and not take away from you guidance and inspiration: for that is in His hand.
And indeed I have arranged this chapter by dividing it into chapters according to what preceded in the chapter on Cautery, from the neck to the feet: so that you may find easily what you seek from it and what you need.
On the treatment of water which collects in the heads of children.
Chapter 1.
You should know that this illness often happens to children at birth or when the midwife tightly squeezes the child's head without gentleness.
And sometimes it also happens due to an unknown cause which is not known.
I myself have not seen this illness in anyone other than children, and death hastened to all whom I saw with it. Therefore, it seemed to me necessary to leave the operation on that.
I myself saw among them a boy whose head was filled with water and swelled every day until he could no longer sit up due to the size of his head. The fluid increased until he died.
This fluid either collects between the skin and bone or collects under the bone over the forehead.
As for the operation on this [condition], if there is fluid between the skin and bone and the abscess is small, then you should make a single slit in the middle of the head's seam with a width of about two joints until the fluid flows out.
This is the form of the operation.
If however the fluid has increased more and the abscess is larger, then make two incisions following this pattern.
But if the fluid is under the bone and its sign is that you see the sutures of the head opened on all sides, and when you press it with your hand towards the interior, water appears and does not hide from you: then you should make three incisions in the middle of the head according to this manner.
After the incision, remove all the fluid. Then close the incisions with bandages and lint. Then dress it from above with wine and oil until the fifth day.
Then remove the bandage and treat the wound with linens and ointments.
And do not leave the head bound unevenly. Feed the patient with dry food of little moisture until the member is strengthened and healed.
And another method of incision is to observe where the size of the abscess and collection of water appear.
Because sometimes it is more in the occiput, or forehead, or on the right or left side.
Therefore, make your incision where you see the abscess and accumulation of water appear, and cut it according to what is possible for you.
And observe that you do not cut the artery, lest there be a flow of blood and the patient dies from that flow along with the evacuation of humidity.
Concerning the Incision of Arteries which are behind the ears.
Chapter 2.
If purulent matter occurs around the eyes or chest of someone, and it becomes chronic without being helped by medical treatment, then the ultimate remedy for that is the incision of these two arteries.
Therefore, when you wish to cut them, it is necessary that the patient uncover his head. Then rub the place with a coarse cloth so that the arteries become visible. Then let the patient tie his neck with any of his clothes. Then observe where the vein pulsates. And these places are located behind the ears and depressed; they are rarely concealed unless in certain individuals.
Then mark both places with cauterization and cut each with a small, sharp instrument on either side until it reaches the bone, and let this be done along the width of the head.
If you wish, insert a small instrument beneath the artery and cut it upward with an incision.
And let the length of the incision be about two fingers joined together.
For when the vein is cut, blood issues forth in a pulsating manner, which flows upwards rapidly and frequently into the inner parts.
But if the artery does not appear visibly, then it is necessary to measure from the ear the quantity of space equal to three fingers. Then mark with cauterization and feel for it until you reach the bone.
And what ought to be emitted from the blood is about six ounces, although you may emit more or less than that. All this depends on the quantity which appears to you according to the strength of the patient and the fullness of his artery.
Then observe the wound. If anything remains attached to the bone from the cutting, then cut that off so that a hot abscess does not occur; then tie the wound with bandages made of linen cloth.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals.
And on extracting arterial vessels found in temples.
Chapter III.
When an old headache or sharp catarrh occurs due to acrimonious humidities and heat in the temples' nerves, or old soda and similar things; and it is treated by medicinal methods of treatment without effect: then we have tried extraction of arterial vessels from the temples, or cauterization of them, as described.
And the method of operation for extracting them is: let the sick person shave the hair on the temples, and direct attention to the visible artery in the temple. Indeed this [artery] becomes evident by its pulsation, and is rarely concealed unless in a few men or due to extreme cold.
But if it is not evident to you, then let the sick person bind his head with any cloth he has; then rub the place with a coarse cloth. Or warm the place with hot steam until its appearance becomes manifest. Then take the scalpel which I made earlier. Then gently scrape off the skin with it until you reach the artery. Then insert a hook into it and extract it upwards until you extract it from the skin, and remove it from all its surrounding tissues.
If however the artery is slender: then envelop it with the tip of the hook. Then cut off a piece from it according to how much its ends extend and contract, so that bleeding does not occur. For if it is not cut or severed, blood will not flow upwards at all.
Then express six drops of blood from it using the hook, up to three times.
But if the artery is large: then it is necessary to ligate it in two places with a strong double thread made of silk or horsehair, so that decay does not reach it before the wound is consolidated and bleeding occurs.
Then cut the superfluity which is between the two ligatures. Do that at that hour or another hour afterward.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a knife cautery until its ends are cut off and remains in the place of the operation.
But this method is better unless the patient has a fever or is of hot temperament. For cautery is among those things which help to dry moistures. So it is the better remedy.
And after the removal of the arteries, it is necessary that the place be filled with old cooked wax and powders properly made be put upon it.
And after the dressing, treat it with dry medicines that promote flesh growth and with linen until it heals.
But if during the operation blood flows from the artery, then quickly cut it with a cautery or fill the place with wax and press your hand until the bleeding stops. But if none of these are present, then put your finger on the place until the blood coagulates. And apply cold water to the place until the pain subsides and the dressing is applied properly.
And for a lighter and easier removal of the artery, cauterize the vein with this cautery having two knives, the sharpness of both knives being like an almadat. But they are much less sharp than the two knives. For if they are as sharp as the two knives, both will become cold quickly and will not cut the flesh quickly. Therefore, since they have some thickness, the heat of the fire is retained in them and the flesh is cut quickly. Therefore this operation is better than any other, lighter, and easier.
And this is the shape of the cautery.
And let the space between two cauterizing irons be as thick as a finger. After you mark two places with the hot iron, set them aside until they reach the bone, and strike the vein in both places so that it may stretch out what is between its ends. For these cauteries do not completely harden.
Chapter IV
On the treatment of frequent and sharp pains in the eyes.
When there are frequent tears and their course is from the veins that are on the outside of the skull of the head, and no cure can be brought about by medicine with medicines; and you see the face of the patient beginning to redden, and he feels a creeping sensation in his forehead, like the crawling of ants, and his eyes are sunken and moist, so that even the lashes have become rotted away through moisture; and the eyelids are hot from the acuteness of the tears: then the ancients needed this operation for their cure, which they called 'operation with a knife'.
And first, you should order the patient to shave the hairs from his forehead. Then make three incisions in the forehead equally spaced along its length. Each incision should be about two fingers long, and one should be equidistant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second a little further along towards the temple, and the third on the other side. Be careful not to cut into the arteries that are on both sides, and keep your hand away from the continuation of the two jaws. The space of each incision should be as wide as three joined fingers. Have ready some dry sponges or many pieces of cloth with which you can wipe away the blood.
Then insert a probe with two sharp ends, whose shape is as follows: Starting from the incision on the temple side, go up to the second incision and strip off with it all the skin that is between the two incisions together with the pericranium that is over the bone. And do likewise from the middle incision to the second incision. Then remove this probe and insert into the first incision another instrument called the scalpel, sharp on one side and blunt on the other. This is its shape:
And hold its sharp edge towards the flesh attached to the skin and its blunt edge towards the bone, and push it until it reaches the middle incision. Then cut with it all vessels that descend from the head to the eyes, except for those which are continuous with the incision up to where the skin appears.
Then do likewise from the middle incision to the other incision.
And after an equal amount of blood has flowed out, squeeze the places with pieces of dried blood.
Then place in every incision a linen cloth from old cotton and put over them poultices soaked in wine, oil, or vinegar. And make sure no hot abscess forms.
On the third day dissolve the suture and apply a lot of warm water rinse. Then treat it with balsam ointment and other remedies used for wounds until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On treating sharp pains and cataracts in the eyes from within the head.
When sharp, biting, persistent cataracts occur to someone: and you see their eyes wasted away, small, and the appearance of them weakened; and the eyelids ulcerated, and hairs falling out from them, and there is sharp pain in the depth of the head, causing injury, and coughing follows: then know that these accidents happen because those materials, and cataracts do not come unless from many veins.
And the best treatment for these is this. And it is to order the patient to shave his forehead. Then make one incision in the middle of the forehead, or slightly longer across its width, and begin cutting from the left temple to the right temple; let the incision be until it reaches the bone. And extend your hand away from the temples which move during mastication until when you expose the bone. Then dry up all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate between the two lips of the wound using a boiled old cloth or linen strips. Then tie it from above with wool. And moisten the wool with wine and oil so that no hot abscess occurs.
And when you open it, and you see that the hot abscess has diminished, then you must scrape the bone until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with a drying regimen that promotes flesh growth: thus if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony, and prepare an ointment from them, it should be applied to the birth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter Six.
On treating what falls into the Ear.
Every object that falls into the ear is of one of four kinds: either a mineral stone, or something like stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as chickpeas and beans; or it is liquid, such as water and vinegar and things like them; or some kind of animal.
When a stone falls into the ear, or some other substance that does not adhere and grow within it, expose the ear to the sunlight. If you see the stone, then pour a little violet oil or rosewater into the ear. Then administer the removal of it [the stone], and cause sneezing with condiments, and hold a cloth against the nostrils upon approach of sneezing, after which you place pieces of linen or wool around the ear and pull the ear upwards.
For indeed many times does it exit with this treatment.
But if it does not exit, then administer its removal with the Gestum, that is, with delicate tweezers; the shape of which is like this.
If therefore it exits with the Gestum, it is good.
And if not, then administer its removal with a delicate bent hook of few turns.
If it does not exit through that [the hook], then make a cannula of brass and insert its end into the opening of the ear far in, and seal what is around the cannula with softened wax mixed with oil so that there may be no way for wind except through the cannula: then extract it with strong suction of your breath.
For indeed many times does it exit with what we have mentioned. But if it does not exit this way: then take a little from the helkalimbat or from dough, that is, prepared gluten with which birds are caught; and place it on the end of a rod after you have wrapped it with a cloth well placed. Then insert it gently into the opening of the ear after you have dried the ear.
If therefore it does not exit with all that we have mentioned, then hurry to cut before hot inflammation or spasm occurs.
And the method of cutting is such that the physician should first perform phlebotomy on the patient at the cephalic vein and remove blood according to his strength. Then make the patient sit between your hands, turn their ear towards the upper parts and find a small incision in the root of the ear at its depressed place. Make the incision crescent-shaped until you reach the stone, then remove it together with what is possible using your instruments.
Then quickly stitch up the wound with care, until it heals.
But if there is a foreign body in the ear like a grain that grows and swells, then perform its removal.
If it does not obey you for exit, then take a thin, fine hook in this shape:
And use it to cut off that kind of grain which falls into the ear. Do not do this when you think that the grain has already been moistened by the vapor of the ear until you put in many pieces. Then remove them with hooks or a fine pair of forceps, or with suction as we have said, for its removal will be facilitated.
But concerning water that enters the ear, the patient should use sneezing first. Then fill the ear with some warm old oil and lie down on the side of the ear where there is water. If therefore it comes out through that [means], it is good.
And if not, then take many stones of the length and thickness of a finger, soften them with heat for a short time, and have the patient insert one of them into the opening of his ear. Then let him stand on one foot on that side and strike the inserted stone gently with another stone from outside. Do not cease doing this with stone and stone until all the water comes out.
And now the water comes out thus, that you may take one from a feather or from a reed and insert one end into the ear, and light the other end with fire until it burns greatly. Then repeat this with another reed many times, until all the water comes out. And remove it with a cannula as has been described previously on the stone.
But in the removal of an animate creature that has entered it, if it is small-bodied, like a flea or similar, treat it as I have said in division.
But if it is large-bodied and apparent to the senses, then perform its removal with hooks and forceps. And this task is easier than all those held in the ear.
But in the removal of a worm born in the ear, when you treat it with what has been said in division and in the treatise on distillation, but your treatment does not succeed: then it is necessary that you look at the ear towards the sun. If therefore something from the worms appears there, then remove it with forceps or with fine hooks.
But if nothing appears there, then take a cannula of this shape, narrow below and wide above. And insert its fine end into the ear according to how much the sick person can tolerate it, and then suck with strong suction. Do this many times until all the worms come out.
But if they do not obey you to come out, then seal around the cannula with wax as I have said on the stone.
If they do not come out with what has been said, then perform the distillations that the ancients have proven effective in killing worms, and you will find this in the treatise on distillation.
And let your pouring be such when you pour oil and medicines into the ear with this instrument, whose shape is depicted here. As you see, make it from silver or bronze, narrow below with a small hole, wide above.
Therefore, let the plunger that fits into the concave part of the cannula be made of well-crafted brass. If you wish, take a wand and wrap cotton around its tip in an appropriate wrapping. Then pour oil or juice or any of the medicines you wish into the cannula, with it placed in the ear. Next, insert the wand with the cotton onto it from above, and press it gently with your hand until the oil is pushed into the concavity of the ear, so that the patient feels its warmth inside. And whatever is dripped into the ear should be warmed a little with fire, and be careful that nothing very cold distills into it, for medicines do not tolerate this.
Chapter 7
On the treatment of accidental perforation of the ear.
Sometimes infants are born from their mothers' wombs without being pierced.
And it has happened that some people have had blockage of the ear's passage due to injury or flesh grown within it.
And this blockage sometimes occurs deep inside the ear canal where it cannot be seen, and at other times it happens near the opening of the ear canal where it can be seen.
Therefore, blockage that occurs deep inside the ear canal and cannot be seen is difficult to treat in most cases.
And with the one that can be seen, you should place the patient's ear in sunlight and look into it. If you see an apparent blockage, open it with a fine spatula, whose shape is as follows: its tip should have a small width, part of which is sharp; the rest of the spatula should have two smooth sides so that it does not harm the ear.
If the blockage is due to flesh grown within the ear canal, then grasp it with a fine hook and cut it away with great ease until all the flesh is removed.
But if the blockage is deep within the ear, then take a thin, gentle probe and heat it slightly with fire. Then insert it into the ear canal.
If you find a blockage with what caused the patient's hearing difficulty, try to remove it gently; and be careful not to injure the nerves of the ear.
Then place a wick made from linen according to the size of the wound's opening into the ear, soaked in Egyptian ointment. Do this for several days until you are sure that the wound has healed properly.
Or take a wick and soak it in rosewater, then dust it with powdered saffron, and use it as such.
But if you see that the ear has begun to swell, then remove the wick and allow it to rest with another wick soaked in cerate made with rose oil until the swelling subsides. Then treat it until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs, then dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear. Then use the other treatments mentioned earlier in this treatise to stop the bleeding.
On the treatment of swelling of the eyelids.
Chapter 8
These swellings that occur on the eyelids can sometimes be moist and at other times dry.
Therefore, hold them with forceps or with a hook, and cut them away from their roots using scissors. Then place powdered saffron on the area.
But if blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a fine lens-shaped cautery.
And it is better to cauterize them after cutting. For they often return when some of their roots remain. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter on the Treatment of Swelling of the Eyelids.
Chapter Nine.
It often happens that something resembling a grain in strength and hardness appears on the eyelids, and because of this it is called a swelling. It is an accumulation of thick moisture in the upper eyelid and lower eyelid.
Therefore, the operation for these is: first consider whether it is a swelling that has appeared on the eyelid and moves towards all parts, and its consistency is soft.
Make an incision over it lengthwise. Then tear it out from every part with ease until it is completely removed. Then hang it with a hook and cut it off.
But if its removal cannot be accomplished without first making a small incision into the eyelid, then it causes harm to the sick person.
But if the incision is large: then gather it together with stitches and care for it until it heals.
And if it is small, no harm will come from it. For the ointment restores it and joins it together.
And if the swelling leans towards the inside of the ear on the inner surface, then turn back the eyelid and hang the swelling with a hook. Except for needing an incision, press it from all sides.
But if the eyelid is elongated by the incision, this causes no harm.
Then rinse the eye after the removal of the swelling with salt water and care for the area with something that promotes healing until the sick person is healed.
Chapter on the Treatment of Swelling of the Eyebrows.
Chapter Ten.
Axirnath is fat that is produced in the skin of the upper eyelid. This happens most often to children and it weighs down their eyes. They suffer from catarrh in the mornings and cannot look at sunlight because tears flow towards them. Therefore, you see them always leaning on their faces and sleeping upon them. Their eyelids are moist under the eyebrows which have already become swollen due to inflammation. The prominence of Axirnath is visible to the eye, and when you press the area with your fingers, you can feel it between them.
And its method of operation is that the sick person places their head on thy lap.
Then take a linen cloth and prepare upon it some thread, and make from that thread a circle according to the size that contains Axirnath on all sides. Then place it upon it, and press with your fingers on all sides so that Axirnath may gather in the middle of the circle.
Then make an incision with a scalpel in the midst of that moisture along its width and not deeper than the incisions made for bloodletting.
Otherwise you must cut the skin all the way until you reach Axirnath, for in many cases Axirnath proceeds from the incision like a piece of white fat.
Then remove it with a cloth that you have moistened between your fingers on the outside and roll your hand to the right and left until it disperses. And beware not to push further into the incision lest you reach the eye with the scalpel and harm it.
But if Axirnath does not appear at first in the incision, you should proceed gently until Axirnath comes forth. Then remove it as we have said.
Then soak some cloths in vinegar and water and place them on the location, and secure them with bandages.
And there are some people who grind salt and apply it to the hollow of the incision so that what remains of that moisture may be dissolved.
Then care for it until it heals.
But if a hot abscess forms there, then treat it with soothing poultices until it heals.
On the ways of treating styes of the eye, that is, when the eyelid becomes inflamed so that it droops as it should not.
Chapter 11.
When additional hairs are born in the eyelids besides their natural course and grow long, they cause harm to the eye and bring about ways of illness, such as constant tears, inflammation of the eyelids, and cloudiness until it causes destruction of the eye.
And styes of the eye are treated in four ways. Or by cautery with fire. Or with acute medicine, as is mentioned in the chapter on cautery. Or styes are treated by incising and sewing. Or by means of cups, as I will explain.
You must put the patient's head on your lap. Then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If it turns easily, that is good. And if it does not turn easily: insert a needle into the eyelid where there is thread from below, and make the needle penetrate upwards with the thread close to the hairs themselves, that is, close to the edge of the eyelid where the hairs are growing; then pull the thread upward with the eyelid and turn it back with your finger.
Then find the added hairs on the inside of the eyelid using tweezers, cutting from the larger tear duct to the smaller one.
Then remove the thread and place a small wisp of cotton or cloth under the eyelid; then mark the eyelid with a cautery in the shape of a fig leaf.
However, the figure should be made according to the size and height of the eyelid, because these vary greatly among people.
For those who need to have a large amount cut from their eyelid, it should be measured according to the size that is softened by the eyelid.
Then with a fine spatula, find the space between the two lines that you have marked, and begin from the larger tear duct to the smaller one. The incision should be one near the natural hairs, like the thickness of a reed.
Then insert the hook into one of the two angles of the skin. Then strip off all of it. Then bring together the two lips with a suture using needle and fine wool thread. Remove any blood, and make what is excess of the threads adhere to both eyebrows. If you wish, use some sticky substances; if not, do not worry about it.
Then leave the suture and threads in place for three or four days before caring for it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without a suture and treat it with something that dries and tightens it. For the eyelid will swell after cauterization of the wound and its closure, but in the future it will be better.
This method of treatment was taught by the ancients called Atashmir.
However, there is carefulness about the sick person involved, and it comes from good practice, not fear.
There is also another method in Atashmir: which is to mark on the eyelid the figure of a myrtle leaf, as we have described.
Then raise the eyelid with three separate or joined hooks according to this following shape. Then cut off what is excess with small forceps according to this shape, making the cuts equal in depth.
But if it is not possible for you to hold the hook and make it equal, then take a needle with thread on it and insert it into the middle of the figure. Then insert another thread near the larger tear duct and a second one near the smaller tear duct. Then bring together the threads between your fingers evenly.
Then raise your hand with equal elevation and raise the skin over which the tumor is marked, as I have described.
Then approximate the two lips of the wound with a suture and care for it until it heals.
But if an abscess should form at your incision or wound, cauterize it with caustic substances and similar ointments that have a soothing effect.
And sometimes it happens that the eyelids also become inverted, such that their hairs turn inward.
Therefore, you should use what we have said about incision in this case, but the suture for both is more artful. Chapter on treating hemorrhoids with ligatures.
And it is necessary that you turn back the eyelid and cut open the wound that is on the inside, as I have described. Then skillfully make two ligatures or thin pieces of wood, whose length is equal to the length of the eyelid and width less than the width of a grain. And you have already cut at both ends where you hold the threads.
Then carefully bring both together over that which protrudes from the eyelid of the eye and tie them tightly on both sides with a strong ligature, and leave it in place for several days. For the skin tied too tightly will die, turn black, and decay until it falls off by itself.
If it takes too long, then cut it with forceps. Then care for it until it heals.
When it becomes firm, raise the eyelid and the hairs will no longer irritate it.
And this is the shape of the two ligatures which you should make according to this figure and measurement in length and width.
Chapter 12
Here follows the method for removing piercing hairs from the eye with a needle.
The operation is as follows: take a fine needle and insert into it a thread of silk, soft and thin. Then bind the ends with a very fine knot, one on each end; let the length of the threads be the span of one hand.
Then place under it another fine thread and bind its ends if you wish.
Then let the patient lay his head in your lap, facing the sun so that the operation may be clear to see, for it is a delicate or minute operation.
Then insert the needle with its thread into the root of the eyelid and push it through quickly until you draw out a natural hair from above.
Then pull the hair up along with the thread; and insert into the thread that one hair only, or two, or three, but not more.
Then pull your hand gently until the thread with its hair comes out from under the eyelid along with the natural hair.
But if the thread comes out without the hair, then pull it downwards along with the other thread you inserted into it until the first thread emerges from above. Then when you see the eye clearly, pull both threads together and remove them; close the eye and let it remain closed for two or three days until the hair adheres to its place and flesh grows over it.
But if the hair is short, then either join it with a longer natural hair and remove them both together, or leave it alone until it grows longer, and then remove it.
Chapter 13
When this inflammation occurs to the eye, it is called "lepra.
And this leprosy is either natural or accidental.
Accidental leprosy occurs from the solidification of a wound, or from an incision, cautery, or similar things.
The method of treatment for this condition is to locate the solidification and separate its lips, then place a linen wick between them and bind it until it heals.
But do not use in its treatment things that dry and constrict, for if you do so, the leprosy will return worse than before. Instead, use things that soften, such as fennel-greek; shave with water in which althea has been cooked and linseed has been steeped; and apply a poultice made from diacilicon ointment, mixed with some oil, and wrap it around a linen cloth for the cure.
And your treasure in healing is to strive by all means to prevent consolidation, according to the original form that it had at first.
And indeed this operation we have spoken of is only a certain correction of leprosy, so that the form of the place does not return entirely as it was before.
Chapter: On the treatment of leprosy that occurs on the lower eyelid and causes wrinkling or contraction upwards towards the right and left.
Chapter XIV.
This leprosy that occurs below is truly called "leprosy. And it can be either natural or accidental.
Accidental leprosy occurs from a wound, fissure, cautery, or similar things.
The method of treatment for this condition is to take a needle with double thread and stain it with flesh color, then make it penetrate from the left tear duct to the right until the thread emerges at both ends of the flesh.
Then stretch the flesh upwards with the needle, and cut it with a broad-bladed knife. If then the shape of the eyelid returns as it should be, that is good.
And if not [the eyelid returns to its proper shape], take a probe and place it in the fissure, and turn the eyelid with it: and make two incisions on the inner side of the eyelid, and let the ends of the two incisions meet at two angles, until they converge and form an angle: so that when they are joined together, this figure is formed, which is the shape of the Greek letter lambda.
Then remove that piece of flesh, which is the acute side facing downwards, from what follows the eyelid. Then join the separated parts together: whether with a thread made of wool.
Then care for them with what we have said about softening medicines and linens until they heal.
And if a hematoma occurs from the incision and suture or cauterization: then it is necessary to make an additional planar incision beneath the hairs of the eyelid, just as before. Then separate between the two lips with linen, as we have said.
And in summary, concerning the treatment of hematoma whether it is above or below [the eye], the operation should be performed according to how the hematoma is prepared.
For indeed it [the hematoma] has many variations in shape: and a skilled artisan prepares his ingenuity with whatever means are possible for him until he restores the shape to its natural form or near it, and the patient himself, according to what is fitting for him from the operation and instruments in many dispositions.
Chapter 15.
¶ Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
Sometimes this condition occurs in many people, such that the upper eyelid becomes adherent to the white of the eye and prevents it from performing its natural functions.
Therefore, it is necessary to insert the end of a probe under the eyelid and raise it upwards or extend it with a hook: then incise the adhesion with a spatha (a blunt instrument), which should not have too sharp an edge, but rather be somewhat blunt: so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and the spatha does not harm the eye with its sharpness. Make your incision as if you are scraping away the adhesion easily, until the eyelid returns to its natural shape and the adhesion heals completely.
Then pour saltwater into the eye and rinse it with water or distill a solution of sulfur dissolved in water onto it.
Then separate the eyelid from the eye with a linen thread and place over the eye a wick soaked in egg white. After three days, use sulfur and salves until it heals.
Chapter on Cutting the Nail and Excising a Fleshy Growth of the Tear Duct
Chapter 16
Know that the nail has two forms. Either it is nervine: and it resembles a piece of hard, thin horn. Or it is not nervine, resembling frozen white moisture. When touched by iron or when tried to be grasped with a hook, it breaks off and the hook does not stick in it.
And both kinds do not begin unless from the larger tear duct, gradually advancing until it covers the sight and blocks light and movement of the eye.
The method of operation for cutting it is that the patient should lay their head on your lap. Then open the patient's eyes and raise the eyelid with your hand. Next, grasp the nail with a hook of moderate size and extend it upwards. Then take a needle and insert into it a hair from a horse, ox, or cow - let the thread be strong and double the end of the needle slightly, and fix it in the middle of the nail. Then pierce it with the needle and tie the nail with the thread, extending it upwards. Then scrape the side of the nail that follows the pupil, as if you were cutting it with the hair until its end.
Then cut the remaining part at the root of the tear duct with a fine scalpel or small forceps. Ensure not to damage the natural tissue of the tear duct so that there is no continuous flow of tears due to the incision.
And sometimes the nail extends only with a hook alone; or with thread, as we have said. Then scrape it gently with a scalpel, which has this shape:
And beware that the scalpel does not touch the corneal tunic and cause a break or perforation of the pupil.
Then after the incision, sprinkle some crushed salt or powdered verdigris into the eye and leave it overnight. Then treat it with what is necessary until it heals.
But if the nail is not nerve-like and you cannot insert a needle into it nor can it hold a hook, then this type of nail is cured many times with medications as I have explained in its division.
But if you wish to cut it, then open the sick eye and take a fine, gentle scalpel with blunted sharpness in this shape: And carefully shave off the nail from above with finesse. But if you see that it becomes rarefied and broken, and the razor presses upon it, then distill from your hour into the eye a powder made of verdigris or red ocher or ground salt. Then bandage the eye overnight.
Then repeat this procedure until all of it is removed. Unless there is a warm abscess in the eye, then leave it alone and treat the abscess until it heals.
Then repeat the procedure on it with care until it is healed.
If the prominence of the lacrimal gland causes great harm to the eye, it should be suspended with a hook and a portion of it cut away; but do not make the last cut too deep, lest the flow of tears be obstructed.
Then instill into the tear duct red sulphur or powder made from cinabar until it is healed.
Regarding the incision of the pterygium and flesh born in the eye
Chapter XVII
A red flesh sometimes grows in the eyes of certain people, spreading until it covers the pupil or approaches and presses upon the eyelids, even causing them to turn outward, resembling a cock's-comb.
And the operation for this is that the patient should place their head on your lap. Then open the eye and grasp that flesh entirely with suitable hooks; hold it with one forceps or your hand.
Then cut into the flesh first, until the entire length of it is reached with the scalpel. And be careful not to injure the eye during the operation. The cut should be made either with a scalpel or with the spatula previously mentioned for nail cutting, or with small forceps, according to how convenient it is after this chapter.
When the incision is complete and the entire pterygium has been removed, then fill the eye with powdered salt or instill red sulphur powder into it, along with other corrosive medications. Then place a white-hot cautery on the outside of the eye to prevent inflammation.
But if some part of the pterygium remains and there is a risk of hot inflammation and you fear an abscess, then leave the eye alone and treat it with something that cools the inflammation.
Then repeat the procedure on it until it is healed.
And similarly do with the added flesh that occurs in the eye of this kind.
Nevertheless, it is necessary that you perform surgery on every weak eye with the aid of a knife, both by way of nature and accident; and do not undertake any of these operations until you have seen that that member can endure them.
Chapter On The Extraction Of A Foreign Body From The Eye
Chapter 18
Sebel are red veins that grow over the eye and hinder its vision, and weaken the eye with prolonged duration.
Therefore, it is necessary for you first to observe whether the eye with sebel is strong and has no other sickness besides sebel; then extract the sebel from it.
And tell the patient to put their head on your lap.
Then suspend those veins with one hook or two according to your discretion; and let the hook be of fine double curvature, like this shape, or let there be two hooks graduated on one body, like this shape.
Then gently grasp those veins with fine forceps and remove the blood little by little until you see that those veins have been removed from the eye and are resolved; and be careful not to injure the eye with the tips of the forceps.
And let your operation take place at midday before the sun, and be very steady in your operation so that you do not touch anything but those veins.
And when you have finished, then apply collyrium either green or red to the eye so that its sharpness may corrode any remainingtrace of sebel.
But if you are not able to extract all of it at once, then apply a plaster to the eye with something that soothes the pain; and let him rest for several days until the pain subsides and hot inflammation is secured. Then repeat the procedure in the same manner until it is healed.
And this is the shape of the forceps.
¶ On the reduction of a dislocated nose.
Chapter 19.
The doctors call it "Arissati," which they treat either with a cautery or by burning it with an acrid medicine, depending on what has been told about its condition.
And if it is not cured, then there is no hope for it except to open a window above the abscess at its origin and remove all the moisture and pus that is within it until the bone is exposed.
When the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness on it, then scrape it with an instrument whose shape is like this:
And it is called "Asperi's head.
Make it from hard iron and let its head be round like a hazelnut, which is pierced with the point of a file or with a fine punch; then place it on the site of the bone corruption.
Then rotate it between your fingers and press your hand lightly until you know that the corruption has been scraped away; do this several times.
Then restore the site with drying astringent medicines.
If the site solidifies and flesh grows there, and the flow of pus is cut off, and it remains for forty days without renewing or forming an abscess, and no complication arises, then know that it has been cured.
And if not, then there is no hope except to reduce the fistula to the nostril according to this shape:
And it is so that you expose the bone from below with iron or an acrid medicine; and when the bone is exposed, then take a probe of this shape, its iron tip being triangular, and its wood tapering to a point like this.
Then place it on the bone itself; let it be near the tear duct; and extend your hand slightly away from the eye. Then rotate your hand with the probe until it pierces the bone and the patient feels it; by this you will know that air is escaping from it when they place their hand over their nose.
Therefore, then restore the site with drying astringent medicines, as I have taught you.
Thus when flesh grows and the site becomes hardened, then the material that was flowing outward will return to the nose; therefore this is easier for the patient.
Chapter on Reducing Protrusion of the Eye
Chapter 20.
When the eye protrudes completely and there is no harm to sight or diminution, then it is necessary for you to loosen the patient; afterwards bleed them; then apply cupping glasses to the neck without incision but with suction.
Then place over the eye a plaster made from acacia, and aloe, and frankincense, and tragacanth gum. Then bind the eye above the plaster with many small wicks.
And the method of the wicks is this: take soft cloths, many of them, and fold each one over four times; make many of these; then place them one on top of another according to the quantity that the eye needs; then bind them with a hard cloth using strong binding.
And repeat cupping with suction without incision.
Then remove the cups and leave the eye bound day and night.
But if the binding becomes soft again, then it is necessary to bind it tightly; then remove the binding after a day and night, as we have said.
If you see that the eye has returned to its normal position, then that is good; but if not, then repeat the plaster and wicks, and binding, and cupping until it heals.
Chapter on Incising the Upper Eyelid
Quando non est uisus profectus in Oculo ex praecedenti medicina, erit necessarium ut incidas uae tunicam: et hoc facies sic.
When there is a disruption in the upper eyelid, then the protruding swelling will come out beyond the eyelids like a grain of barley and disfigure the face; if you wish to incise it, then perform the operation as follows: insert a needle into the root of the swelling from below towards the top.
Then insert another needle with a doubled thread on the lacrimal side and pass it through; leave the first needle in its original position.
Then cut the location of the doubled thread and tie the eyelid around the needle with it; then pull it tight.
Then remove the needle, place a piece of wool soaked in egg white over the eye; leave him until the thread falls out with the swelling.
Then treat the eye with ointment that will strengthen it until the wound heals.
And sometimes the swelling is tied from all sides with a bandage made of cloth until the eye becomes very protuberant.
Then pierce it with a fine trocar that reaches deep into the eye; let the aqueous humor flow out and allow the eye to sink down immediately; then bind it until it heals.
And concerning the treatment of haemorrhoids:
Chapter 22
This condition called haemorrhoids is nothing but a collection of watery fluid in the eye, and it is not actually blood.
The method for treating this condition is to have the patient sit on a prepared or upright chair.
Then take hold of his head with both your hands and move him until you see the fluid reaching below his eyes. Then catch it quickly so that it does not flow down and blur the vision; if the patient can see clearly again, know that it was water.
But if the fluid does not flow down as described above, then have the patient sit between your hands. Then take a fine trocar and pierce the conjunctiva slightly above the limbus of the cornea until the fluid comes out.
When the fluid comes out, then instill warm water mixed with honey into the eye: or warm water in which you have already boiled mallow and honey: thereafter treat it with the remaining treatment until it is healed.
On the treatment of water that descends into the eye, or cataract.
Chapter XXII
We have already discussed the types of water and which is suitable for piercing, now take that same one according to its suitability.
Then have the patient sit between your hands facing a square opening towards the light, near the sun: and tie their healthy eye, and bind them tightly: then with your right hand lift the eyelid of their left eye if it is the eye with the water, or with your left hand if it is the right eye.
Then take an eyelid retractor in your right hand if it's the left eye, or in your left hand if it's the right eye.
Then place the end of the eyelid retractor near the limbus towards the thickness of the radius where the smaller lacrimal duct appears: then push the eyelid retractor with force and you rotate your hand with it until it penetrates into the eyeball and you feel that the eyelid retractor has reached an empty space.
And it is necessary that the measure of the path of the eyelid retractor into the depth be equal to the space from the pupil to the end of the blackness which is the limbus of the eye. For you will see it in the pupil itself through the clearness of the corneal tunic.
Then place the eyelid retractor until it reaches the location where the water is. Then press down on it repeatedly, one after the other.
If water descends immediately, then the patient will see the object upon which they opened their sight immediately, and the eyelid retractor will be in their eye.
Then rest a little. If water descends, then pour it out again, except for the eyelid retractor.
When the fluid is drawn up and does not ascend, then remove the eyelid retractor gently; and you should slowly roll your hand back.
Then dissolve some clear salt of the stone in water and wash the eye with it inside. Then place a wick or wool soaked in rose oil outside the eye, and press gently on the eyelid; then bind it with a healthy eye.
But we, and those of our time, place powdered incense mixed with egg white upon him.
If the eyelid retractor does not obey you when entering the eye due to its hardness, because it is made from human bone and has a very hard eye, then you should take spatumile, also known as Albied, which has this form.
Therefore insert it only into the joint with it, and do not put your hand further into the opening; otherwise you will be unable to reach the delicate eyelid retractor.
Then introduce the eyelid retractor as we have described.
And at the completion of your operation, prepare for the patient a well-made bed where they can sleep on their back in a dark room; prevent them from all movements and coughing, and give them food that soothes their nature. They should not move their head to the right or left at all.
And let the bandage remain in place according to its arrangement until the third day.
Then remove it on the third day in that dark room, and test his sight; make him see objects. Then reapply the bandage until the seventh day.
And it is not appropriate for him to do this at the time of treatment or immediately after an incision; rather, it should be done by someone else because water ascends rapidly due to intense staring.
If however a hot swelling occurs, then it is necessary to open the eye before the seventh day, and correct it with something that subdues the swelling until it is subdued; then without its sight, place on his face an eyepatch which regulates his vision for several days while he remains in that dark house.
Then gradually leave the house and return to one's own home.
And know that during the zealot period, the disciple is not excused from seeing sometimes; then he may dare to perform an operation.
And indeed, one of those from Al-Rayy has come to us who said that a cistern was pierced there through which water flows.
Therefore I have not seen anyone in your land who has done this, nor have I read it in any of the ancient books; and it is possible that this is something new.
And these are the forms of creatures that come from the cistern, so that he who does not know may stand upon them.
Let them be made properly out of bronze and their ends be triangularly pointed with the same fineness, and let one end be sharp.
Chapter 24
Prologue: On the Treatment of a Flesh Growth in the Nose.
Sometimes various kinds of flesh are born in the nose from which there is something resembling a many-legged scorpion, and from it there is fleshy, bony, dark-colored flesh; and from it there is also soft flesh that is not dark-colored.
Therefore, in the case of that which is soft and not deceitful or cancerous among these kinds of flesh, it is necessary for you to make the patient sit between your hands facing the sun, open his nostrils, insert a hook into those fleshy growths, then pull them outwards, and cut off with a sharp and fine spatula from one side what you have grasped until you know that all the flesh has been removed.
But if there remains something of it which cannot be cut off: then shave that with one of the subtle instruments with ease until nothing remains. But if blood dominates over you, or a hot abscess occurs: then apply to it what is necessary according to what is appropriate. Or if it is one of the malicious kinds of swellings, then hurry to cauterize him until the blood is cut off and all the flesh is removed.
Then after the incision, throw vinegar and water or wine into the nostril. But if the nostril opens up and moisture proceeds from it to the palate, then know that he is now healed.
But if moisture does not penetrate as it should, then know that there is flesh born in the upper bones of the nostril which was not reached by the instrument with the incision.
Therefore it is necessary that you insert a thread of linen with some thickness and many knots tied in it, placing between each knot the amount of a finger or less. Then let the patient introduce one end of the thread into his nostril with a probe or whatever he can manage afterwards making it resemble an awl, and draw out his scent until it reaches the throat and emerges above his throat.
And indeed many children do this like thing in schools: and it is easy for him who wishes to do so.
Then place two ends of the thread, one that emerges above the nostril and another that remains inside the nostril. Then bring a cutter for cutting flesh with knots on the thread. Do this until you know that the flesh is already bound with knots of the thread.
Then remove the thread and insert into the nostril, after wiping away blood, a wick which you have dipped in Egyptian ointment. Do this for three days or more until the ointment has eaten away all that remains of the flesh.
Then place at the end of it into the nostril a tube of lead for several days until it heals.
If however treatment that dries is needed, use that one.
For making a head-purge vessel for distilling oils and other medications into the nostril, make it of silver or bronze, resembling a small lantern with an opening like a shell, having a similar passage.
And if you wish to make a closed tube, such as a reed.
And the shell of the vessel for making a head-purge should be full and open at the bottom with a handle at its end with which to hold it when heating oil or other fluids in it.
Chapter 25
The nosebleed often originates at the tip of the nose and grows larger and increases with days until it becomes unsightly.
And because of this, it is necessary to cut off its beginning as soon as it appears and erase all of it entirely. Then apply either a cautery or a burning medicine to the place where it was, so that it stays in its location.
If the cutting of it is delayed until it becomes large, then consider if it is stony hard, dark in color, and insensitive. Do not touch it with iron, because it is a cancerous abscess.
For I have often seen that those who cut into such an abscess suffer great harm from it.
But if the abscess is soft to touch, not dark in color, and you see that it can be cut into entirely, then evacuate the patient and cut into it without fear or worry. Treat the place with something that dries and contracts until it heals.
Chapter 26
On the suture of the nose, lip, and ear.
Know that when there is a break in the continuity of one of these cartilages, it does not benefit much unless in certain people.
Therefore, whenever something happens to someone from that area, you should consider if it is a fresh wound with its own blood. Then approximate the edges of the wound with suturing, and then treat it until it heals.
But if the lips of the discontinuity are already separated and each part has healed separately, then it is necessary to excise all the skin that appears until it bleeds. Then approximate the two lips with sutures and tighten them. Powder over the blood some dragon's blood powder and ground frankincense. Place on top a piece of plaster called diafinicon and another consolidated plaster, and leave it bound for two or three days. Then remove the dressing after two or three days, change the medicine, and leave it alone until the stitches fall out by themselves. Then treat it with ointment until it heals.
The method of suturing is as follows: approximate the discontinuity either with needles, as we have described for abdominal closure, or with thread, as I have shown you.
Chapter 27
On the removal of nodules from the lips.
Many people often have hard nodules like grains of wheat on the inside of their lips. Some are smaller and some larger.
Therefore, turn the lip over and make an incision over each node. Suspend it with a hook and cut into it on all sides.
Then fill the place after the incision with ground zinc oxide until the bleeding stops.
Then it is bound with vinegar and the wound is treated with it, in which there is astringency, until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 28
Concerning the incision of flesh born upon the gum.
Flesh often grows on the gum which the ancients call abuluss.
Therefore you should suspend it with a hook or hold it with forceps and cut it at its root, allowing blood or pus to flow.
Then apply ground zinc oxide powder or other drying astringent powders to the wound.
But if that flesh returns after treatment, for it often does return, then cut it again and cauterize it; for it will not return after being cauterized.
Chapter 29
Concerning the extraction of teeth with iron instruments
Sometimes teeth are surrounded by rough and foul layers on their inner and outer surfaces, and between the gums, becoming blackened, yellowish, or greenish, until corruption reaches the gum and the teeth become fetid.
Therefore make the patient sit between your hands and place their head in your lap. Grind away with a file any layer or deposit on the teeth that appears like sand until nothing remains of it, and do likewise for blackness, greenness, yellowishness, and other such impurities until the teeth are cleaned.
If what is being removed at the beginning of the extraction is good, that is fine. But if not, then repeat the extraction on another day, second and third times, until finally you achieve your goal.
And know that teeth require files of various shapes and forms according to how they are prepared for your operation. The file used to grind the lower part of a tooth is different from the one used between teeth due to their varied shapes.
And have these forms of files ready at hand for you to see.
Chapter 30
Concerning the Extraction of Toothache.
You should strive to remove a tooth from its pain with all your skill, and be slow in extracting it. For there is nothing that can replace it once it is extracted, since it is a most noble substance. Therefore, until there is no excuse for extracting it, then it becomes necessary to extract the tooth when the patient is compelled to do so, in order that it may be made firm and certain in your hands.
For pain often deceives the patient, making him believe that it is in a sound tooth and causing him to extract it. But then the pain does not cease until the diseased tooth is extracted.
We ourselves have seen this in the practice of bloodletting.
Therefore, when you have assured yourself of the painful tooth itself, then it is necessary that you make an incision around the tooth with a scalpel in which there is some virtue, so that the gum may be loosened on all sides. Then move the tooth gently with your fingers or with fine forceps little by little until you feel it give way.
Then place large forceps with good position on the tooth and put the patient's head between your knees. When you have held it firmly so that it does not move, then pull out the tooth in a straight line so as not to break it.
If it does not come out, then take one of those instruments and insert it underneath the tooth on all sides with ease and try to move it as you did at first.
If the tooth is perforated or decayed, then it is necessary to fill that hole with cloth and tie it with a fine thread so that it may not break during your constriction of it with forceps.
And you should make an incision around the gum on all sides very carefully and thoroughly.
And guard your strength so that you do not break it off, lest a part of it remain and return upon the patient with greater pain than before.
And take care not to do what ignorant bloodletters often do in their haste and rashness when removing it beyond what we have instructed.
For oftentimes they cause great diseases to happen upon men, which are more difficult to cure, and all the roots of it remain, or some of them; or they draw it out with certain bones of the jaw, as we have often witnessed.
Then let it be cauterized after its removal with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
But if bleeding occurs from the site (which often happens), then crush some incense and fill the place with it; and if you do not have incense, cauterize the spot.
The forceps used to move a tooth should be fine-pointed with long tips and short handles, thick so that they do not close together when pressed against the tooth. They should resemble thick handles until when you squeeze them together they do not pinch or double over. The tips should be short and made of well-tempered iron or bronze, straight-edged, with teeth on their ends which fit into each other and grip tightly.
And sometimes the tips are shaped like a bird's beak called a stork's beak, and these have a strong gripping power.
Chapter 31.
[About] The Removal of Teeth and the Extraction of Broken Jaw Bones
When a tooth remains to be removed which has broken off, then it is necessary to place cataplasm of butter upon the spot for one or two days until the place becomes softened. Then introduce an elevator or forceps whose tips resemble the shape of a bird's mouth called a stork's beak. This is the shape of the forceps.
Their tips should be made like an awl inside: or like an alkibibis.
If the patient does not yield to you at the exit with these forceps, then it is necessary for you to make incisions over the root and strip all the flesh, and insert the instrument called "small lever," whose shape is as follows.
Let its extremity be short, thick for a moment: and let it not be hollow, so that it does not break.
If the root emerges through this (instrument), it is good; and if not, help must be given with other instruments whose shapes are as follows.
The shape of the first is triangular at its tip, with some thickness.
Shape of the second: triangular tip, thin.
And with other instruments and tools that preceded it in the treatment of teeth.
And also help is given with this instrument which resembles a large hook, of this shape, having a twisted triangular tip with some thickness so that it does not break; and let it not be hollow.
And know that there are many instruments for teeth; and similarly the rest of the instruments are not numbered, and a skilled craftsman, subtle in his art finds an instrument himself according to what operation and conditions require.
Because there are conditions to which the ancients did not mention an instrument because of the diversity of their kinds.
But if the bone breaks from the jawbone or other bones of the face, or becomes putrid, then examine it at its place with whatever is suitable for it among the instruments and forceps that I mentioned for extracting roots, and help with splints whose shape is as follows.
Let there be some thickness gradually tapering in it so that it may bind or hold the bone and prevent it from escaping or moving until the bone comes out; and restore the place with suitable medicines for it.
But if there is a small decay in the bone, then scrape off the decay itself and treat it until it heals.
Chapter 32.
On the treatment of prominent teeth above others.
When teeth are prominent beyond their natural course, it causes an unpleasant appearance, especially when this happens in women and young girls, namely those who are sold [i.e., married].
Therefore, one must consider. For if a tooth is born behind another and cannot be placed or its prominence cannot be corrected by trimming it, then extract it. And if it is attached to another tooth, then cut it with this instrument of which this is the shape.
It resembles a small beak and is made of iron, very sharp at the tip.
And your cutting should take place over many days, on account of the hardness of the tooth and to avoid disturbing any other teeth.
But if it is prominently exposed to trimming, then trim it with a file made of iron whose shape is like this.
Let it be entirely of iron, and its handle full of many small points like the file used for making needles. With this file, the tooth is slowly trimmed over many days with ease so that it does not move or fall out. Then polish it gently, and finally scrape it smooth with some razors.
But if there is a tooth from which a piece breaks off and hurts the tongue, then you should file it down until its sharp edge is removed and made smooth, so that it no longer hurts the tongue or interferes with speech.
Chapter 33.
On holding teeth in place with gold threads.
When the front teeth are shaken and moved by blows or accidents and the patient cannot bite firmly without them falling out, even after treating them with medicinal astringents, then it is necessary to hold them in place using gold thread because silver turns green and decays over time.
And the thread should be of medium thickness, neither too thin nor too thick, according to the space between the teeth it must fit into.
The method of binding is as follows: take the thread and insert its double between two healthy teeth. Then tie it with both ends between the loose tooth or teeth until you reach a healthy tooth on the other side. Then repeat the tying until you reach the side where you began. Tie with care and wisdom so that it does not slip at all; your tying should be around the roots of the teeth so that it does not come loose.
Then cut off the excess ends of the thread with forceps and join them together. Bend them towards each other and hide them between the healthy tooth and the loose one so that they do not harm the tongue. Then leave it tied tightly like this until healing occurs.
If it loosens or breaks, tie them again with another thread and they will remain stable like that for the entire duration of treatment.
This is the form of the teeth and the figure of binding two healthy teeth with two loose ones, as you can see.
Sometimes it happens that one or two teeth return to their original places after falling out completely. If they are held in place as we have described and remain there, this is only possible with the skill of a knowledgeable and experienced artisan.
Sometimes a piece of bone from the jawbone is prepared and shaped like a tooth. It is then placed in the gap where the tooth was lost and held in place with thread as described earlier. This will remain and serve as a long-term solution.
Chapter 34.
Sometimes this ligament under the tongue is natural, with which man is born, or it is accidental, due to an injury that has already healed.
And the operation on it is thus: Open the mouth of the sick person in your lap and lift his tongue. Then cut this nerve ligament across its width until the tongue is released from its restraint.
But if there is something hard and knotty present, and it is due to the healing of a wound, then use a hook to throw into it and find it across its width until you cut the ligament and release the knot.
And beware lest there be a deep cut in the flesh and you cut an artery there, so that bleeding occurs.
Then let it be sewn up at the site of the incision with rosewater, or vinegar, or cold water.
Then place under the tongue a linen bandage which the patient should hold in place every night so that it does not heal again.
But if bleeding occurs, then put on the site of the cut a poultice made of cooked mustard. But if blood overcomes you, then cauterize the site with a slow-burning cautery suitable for that purpose. Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter 35.
Sometimes under the tongue there appears a swelling resembling a small frog, which hinders the tongue from its natural movement, and perhaps becomes so large that it fills the mouth.
And the operation on it is thus: Open the mouth of the sick person towards the sun and observe the swelling.
If you see it is dark colored or black, hard, and without sensation for the patient, then do not touch it because it is cancer.
But if it is inclined towards whiteness with moisture present, then throw a hook into it and cut it with a sharp scalpel and free it from all sides. But if blood overcomes you during your operation, then place upon it ground ginger until the bleeding ceases; then return to your task until you remove it with its entirety.
Then cauterize it with vinegar and salt; and treat it with other appropriate treatment until it is healed.
On the Treatment of Abscesses of the Two Mandibles
Chapter 36
Sometimes swellings occur between the throat and the glands, that is, the glands which resemble almonds, which occur externally; these are called the two tonsils. When you treat them with what we have said concerning division, but they do not heal, then consider.
If therefore there is a hard abscess of dark color with little sensation, do not touch it with iron.
And if it is of red color and its root is thick, then do not touch it even with iron, fearing bleeding, but leave it until it ripens. And then either you will pierce it or it will burst by itself.
But if it is of white color, round, and its root is thin, then it must be cut open.
And the operation on it is as follows: before beginning your task, consider whether the abscess has become completely quiet and cold or has decreased somewhat. Then make the patient sit facing the sun, with his head in your lap, open his mouth, and have an assistant take hold of his tongue from below with an instrument shaped like this.
It should be made of silver or bronze, thin like a scalpel.
When you press the tongue with it and the abscess is revealed to you, then take a hook and insert it into the tonsil, and draw it out towards the outside as much as possible without tearing any of its membranes.
Then cut it open with an instrument shaped like this which follows: It resembles forceps; however, its ends are curved so that the opening of each is directly opposite the other at an acute angle. Therefore, ensure it is made from polished iron or gold-plated.
But if this instrument is not available, then cut it open with a scalpel shaped like this: Let it be sharp on one side and blunt on the other.
And after cutting one tonsil, cut the other according to the same manner of incision.
Then after the incision, let the patient gargle with cold water or with vinegar and water.
But if bleeding occurs, let him gargle with water in which pomegranate rinds have been boiled, or with myrtle leaves and similar astringent substances, until the bleeding stops; then treat it until it heals.
And sometimes other swellings appear in the throat besides the two tonsils. Therefore cut those likewise according to what I have said regarding the equal cutting of both tonsils.
And indeed I myself once treated a woman for an abscess that had formed in her throat and was drawing towards the windpipe, causing difficulty in breathing and making it almost impossible for her to swallow water. She could barely speak and was close to death, so that if left untreated she would have died within one or two days.
And the abscess had grown so much that it had sent forth two branches which had emerged through both nostrils.
Therefore I hastened quickly and inserted a hook into one of them. Then I pulled it out, and a large portion emerged from it.
Then I cut it off where it could be grasped through the nostril.
Then I did to her what had protruded from the other nostril.
Then I opened her mouth and pressed her tongue down. After that, I inserted a hook into the abscess itself, cut off part of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Thus the woman's throat was cleared, and she immediately began to drink water and then took some food.
Therefore I did not cease cutting away at that abscess many times over a long period of time. And the abscess kept growing back in place of what was being cut off, until it became extremely prolonged and grew with her.
I then applied an ipecacuanha ointment and cauterized the abscess between the throat.
And so it ceased to grow worse. Then I departed from her, not knowing what God would do with her afterwards.
On the Incision of an Abscess on the Throat.
Chapter 37
When phlegm descends to the throat and an abscess forms, if it is long, then it is called a "perpendicular"; but if it is thick and rounded at its lower part, then it is called a "grape".
When treated with the method described earlier and no improvement is seen, and you observe that the abscess has become hot and thin, then it must be cut.
And if the one that is joined to it is round and has no length, and is bloody or dark colored or black, and has no sensation, then avoid cutting into it. For there is danger in harming the healthy part.
Therefore, when you see it as I described earlier in terms of its whiteness and length, make the patient sit facing the sun. Then press down her tongue with an instrument about which there is mention elsewhere. Insert a hook into the uvula and pull it downward. Cut it open using one of the two instruments mentioned for cutting two tonsils.
And it is necessary that you should not cut anything beyond what exceeds its natural limit without adding to it.
For if you cut more than that, you will injure the voice and speech.
Then after the incision, use what we have described regarding the incision of two tonsils: and treat it until it heals.
But if the patient is frightened by the incision, then you must use your ingenuity in cauterizing it without fear and dread.
And the method of cauterization is not otherwise than with sharp water: and it is, that you place the head of the patient upon your bosom. Then press his tongue with the instrument we mentioned. Then take some of the water that I told you about in the chapter on cauterization, and dip into it an un-extinguished ember, and place it not thickly nor thinly, and fill with it the hollow of this instrument, which has this shape.
Let its end where the medicine is placed be concave like the concavity of a spoon's bowl.
And place the instrument with the medicine upon his throat: and let the patient lie on his side so that the salt water may flow out of his mouth from the internal medicine, so that nothing may fall down into his throat and harm him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you put it on his throat for the first time in the amount of half an hour until you see that it has turned black and the burning sensation of the medicine has subsided.
And if you wish to take cotton and wrap it around the end of the spoon, and pour the medicine over it, and insert the spoon with the cotton into the cannula from above until the cotton adheres to his throat.
Do this repeatedly until you achieve what you desire regarding the cauterization of the throat. Then leave it; for it dries up and falls off after three or four days.
But if you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it; and after cauterization, wipe around the throat with cotton soaked in butter, and cleanse with it what is around it from the medicine; and rinse with cold water.
And let it be treated externally with poultices, and internally with gargarisms, until it heals.
Therefore, this method of treatment is more salutary after incision and further from fear.
And sometimes the throat is also cured with something that is gentler than cautery and incision; it is cured with fumigation, according to this method. Take rue, and hyssop, and oregano, and sage, and chamomile, and abrotane, and similar herbs; and mix them all together, or some of them in a pot, and submerge them in vinegar, and let them boil; and the pot should be properly luted with clay: and there should be a hole in the middle of the pot, over which an concave instrument is placed in this manner.
Let it be made of silver or brass; and the extremity with the pomegranate shape should be inserted into the sick person's mouth until the vapor reaches the throat through the tube, until the throat becomes very dark.
Then repeat this many times until it dries out.
And beware, do not perform this treatment at the beginning of an abscess formation. For usually it adds to an abscess; and you should not do this unless it is applied when the abscess is about to burst.
But if you do not have this instrument, then take a reed and attach the skin of an egg at its end so that it does not burn the sick person's mouth; because the egg skin prevents the vapor from burning the mouth; and this is also good treatment with safety.
On the Extraction of Thorns and Bones from the Throat
Chapter 38
Many times a bone or fish spine becomes stuck in the throat.
Therefore you must extract what is visible above it by pressing the tongue with an instrument towards the sun, and then it will be clear to you what is in the throat.
And whatever does not appear to you and is concealed in the throat must be done by vomiting before food is digested in the stomach. Perhaps the attached matter will come out through vomiting.
Or let the sick person swallow a piece of radish root, or lettuce root, or swallow a dry piece of bread; or take a piece of dried sea sponge and tie it on a thread, then swallow it. When it reaches the place where the spine or bone is, and is extracted, then it will come out.
If however it does not come out with what we have said, then use an instrument made of lead in this shape.
Let it be thicker than a small rod for a moment, and at its end let there be a curvature which he introduces into his throat easily; and it itself should have its head raised upwards.
And let him be careful not to touch his epiglottis so that he does not cough, and with it he should press the bone or spine, or the doctor introduces it by hand.
But it is better for the sick person to introduce it himself because of his knowledge of where the attached matter is located, and he should press downwards or draw his hand upwards with the instrument, all of this according to the amount that is prepared for him until it comes out.
On the Extraction of Bloodsuckers.
Chapter 39
When the leech is treated with what I said regarding its division for treatment with medicines, and it does not attach itself, then examine the throat of the sick person before the sun or after pressing his tongue with the instrument that I have told you about.
If therefore your sight falls upon the leech, extract it with a small hook or with delicate tweezers skillfully.
But if this is not possible with them, then take a hollow cannula and introduce it into the throat of the sick person up to near his throat; then introduce a red-hot iron with fire into the concave part of the cannula. Do this repeatedly, and let the patient abstain from water all day long.
Then let him take a vessel full of cold water and open his mouth in it; and let him hold his mouth in the water without swallowing any of it, and move the water with his hand once every hour. For the leech will fall into the water immediately upon sensing it.
