But if it does not come out with what we have described, then fumigate the patient with flies or with frankincense using the instrument which I mentioned for fumigating the throat.
Do this repeatedly, since it will fall by itself.
The method of operation in fumigation is as follows: take a pot in which there are live apples with fire; and let the pot be covered with its cover, in whose middle there is an opening; and place the end of the instrument on that opening.
Then cast the fumigation into it, and let the patient place his mouth at the end of the cannula and close his mouth so that the fumigation does not escape until you know that the fumigation has reached the leech. For the leech will fall immediately.
But if it still does not fall, then repeat the fumigation many times.
And let the patient endure thirst and eat salt and garlic without drinking water.
Therefore it is necessary that it should come out with this treatment.
And this is the shape of the instrument with which to remove the leech from the throat when it falls on top of itself within sight.
It resembles forceps as you see [here], except that it has this curvature which is inserted into the throat. And its end resembles the beak of a bird called the stork, having the roughness of a file; when it grasps something, it does not let it go entirely.
Concerning the opening of boils and their incision, and their treatment in general.
Chapter 40
There are many kinds of boils [abscesses], diverse in their formation, recalling one by one as they come to mind; namely one kind is mentioned here, and they differ in piercing them and cutting into them in two ways.
One of these kinds [of abscess] is that which is formed within itself due to the accumulation of moistures.
And the second manner is because of places in which it occurs from the body.
For an abscess occurring on the head is different from one occurring in the anus; and an abscess occurring in a fleshy part is different from one occurring in joints; and each of them has its own judgment according to its operation.
And among abscesses there are those that should not be pierced unless after the maturation of pus within them and its completion; and among them there are those which must be pierced while they are still raw and do not mature according to completion.
Such as abscesses which are close to joints.
For when an abscess occurs near a joint and extends to the point that what is around it putsrefies, perhaps the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted; therefore, there is a greater cause for shortening of that limb.
Or if an abscess occurs near a main member; for if you delay its piercing until it matures, it will harm either that one or through that main member.
Or if it is near the anus, then pierce it even while immature; and do not pierce a raw abscess with incomplete digestion unless it is near the anus lest it putrefies deeply, extends to the interior of the anus, and becomes a fistula that does not heal.
You should know the right time to pierce an abscess that has matured according to completion; this is when there is cessation of pain, reduction of fever, decrease in redness and throbbing, sharpness at the head of the abscess, and other signs.
It is necessary to make the same incision at a lower point on the abscess, at its most prominent swelling. The incision should extend along the length of the body if the abscesses are located on the limbs or joints, sinews, nerves, arteries, and in all other equal places where there is no reflection.
But for those that are reflected, proceed with the incision according to that limb.
When an abscess is located in fatty tissue, it is better to delay the incision until its digestion becomes firm, as we have said according to completion.
For if you pierce it before its digestion is firm, the course of the virus will be prolonged from it, and there will be much foulness and filthiness, and perhaps its lips will harden and its depth increase.
Some abscesses are incised along the length of the body as needed, or according to what the limb requires.
It is necessary to make a small incision for small abscesses and a large one for large ones; or multiple incisions similar in size to the abscess.
There are also abscesses in which it is necessary to excavate the skin and make an incision when it has become as thin as a cloth and reached its termination, so that it is already dead; such as those that occur in many cases of swelling and buboes.
And among these are those which are incised with a three-cornered cut. And among them is one that is incised like the figure of a myrtle leaf.
Such as an inguinal abscess.
And among them are those in which a circular and crescent-shaped incision is made; similar incisions are also used for these.
But those without a head, like soft subcutaneous abscesses, should be punctured with a superficial or flat incision.
And when the abscess is large and has collected much matter, it should not be rushed to expel all the pus at once upon puncturing; rather, a portion of it should be drawn out. Then apply a dressing to the abscess until the next day. Then draw out some more of the pus again.
Do this repeatedly in stages, until it is completely drained, especially if the patient is weak or an elderly person, a pregnant woman, or a young child, as the vital spirit is easily depleted along with the discharge of pus. Otherwise, the patient may die and you might not even realize it.
Therefore, be very careful with this chapter.
And after puncturing the abscess as described above, you should cleanse the wound and observe it carefully. If the wound is small or if a flat incision was made, then apply a lint pad or an old cloth compress. And if the abscess is large and there are many punctures, then insert a lint pad into each one until they join together.
And if you have excised a portion of the skin around the abscess or made an indentation in it, then fill it with old cotton, like when dressing a cloth wound or shaving lint without moisture. Then bind it for three days.
Then remove the dressing and treat it with the appropriate ointment until it heals.
But if bleeding occurs during your procedure, then apply cold water and vinegar, after they have been soaked into a cloth, to the area of bleeding repeatedly.
But if the bleeding persists, then apply the remedies, treatments, and powders that I have mentioned elsewhere in this book according to their indications.
If it is wintertime and the abscess is near many nerves, then apply hot poultices made of linseed meal with wine and oil.
And if it is summertime and the area is fleshy, then apply poultices moistened with water and oil, or with wine and oil, ensuring that all are cold. Until the third day comes, as we have said, then you should open the abscess, clean it, and administer whatever treatment is appropriate until it heals.
This is therefore all that is necessary concerning the general knowledge of treating abscesses.
In accordance with the path of distinction, I have already spoken about every kind of abscess and how to treat each one individually.
Chapter 41
Chapter on the Incision of Abscesses that Occur on the Scalp
Small abscesses occur on the scalp which are like nodules enclosed by their own sacs, resembling swallowed chickens' eggs.
And there are many kinds of them.
Among them are the fleshy ones; and those that contain moisture like mud; and those that contain a fluid like pus or mucus; and those resembling these; and there are also stone-hard ones.
And in the incision of all kinds of these abscesses, and their removal, there is no fear, provided that an artery does not interfere with their incision.
The operation for incising these abscesses is to use the instrument whose shape is described below, called an "Introductor," until you know what it contains.
For if it contains moisture, then incise it lengthwise with a superficial incision following this shape: \[ \cdot \]
Begin the incision from point B to point G. When the fluid has been evacuated, then remove the skin K that contains it, and leave none of it behind.
For it often returns if any of it remains.
Then immerse the wound in Egyptian ointment if it is available; otherwise, in water and salt. Fill the wound with it and leave it alone until the next day. For what remains of the matter will rot.
Then repeat the application of Egyptian ointment on the wound for a second and third time, and continue doing so until you are confident that no more matter remains. Then treat the wound with the ointment until it heals.
But if the swelling contains a fatty tumor: then cut it according to this shape, with an incision having the form of a cross. Then insert hooks between the lips of the incision and excoriate it from every part. And strive with all your strength to extract the tissue that contains it.
But if an artery opposes you, then do what I will tell you afterward.
And similarly, do the same for a hard swelling: cut it and treat it in the same manner that we have described.
And the incision over a hard swelling is easier to perform because it contains little blood or moisture. And sometimes there are certain swellings among these which occur on the head of some people, with which there is absolutely no humidity.
That is to say: for I once made an incision on a swelling on the head of an old woman, and found the swelling to be like a solid and hard stone, rough, white, which could not be broken, so that if anyone were to strike it with force, they would wound themselves.
But in other swellings that occur on the head, except for those that occur on the heads of children and at the roots of the ears, let them all be incised superficially.
Or else make a perforation in these swellings always from their lower part, so that the flow of pus may be relieved downward.
Then treat them with what is suitable for them in terms of treatment.
Chapter on the Incision of Swellings in the Neck
Chapter 42
These swellings often occur in the neck, armpits, and groin, and sometimes also in other parts of the body.
And among those that occur in the neck there are both singular and multiple ones; they generate from each other, and every cordlike structure arises within its own sheath, as is the case with knots and swellings of the head, as we have described.
And there are many kinds of these cordlike structures. Among them are those that are hard like stones; and among them are those that contain fluids; and among them are the malignant ones that do not respond to treatment.
Therefore, if you see one among them that appears well disposed to the touch, which is close in color to the skin and moves freely in all directions, and is not attached to any nerves of the neck or tendons or arteries, nor deeply seated: it should be removed by making a superficial incision from its upper part downwards along the line b to the line g, and excoriate it completely on all sides; then extend the two edges of the skin with forceps or many forceps if necessary, as we have described for head swellings, and extract it gradually.
And beware not to cut any vein or nerve.
And use a blunt rather than sharp instrument so that your hand does not slip during the incision, nor disturb the patient unnecessarily, and do not cut anything that is not necessary.
But if you cut a vein or artery and it prevents you from proceeding with the operation, then place powdered frankincense or some other absorbent powder on the wound, and bind it; let it be until the sharpness of the swelling has subsided and the wound begins to soften or shows signs of suppuration.
For blood absorbs the tunic. Therefore return to your operation until it is completed.
Then examine with your index finger if there are any other small cordlike structures remaining; therefore cut them and cleanse them.
But if there is a large vein at the root of the cordlike structure, then it is necessary not to cut that cordlike structure from its root; rather, tie it with doubled strong thread and leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old cotton which you have already immersed in Egyptian ointment. Then care for it yourself.
If the cordlike structure is cut entirely, then you must approximate both lips of the wound and stitch them immediately, after you have seen that there is no excess left.
But if you see that there is excess due to the size of the cordlike structure, then you must intend to cut its root and remove it; and perform the stitching that we have mentioned.
And as for those cordlike structures that contain moisture, pierce them with a superficial puncture so that you can see the location of their maturity; and place the puncture below what follows in the body, as we have said.
Then apply a linen strip with ointment after the puncture, and similar things to remove what remains of corruption until you see that the wound is already clean; then treat it with flesh-generating ointment until it heals.
Chapter 43
On the cutting of the epiglottis due to suffocation occurring inside the throat.
The ancients said that this operation is performed on the epiglottis, but I have not seen anyone in our land who has done it; and this is their written account.
But in those who have synanche (suffocation), the section of the epiglottis should be avoided because it will not provide relief; this is because all the veins and lungs are weak.
But in those who have a warm swelling in the mouth or throat or both tonsils when there is no illness in the canal, one should use the section of the epiglottis to escape from impending destruction due to suffocation.
Therefore, when we want it, we must cut the epiglottis under three or four circles of the epiglottis, making a small incision through its width in what lies between two circles; this incision should be made on the soft part, not on the cartilage. This place is suitable for cutting because it is devoid of flesh and the blood vessels are far away from it.
But if the surgeon is afraid, then he should stretch the skin of the throat with a hook. Then, after opening the skin until it reaches the canal; avoid any blood vessel if you see one of them.
Then cut the soft tissue we mentioned; it is indicated by the flow of mucus from the canal along with what is cut and the loss of voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
Therefore, when the danger of suffocation has passed, approximate the two lips of the skin wound and sew it alone without cartilage.
Then apply medicines that promote tissue growth until healing is complete.
Thus says Albucasis. The summary of this discourse which we have related is that it is not necessary unless it becomes apparent that one of these swellings has already closed the throat of a patient; and if the patient has difficulty breathing and wishes to cut the epiglottis so that he may breathe through the wound and be saved from death, then they commanded that the wound should be left open until the malady is consumed, which takes three days or more. Then they commanded that the wound be sewn up and cared for until it heals.
And this is what I myself have seen: a woman took a knife and inserted it into her own throat and cut into the windpipe.
And I was called to treat her, but found that she was roaring like one about to die.
Therefore, I cleaned the wound and found that only a small amount of blood had flowed from it. Therefore, I was amazed that she had neither cut the vein nor tied it, and yet breath was issuing from the wound.
I then took care of her wound and treated it until it healed. Nothing happened to the slave girl except for hoarseness of voice, and she returned home after a few days in better health.
Therefore I say that there is no fear in cutting the epiglottis.
On the Incision of a Cyst on the Throat.
Chapter 42
This swelling called a hernia of the throat is a large swelling that matches the color of the body and is common among women.
And it comes about in two ways: either naturally or accidentally.
Certainly, there is no innate tendency in the natural type.
The accidental kind occurs in two ways, one of which resembles fatty tumors. And the other way is similar to swellings caused by the obstruction of an artery. And there is danger with this type; one should not attempt to cut it with a knife unless it is small. First probe and examine it with an introducer. If you find it to be like a fatty tumor and not suspended with any veins, then cut it like other tumors. Remove it along with whatever is inside the cyst, if there is one present; if not, then examine its entirety. Then treat the site as appropriate until it heals.
Chapter 45
On Incising over the Type of Tumors
There are many kinds of tumors, and I have already spoken about all their types in the section on divisions. However, here it is necessary to distinguish between a tumor and an abscess, as there may be doubt between them.
I say that when an abscess is hot and causes fever and severe pain until the eruption of excess fluid occurs and the decay is completed, then the fever and heat will cease.
When there is no heat or fever or pain and the swelling contains them, called a kystis sifachin which is a specific sack having the same color as the body, appearing like a chickpea at first but becoming larger or smaller.
And there are two kinds of swellings: either fatty ones or those that contain fluid. The colors of the fluid vary according to what was said earlier under division.
Therefore, when you come to treat a swelling, it is necessary that you examine it and inquire about it first using an instrument called the introductor, as will be described in the following chapter.
The method of examining abscesses and all swellings is to take this instrument and insert it into the moistest place that you find in the swelling. Then gradually turn your fingers along with it until you know that the instrument has pierced the skin. Then bring your hand around according to the size of the abscess.
Then remove the introductor and observe what emerges from its trail.
If fluid of any color flows out, then cut it with a superficial incision as described for other abscesses.
But if no fluid comes out at the introductor's trail, then know that it is fatty. Therefore cut it with an incision in the shape of a cross as shown earlier. Then suspend it with hooks and remove the skin from all sides easily. Keep the kystis intact if possible so that you can extract it along with the swelling.
But if the kystis ruptures during the operation and it is not possible to extract it intact, which happens often, then remove it in pieces until none remains.
For if some of it remains, whether a little or much, the swelling will likely return again.
But if it defeats you and little remains: then fill the wound at its completion with some caustic and sharp powders; and bind the wound, and place upon it what sits upon an aposteme of heat, and treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
But if there is a large swelling, then draw apart its two lips and treat it along with that which is inflamed.
But if you have an opposing pulsating or non-pulsating vein: then hasten, and fill the place with pulverized myrrh; leave it bound for two or three days until the wound putrefies and the ebullition of blood ceases. Then return to cutting what remains of the swelling.
Chapter 46
Concerning external instruments for incision and perforation.
Among these are shapes of intrusive tools; they are three kinds.
For among them there is a large one, and among them there is a medium-sized one, and among them there is a small one.
They should be made of iron with squared ends neatly, so that they may hasten entry into the swelling.
And these are shapes of trial tools, and no, that is, perforators; and one of them is called beris, that is, the perforator; and they are three kinds.
For among them there is a large one, and a medium-sized one, and a small one.
They are suitable for investigating by means of them swellings, and fistulas, and boils from that which is between them of the bones.
And others are made round, smooth, and soft like large needles with which sutures are made, of citrine or of bronze or iron or silver.
And those among them that are made of citrine are the best.
And trial tools are also made of black lead which are suitable for investigating by means of them fistulas whose concavities are reflections so that they may reflect with their smoothness together with those reflections.
There are again three kinds. Because among them there are long ones, medium ones, and short ones, according to the size of the width of the fistula and its narrowness.
Shape of Hooks, and they are many kinds.
Because among them there are simple ones, namely those with one curvature; and they are three kinds as you see, Large, Medium, and Small.
And among them there are blind hooks and they are three kinds.
And among them there are hooks having two curvatures and they are three kinds; and among them there are hooks having three curvatures and they are three kinds; and all these are necessary in their place.
And these are the shapes of knives with which knots and boils are cut and scraped; and they are three kinds: because among them there is a large one, medium, and small.
Their tips with which cutting is done are sharp, the other ends are not sharp; and they are not placed thus except that with them assistance may be given in scraping knots when there is fear of cutting a vein or nerve: and so that by means of them the sick person may be healed, and may find a little rest from burning which he finds at the scraping of an abscess.
They are made of air and they resemble the ray with which distillation is performed; and at its broad end there are hidden holes like those of a syringe; and through it fluid runs inward and outward as desired.
Shape of syringes which are hidden between the fingers during piercing of boils; and they are not perceived by the sick person; and they are three kinds, large, medium, and small.
Forms of Suction Cups with which the flow of blood is cut off; and they are three kinds, large, medium, and small.
Let them be made of brass or glass, round, rather long, as you see; and let them be somewhat thin.
And it is necessary that these kinds of suction cups are with you, large and small: so that you may cut off blood through them swiftly in case of necessity, and then when medicine is not present.
But do not use them for cutting off blood in every part of the body; and use only in fleshy places: such as thighs, hips, arms, breasts, belly, groin, and similar ones, from moist flesh.
And sometimes other small instruments similar to the bark of fisticus are made from them according to their shape; with which blood is cut off when it flows out from a place of phlebotomy, or at an incision of vein or artery: and sometimes they are made round in this shape.
Chapter 47.
Concerning the care of Breasts of men which resemble the breasts of women.
Sometimes the breasts of some men become swollen at the completion of sleep (that is, when they begin to grow during sleep) until they resemble women's breasts; and they remain offensive with abscesses.
He therefore who detests this, it is necessary that he cut over the breasts with a crescent-shaped incision according to this shape out of line, b to line, g. Then scrape off all the fat. Then fill the wound with an embellishing medication. Then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture and care for it until it heals.
But if the breast inclines downward and softens because of its size, as happens to women: then it is necessary that it be cut in two on its upper sides with two incisions similar in shape to a crescent moon, each of which continues with the other at their ends until there is a larger line containing a smaller one, according to this shape from line b to line g. Then scrape off the skin which is between the two lips and remove the fat, and apply what we have said about suturing and powders, which is necessary for healing, until it heals.
But if you do not complete the incision that you ought to make because of the patient's discomfort or because of bleeding: then it is necessary that you fill the wound with cotton soaked in a corrosive ointment and leave it until it corrodes the remaining fat. Then treat it until it heals.
On the perforation of an abscess that occurs under the inguinal region.
Chapter 48
These abscesses that occur under the inguinal region are of hard species of Struma, which contains a cyst, and among them are those that contain fluids.

Chapter 49
On the incision of an abscess that occurs because of an artery and vein.
When an artery is ruptured and the skin above it becomes consolidated, then it often happens that an abscess forms there. The same happens with veins, so that an abscess and swelling occur there as well.
And the signs by which it is recognized that an abscess and swelling are due to an artery or vein are these: because when an abscess is due to an artery, it is long and gathered deep within the body; and when you press on the abscess with your fingers, you feel as if there is a hissing sound.
And that which is due to a vein is round and prominent on the surface of the body.
And cutting into this abscess is an error, especially when it is located under the armpits, groin, neck, and many other places on the body. It is very large indeed. Therefore, it is necessary to avoid treating these abscesses with a knife, and those that are located on the extremities or on the head should also be avoided.
Thus, for those abscesses due to swelling from an opening of an artery, make a cut in the skin along its length. Then open the incision with hooks, and afterwards expose the artery; free it from its sheaths until it is revealed.
Next, insert a needle under it and cause it to pass through until it reaches the other side, and tie the artery with doubled thread in two places, as I have taught you regarding the extraction of the two arteries that are in the temples.
Then puncture the space between the two ligatures with a spatula until all the blood in it has flowed out and the abscess is resolved.
Then apply treatment that produces pus until the ligatures fall away; afterwards treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals.
But if the abscess is due to a cut vein, then you should hold back as much of the skin along with the abscess as possible in your hand. Then insert a needle under the place you are holding and cause it to pass through until it reaches the other side, with doubled thread attached so that you may pull it out on the other side. Then tie the abscess with a good ligature as I have shown you for the vein, taking two parts from both sides with two ends of the thread.
But if you fear that the thread may come out, then insert another needle with a different thread beneath the abscess at the point of incision; press down and secure your threads at four places.
Then cut through the center of the abscess until it opens up; then make an incision for any surplus skin and release what is bound within it.
Then place on it a powder which you have soaked in wine and oil. Then apply dressings and ointments until the wound heals.
Chapter 1.
About an abscess that occurs due to twisting of the nerves.
Just as an abscess occurs in the artery and vein, similarly it happens in nerves due to blows or excessive exertion, etc., most commonly at joints like elbows or heels, and wherever nerve junctions move. Such abscesses are severe.
It appears similar to the rest of the body and is usually without pain until it becomes quite inflamed when pressure is applied, causing numbness akin to stupor.
This abscess does not form deep within the body but is beneath the skin. It can move in any direction and does not travel forward or backward.
Therefore, those occurring at joints should not be lanced with a blade. For it may cause contraction.
But if one occurs on the head or forehead, use a scalpel to make an incision through the skin. If it is a small abscess, then grasp it with forceps and cut it off at its root.
If it is large, then suspend it with a hook and tear it out; remove it and close the wound with sutures. Then dress it until it heals.
Chapter 51
On the Incision of an Abscess that Occurs in the Belly
Some men have many times in their bellies and other parts of their bodies growths called "thelil," resembling fungi in appearance.
Their root is thin, and their head is large, and their lips are turned outward. And there is among them a small one, and also one that greatly enlarges.
I myself have seen a man whose belly contained two such growths, resembling fungi more than anything else: they were alike in whiteness and small root, their lips turned inward, and they were inflamed and discharged moisture constantly.
I cut these growths, and found one to weigh about ten and eight ounces, and the other about six. And their operation is this: if the patient is moist and the color of the growth is white and moist, with a thin root, then incise it with a broad-bladed knife, and have cauteries ready in the fire.
For often much blood flows out at its incision; therefore be prepared to cauterize it if you are overcome by the blood.
But if you see the patient afraid and fearful of being cut with iron, then take a thread made from lead, well-made, and bind it around the growth. Its manner is as we have said, and leave it for two days.
Then add to the binding of lead. Do not cease binding with lead whenever it presses upon the growth until it is severed and falls off by itself without effort.
But if the growth has a large root, there is cause for fear and caution in its incision, especially those that are in the belly.
Nevertheless, it is possible to cut through half of it. Then cauterize it so that it does not return.
Then treat it until the wound heals.
And do not dare to cut into a swelling that is dark in color, has few symptoms, and appears foul: for it is a cancerous abscess; and I will speak more about cancer later on.
Chapter 52
¶ On the treatment of hernias of the navel.
A hernia of the navel may occur due to many causes. Or it happens through tears or openings that are above the belly, and either a bubble of wind or an intestinal loop protrudes from it, just as occurs in other ruptures. Or it arises from blood flowing from a vein or artery, according to what was mentioned previously. Or from constricted windiness within it.
But if it is due to a tear or opening at the navel and there is a prolapse of the intestine, then its appearance resembles that of an abscess, with no pain; it is soft to the touch and has a varied position. And if it is due to prolapse of the intestine, then its position varies even more markedly than what we have described. And when you press it with your fingers, it disappears but then returns; often there is a noise associated with its return. It also becomes greatly enlarged at the entrance to the bladder and causes intense pain.
But if it is due to excessive moisture, then it remains soft when you press it with your hand, and there is no constriction or increase in size.
If it is due to blood, then it appears as an abscess that is dark bluish-red in color.
But if it is due to a fatty tumor, then the swelling is hard, coarse, and large; its size depends on the amount of fat present.
If it is due to windiness, then it feels soft to the touch.
And its treatment is as follows: first, determine whether the hernia of the navel has occurred due to blood from an artery or vein, or windiness. If so, do not attempt to treat it. For there is fear and fright in that matter, as I have taught you in the chapter where I spoke about abscesses caused by an artery or vein.
But if the protrusion of the navel is due to an intestine or a hernia, then you must instruct the patient to hold their breath and remain standing upright. Then make marks with a hot iron around the entire circumference of the navel. Next, instruct them to lie down on their back between your hands. Then circle the navel with a wide bandage around the spot you marked with the cautery. Next, extend the center of the swelling upwards using a large hook. Then bind it firmly around with thread or silk cord, and make sure the knot is tied tightly.
Then open the center of the swelling where you have extended it above the bandage. If you find that it is already captured by the bandage, then extract it carefully and insert your index finger into it. Push the intestine back inside the abdomen, and if you find a hernia, then grasp it with the hook and cut its excess.
But if an artery or vein is contrary to your intent, then bind it very tightly. Then return to the operation. And take two needles and thread two strong threads through them. Insert these needles into the circumference you made around the swelling like a cross, making sure they penetrate.
Then tie the swelling at four points over the needles. If you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the extended flesh rots and falls off on its own, or cut it when it becomes thin and rotten.
Then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals.
But if the navel hernia is from fatty tissue or moisture, then you must hollow out the swelling as we have described. And draw out blood or moisture that you find within it.
Then treat the hernia with something that will hold the place.
Chapter 53
On the treatment of a CANCER.
We have already spoken about the division of species of CANCER and how it can be treated with medications. We have also warned against its treatment with iron lest it should become ulcerated. And we spoke about CANCER generated in the womb and precautions against its treatment.
And the ancients said that sometimes CANCER occurs in a location where its removal is possible, such as CANCER found in the breasts or thighs and similar parts, from which it can be completely removed. Especially when it is just beginning and small.
But when it has spread and become large, you should not attempt to approach it
For indeed I have been unable to cure anyone from this condition, nor have I seen another before me who succeeded in doing so.
And the operation when it is located as we have described, is to cut around and solve the inflamed area with black bile occasionally. Then perform phlebotomy on it if you find it visibly engorged
Then prepare the patient with a preparation for the operation.
Then insert hooks into the CANCER that fit it Then hollow it out from all sides with skin, according to your examination, until no trace of its roots remains. Let the blood flow freely and do not cut it off hastily, but squeeze the area and wipe away thick blood completely either by hand or with whatever instruments are possible at the time
But if there is difficulty in the flow of thick blood from the incision due to an artery or vein, then cauterize the vein until bleeding stops. Then treat it with the remaining treatment until it heals
On the treatment of Dropsy
Chapter 54 We have now described the division of kinds of dropsy and how water collects; also signs of each kind and their treatment with medications.
And the one that is treated with iron is only a single kind of ascites; it does not approach the kind called tympanites, nor at all that called hygrosarcoma, for the latter is fatal.
But when you have treated this species of dropsy called tympanites with medications and your treatment has not helped, For if weakness has already come upon the sick person or there is another illness besides dropsy—for example, coughing, diarrhea, and similar ailments—then beware of treating him with iron, for it carries danger.
But if you see the sick person is robust and there is no other illness besides dropsy alone, neither a child nor an elder: then the way of operation in this case is that you should lift the sick person standing between your hands, and your assistant behind him press his belly with his hand to push the water towards the lower part of the chest.
Then take a pointed spatula of this shape. It should have two pointed ends and be long, with a sharp point like a spathulum. But let it be somewhat shortened gradually, so that you do not pierce through with it during the operation all the way to the intestine and injure him.
Then consider this: For if the dropsy comes from the intestines, then you should lengthen the incision by three finger widths below the navel directly upwards towards the chest.
But if the dropsy is due to an illness of the liver, then your incision should be three fingers to the left of the navel.
And if its cause is the spleen, then the incision should be on the right side; and it must not be made on the side where the sick person wishes to lie, so that the excess fluids do not flow into that weak spot.
Then pierce the whole skin with an instrument. Then insert your hand with a speculum into that incision, and scrape the skin as if you were scraping it off; and the amount scraped should be about the size of a fingernail or somewhat like that.
Then pierce the skin while scraping until the speculum reaches an empty space; and this is the location of water (fluid). Then remove it and insert into the opening an instrument whose shape is as follows. And it resembles a cannula; made of silver, or brass, or bronze, smooth and polished. Its lower part has a small opening, and there are three openings on its sides - two on one side and one on the other. Its end is cut like a reed, as you see, with an eyelet at the upper end.
For when the instrument reaches the water (fluid), it will immediately be drawn up into it; therefore, empty out half of it in an hour.
Because if you remove more than what is necessary from it in an hour, the sick person may perhaps die from the loss of their vital spirit; or a syncope (fainting spell) may occur that approaches death.
But remove from it according to its quantity and according to what the conditions of the sick person indicate to you through the strength of their pulse, and according to their good constitution.
Then remove the instrument and retain the water (fluid).
This is because it is retained immediately due to the skin that retains the opening which is on top of the speculum, about which I have spoken earlier, so that you pierce it in that manner.
Then repeat the procedure on the second day if you see that the sick person tolerates it; and also remove a small quantity of water.
Do this for several days without fear, and guard yourself against error until only a little water remains.
But if you are afraid for the sick person and allow much water to remain, then treat them with burial in hot sand and much sweat in a bath and sunshine, and let them wait until they are thirsty; and treat them with drying medicines until they recover: and cauterize them on the stomach, liver, and spleen after removing the water, according to what I have described previously.
On treating infants born with incomplete urine passage.
Chapter 50.
If a child comes out of its mother's womb and the head of its foreskin is not pierced, or is pierced too narrowly or in the wrong place,
It is therefore necessary to immediately assist with its piercing using a very fine spatula, according to this shape.
Then place a fine lead peg in the hole and tie it; hold it for three or four days.
When he wants to pass urine, remove the peg and let him void. Then replace it.
But if you do not use lead, then urine flowing over the spot may cause it to close up again.
However, one whose hole is too narrow is treated with lead, as we have said, for many days until it widens.
Those whose opening is not in the correct place are harmed. This is because in some newborns the opening is at the tip of the foreskin: therefore they cannot void urine properly until they lift the foreskin with their hand. And there is no procreation because semen cannot reach the womb properly: and it causes great distress.
The method of treatment for this condition is to lay the sick child on its back. Then stretch the head of the foreskin with your left hand using great force, and cut the tip of its head with a sharp needle or scalpel like cutting a reed, so that it resembles the head of the foreskin and so that the hole falls in the middle as it should.
And beware during your procedure from blood flow. For this happens very often. So apply to it what stops bleeding and care for the wound until it heals.
On the Elephantiasis which occurs on the foreskin and head of the penis, and corruption, blackness, and adhesion.
Chapter 65.
Often these elephantiasis occur on the penis, which is an elevation of corrupt flesh.
And among them there is one that is malignant, and another that is not malignant.

But if the elephantiasis is malignant and of foul color, then you must apply a caustic after cutting it off along with its root.

But in the case of adhesion of the foreskin to the head of the wand, which indeed does not occur except in one whose foreskin is healthy and circumcision is not required upon it: and sometimes adhesion occurs due to a wound or an abscess: therefore you should scrape it off with a blunt spatula until the bond is loosened and the head of the wand is freed from all sides.

Chapter 57.
Concerning the purification of infants and the treatment of errors that may happen to them.
Circumcision is not the solution to continuity as other wounds are: nevertheless, since it is our operation by our will and its use is proper for boys, it is necessary that we mark a better operation and an easier path leading to health in it.
I say therefore that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books because they do not use it in their laws and it is only what we have acquired through experience.
It is because I have found most artisans and bloodletting practitioners using cleansing, circumcising with razors or forceps, and binding with thread and cutting with a nail.
And I have already tested all these methods and found no better cleansing than with forceps and binding with thread.
For in cleansing with a razor, the skin often slips away. For the foreskin has two coats of skin, so perhaps the upper one is cut and the lower one remains intact. Therefore, he must make another incision to cut the other [coat], causing pain. And with cleansing done by cautery, that is, digitally, as surgeons use it, the incision of the end of the male organ is not secure because perhaps it may enter its hole.
But with cleansing done by nail, perhaps the iron may slip and spoil your operation. And indeed, the skin of a boy is naturally short, and many are born such that they do not need cleansing, as I have seen.
However, cleansing with forceps and binding with thread has shown me a good result because the forceps are proportionate to the incision, so that the sharpness which is above is like the sharpness which is below. Therefore, when you press your hand [to hold the foreskin], two sharp points are proportionate and they make one incision at once. And compression is made in a similar figure, containing the skin of the penis on all sides, with which there is no possibility of error.
And the way of your own operation is such that you should make him understand, especially a boy who understands somewhat, that you are only tying the thread on his penis and leaving him until another day.
Then make him rejoice and be glad in every possible way according to his understanding. Then make him stand between your prepared hands, and not sit. Hide the scissors in your sleeve or underfoot so that the child sees nothing at all of them nor any of the instruments.
Then insert your hand towards his buttock and blow on the skin, then pull it upwards until the head of the probe emerges. Then cleanse it with what gathers there from filth.
Then ligature the marked place with doubled thread. Then tie below that a little lower. Then hold it with your index finger and thumb while retaining the lower ligature well, and cut between the two ligatures. Then elevate the skin upwards swiftly, and extract the head of the probe. Then let the blood flow gradually (for it is better), and put powder on top. Then place on the powder a cloth in which an egg yolk boiled in rose water mixed with rose oil has been shaken. Then bind it well and leave him on it until the next day. Then treat him with the rest of the treatment until he is healed.
This is the shape of the scissors used for cleansing:
Let the two ends be blunt and the points meet so that no branch of the nail may escape. Let the length of the two sharp parts be equal to the length of the two handles.
The error occurring in cleansing is that perhaps the entire inner skin or a part of it escapes at the incision.
Therefore you should stretch it out with your fingernail before the application of the ligature and cut it according to its length.
But if you cannot hold it with your fingernail, then extract it with a hook and cut it.
But if three days pass and the swelling around the probe remains unabsorbed, then leave it until the swelling is hot. Then remove it easily and cut it according to how it prepares itself for you, but beware of cutting off the head of the probe. This will not harm him.
Therefore treat it with the substances that heal wounds from powders, which we have mentioned in the book on powders. And if it is cut deeper than intended and reaches internal organs, it will still not harm him.
So treat it as we said until it heals.
Chapter 58
On the Extraction of a Retained Foreign Body from the Bladder.
Retention of urine in the bladder occurs due to obstruction caused by a stone, coagulated blood, pus, or flesh growth, and similar conditions, as we have discussed among the methods of treatment in division.
And if the urine does not dissolve and you see that its retention is due to a stone already in the neck of the bladder, then it is necessary for the patient to be positioned on his knees. Then place his feet over his back and have him stretch out his abdomen from the upper part of his back. The patient should hold his breath as much as possible. So press on the neck of the bladder towards the posterior side for the expulsion of the stone, whereby urine is released.
If this does not work as we said and the condition is urgent for the patient, then you must perform its extraction using an instrument called a 'syringe,' which has the following shape: It should be made of silver, smooth, gentle, hollow like a quill's cannula in thinness, about the length of one and a half palms. Its cannula should be fine at its head.
And the method of extracting urine through it is as follows: Take a doubled thread and tie some wool or cotton at its end with a good ligature. Insert the end of the thread into the lower part of the syringe. If there is any leakage from the wool, cut it off with forceps. Then insert the cannula forcibly like a dart.
Then fill the syringe with oil or butter and egg white. Have the patient sit on a chair and expose their bladder, then insert the tip of the syringe with oily lubricant made from olive oil and warm water.
Then gently insert the syringe into the urethra until it reaches the root of the penis.
Then place the bulb above, on the side towards the navel.
Then push the syringe into it until it reaches the bladder. The patient will feel that it has reached an empty space. This is not done in this order unless the path through which it goes is tortuous.
Then gradually pull out the thread with wool. For urine follows the wool.
Then withdraw the wool and let urine flow through its path. Repeat this procedure until the bladder is emptied and the patient finds relief.
On the nature of bladder irrigation with a siphon.
Chapter 59
When an ulcer forms in the bladder or blood congeals in it or pus hardens within it and water and medicines should be poured into it, then use an instrument called a siphon, which is shaped as follows:
It is closed at one end. And it has three holes: two on one side and one on the other, as you can see.
And the concave space where the piston is placed should be exactly its size without any extra space. When it is pulled back with it, some moisture will be drawn from it, and when it is pushed forward with it, it will be expelled far away, just as a siphon expels naphtha, that is, Greek fire in battle.
Therefore, when you wish to project moistures into the bladder, insert the tip of the siphon into the moisture and pull it back while pushing the piston upwards; for the moisture will be drawn into the concavity of the siphon.
Then insert its tip into the penis, as we have described for a syringe. And then push the moisture with it until it reaches the bladder so that the patient may feel it immediately.
The shape of a fine enema syringe used for enemas into the bladder.
It should be made of silver or gold. Its tip should be slightly shaped like an alkahest and below it there should be a hollow where the ligature falls.
Then take a bladder of a ram and place in it the moisture with which you wish to perform an enema on the bladder. Then tie it between two hollows with a strong ligature and double thread, and warm the moisture slightly over the fire.
Then insert the tip of the enema syringe into the penis and afterwards compress your hand over the bladder with gentle pressure until the patient feels that the moisture has reached the bladder.
But if you do not have a present bladder, then take a piece of parchment and make it into a circle, and pierce it all around its circumference. Then insert a strong thread through those holes as they revolve. Then gather the circle together like an apron, after you have placed within it what you wish from moistures, oils, and waters.
Then tie it to the instrument, and do with it as you did with the bladder through compression with your hand until the moisture reaches the bladder.
Chapter 60.
On the Extraction of the Stone.
I have already spoken of the division of kinds of stones and their treatment with medicines; and I have mentioned the difference between stones generated in the kidneys and bladder, and the symptoms of each; and I have spoken of the stone on which manual operation and section is performed: this stone belongs properly to the bladder, and that which adheres to the ureter. I am now narrating the latter with clear explanation and abbreviation.
I say therefore, that stones generated in the bladder most commonly occur in children.
And among its signs is that urine exits the bladder like water in its fineness; and it appears white sand in it, and the sick person rubs his penis frequently, and labors with it, and often hangs down. Then he is raised up, and passes out with it the anus in many cases.
And the cure of children is easy until they reach fourteen years old. And difficult in the elderly. But in young people it is moderate; and in one whose stone is large the cure is easier; but if small, its cure is conversely difficult.
Now when we come to the treatment, it is first necessary to cleanse the sick person with a clyster, which extracts all the excrement that is in his bowels; for sometimes this prevents the discovery of the stone during examination.
Then let the sick person be taken with his feet and shaken and moved towards the lower parts so that the stone may descend to the bottom of the bladder or leap down from its high place several times.
Then make him sit between your hands prepared, and his hands under his buttocks so that the bladder may be entirely inclined towards the lower parts.
Then examine him externally and touch him.
If you feel the stone in space, then hasten immediately with an incision above it.
But if it does not fall with your touch at all, then it is necessary that you wipe off the index finger of your left hand with oil, if the patient is a boy; or the middle finger if he is a full-grown youth. Then insert it into his anus and search for the stone until it stands under your finger. And gradually turn it towards the neck of the bladder.
Then press upon it with your finger and push it towards the external place where you wish to make the incision. And instruct an attendant to press on the bladder with his hand, and instruct another attendant to stretch the testicles upwards with his right hand, and with his other hand to remove the skin which is beneath them in the part near the place where the incision will be made.
Then insert the Spatulum incisium, the form of which is as follows. And find it between the anus and the testicles, not in the middle but to the left side of the birth, and make the incision upon the stone itself, keeping your finger in the anus. And let the incision be transverse, so that it is wider externally and narrower internally, according to what is possible without making the exit of the stone from it too large.
For perhaps the finger which is in the anus will compress the stone at the incision and it will come out without difficulty.
And know that when a stone has corners or edges, its exit is made difficult on account of this.
And when it is smooth like a gland and round, its exit is easy.
Therefore for one that has corners or edges, make an extension in the split.
But if it does not come out thus, then you must hook upon it or use a clamp properly, the end of which is like the crescent moon that holds onto the stone and does not slip off. Or else insert under it an instrument with a curved tip.
But if you cannot do this, then widen the opening somewhat. If something made of blood binds you, cut it off with a scalpel.
But if the stone is larger than one, then firstly introduce a large one into the mouth of the bladder. Then cut upon it. Afterwards insert a small one afterwards; and do likewise if there are more than two.
But if it is very large, then it is ignorance to attempt it with a large cut. For one of two things happens to the sick person, either he dies or a continuous dripping of urine occurs because the place does not consolidate at all.
But remove it by expelling it until it comes out: or break it with forceps and remove it piece by piece.
When you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with frankincense, aloe, and dragon's blood; bind it and place over it a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or rose oil and cold water to cool the hot inflammation.
Then lay the sick person on their back with their head raised, and do not loosen the bandage until the third day. But when you untie it, irrigate the place with water and much oil.
Then treat it with diaphoretic ointment and basilicon ointment until it heals.
But if a hot inflammation with corrosion and similar things occurs in the wound, such as when blood clots in the bladder and prevents urine from flowing out, which is recognized by the exit of blood with urine. Therefore introduce your finger into the wound and remove that blood. For if it remains, it provokes the corruption and putrefaction of the bladder.
Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt; and apply whatever is appropriate from remedies until it heals.
And during the entire period of treatment, you must bind both thighs and press them together because of the medicinal applications placed on the location.
If a stone is small and situated in the urethra's opening, causing an obstruction to urine flow, treat it according to my previous instructions before proceeding to perform an incision. For many times you will be spared from making an incision through this treatment; I have proven that.
And take aPerforator made of iron, which has the shape of a triangular tip and is sharp, fixed onto wood.
Then take a thread and tie it to the urethra below the stone so that it does not retreat back into the bladder.
Next, gently insert the perforating iron into the urethra until the triangular tip reaches the stone. Then slowly rotate the iron with your hand on the stone and attempt to bore through it until you pierce through its other side.
For urine will be immediately relieved.
Then press your hand over the remaining piece of stone on the outer part of the urethra. For it breaks apart and exits with urine, and the patient is cured.
But if this treatment cannot be prepared due to some obstruction, then tie a thread below the stone and another above it. Then cut into the urethra above the stone between the two ligatures. Then extract it. Afterwards, untie the ligature and cleanse any congealed blood that has formed at the wound.
And it is not necessary to tie a thread below the stone unless to prevent it from retreating into the bladder. And another ligature above is only necessary so that when the thread is untied after the stone's removal, the skin returns to its place and covers the wound.
Chapter 61.
§ On the Extraction of a Stone in Women.
A stone is rarely formed in women.
But if a stone happens to any of them, then treatment is difficult and hindered due to many methods.
One of these [causes] is that the woman may perhaps be a virgin.
And secondly, because you will not find a woman who reveals herself to a physician when she is chaste, or among those who have husbands.
The third reason is that you cannot find a woman who knows this art well, especially the operation with hand.
And the fourth reason is the remoteness of the stone's location; therefore it requires deep incision, and there is fear involved.
But if necessity compels you to perform this [operation], then it is necessary that you take a woman physician who knows well; and she is rarely found.
But if you lack this [a woman physician], then seek a chaste, skilled physician and present a midwife well-versed in women's matters or a woman who understands part of this art; therefore make her present and especially instruct her to do everything that you prescribe concerning the investigation of the stone.
Firstly, she must look and see if the woman is a virgin; then it is necessary that she inserts her finger into her anus and seeks the stone.
If she finds it [the stone], then let her hold it with her finger and then instruct the midwife to insert her finger into the patient's vagina and inquire about the stone after placing her left hand over the bladder and compressing it well.
If she finds the stone, then it is necessary that she gradually moves it from the opening of the bladder downwards with all her strength until she reaches it at the root of the thigh.
Then cut over it at the opposite side of the middle of the vulva at the root of the thigh from whichever side you prepare her for and feel the stone in that part; do not remove your finger from the stone but hold it under it, and make the incision small initially. Then insert a probe through that incision.
Then insert a probe through that incision. If he feels the stone, then increase the size of the incision according to how much the stone protrudes from it.
And know that there are many kinds of stones. Among them is a small one and a large one, smooth and rough, long and round with branches.
So know its kind so that it may be made clear to you about that which you desire.
If blood overcomes you, then add powdered myrrh to the wound and hold it there for one hour until the bleeding stops.
Then return to your procedure until the stone comes out; and prepare with you the instruments that I mentioned for extracting a stone from the bladder so that they may assist you in your procedure.
But if the flow of blood overcomes you and you know that the bleeding is from an artery that has been cut, then place powder on the wound and bind it with a suitable bandage; do not remove it and leave the stone alone, do not take it out. For perhaps the woman may die if she is weak. Then dress the wound.
When the bleeding has subsided after a few days and the wound begins to putrefy, then return to your procedure until the stone comes out.
Chapter 62.
Section on a watery hernia.
A watery hernia is not merely a gathering of moisture in the white sac which is beneath the skin containing the testicle, and this is called the sac.
And sometimes it is in its own membrane which nature has prepared for it in the part of the testicle until it bursts; this is another testicle and is the skin of the testicle between the white sac which we mentioned, and this happens rarely.
And indeed, the cause of this branch's formation is weakness that affects the testicles; therefore, this matter flows towards them. And sometimes it occurs due to an impact on the testicles.
This moisture has many colors. Or it may be inclined towards yellow, or red and bloody, or black and foul-smelling, or white and watery; but the latter is more frequent.
And these are the signs by which one knows where a water gathering occurs: For if it is in the white sac which we mentioned, then it is a round abscess, slightly elongated like an egg shape; and the testicle does not appear because moisture covers it on all sides.
And if the moisture is in its own membrane, then it is a round abscess with an egg-like part; and because of this, one thinks that there is another testicle.
And if the moisture is between the skin of the testicles and the white sac, then it falls under sensation.
Therefore, when you wish to know the color of the moisture, then examine the color of the moisture in the abscess by using a square instrument. Based on what comes out in the trace of the instrument inserted, judge about that which is between it.
When we come to the treatment with an iron instrument, then it is necessary that you order the patient, if possible for him, to undergo bloodletting, and see if his body is full.
Then he should lie on his back over something high, and place many cloths under him.
Then sit on his left side, and order the assistant to sit on his right side, who should stretch out his virga towards one of the two sides of the skin of the testicles, and towards the part called mirach of the abdomen.
Then take a broad spatula and cut the skin of the testicles from the middle along its length up to near the pectin; make the incision according to the straightness of an equidistant line that divides the skin of the testicles until you reach the white siphac that encircles it: and strip off its skin and observe it, or be careful not to cut it off; and let your stripping be on the part that hangs with the egg more: and finely examine it with the stripping according to the quantity that is possible for you.
Then pierce the full siphac with a wide piercing and drain out all the water.
Then separate between the two lips of the incision with hooks, and extend the siphac upwards, and do not touch the skin that embraces the testicles: and cut the siphac however it is possible for you to cut it, either with its entirety or in pieces, especially its thin side. For if you do not examine carefully, there will be no safety in its incision, lest water return.
But if the egg protrudes outside its skin during your operation: then when you have completed the incision of the siphac, replace it. Then join together the two lips of the skin of the testicles with a suture.
Then take care of it as you would other wounds until it heals.
But if you find the egg corrupted from another blackness, then it is necessary for you to tie the suspending vessels, fearing a flow of blood.
Then cut off the testicle with its suspender and remove the egg: afterwards take care of it according to what we have said.
If however water is collected in two parts, then know that there are two branches: therefore cut the other part equally as you did the first.
But if it equals to you that it may be one operation, then do thus: afterward wipe away the blood. And introduce into the incisions wool immersed in oil or rose oil: and place outside wool which you have immersed in wine and oil, and spread it over both testicles and the navel: and place over them doubled cloths, that is bandages: and bind them over with a bandage that has six folds according to this shape.
Then two cloths are taken and soaked and filled with red wool, according to this likeness: and they should be finished with cotton or wet wool at the ends with which it is pressed: and they shall be gathered together with a bandage around the loins of the thighs: and one end shall take over the anus up to the back, and the second shall take to the part of the chest, and two ends shall lie on both hips below, and two other ends shall lie on both groins, and the whole bandage shall be gathered together at the loins of the thighs.
And when this branch is artificially treated with a cautery instead of cutting with a knife, and it is such that you take a sharp scalpel cautery, and cut the skin of the testicles with it: and it is heated, according to what we have described: until when the white matter containing water appears, then take the cautery, according to this shape.
The Greek letters are similar.
Then pierce that white matter with it and burn it until all the moisture has come out.
Then stretch out that white matter with hooks and scrap it off with a fine burning cautery, and cut into it as much as you can until you have removed all of it.
And be careful not to burn the egg itself.
And know that this operation is safer and further from bleeding, and better than the operation with cutting.
But if you find opposition at the time of cutting or cauterizing, either a hot abscess or bleeding, or something else: then it is necessary that you treat what opposes you with that treatment which suits it, according to what has been prescribed.
If the patient is fearful and does not await this operation because of its horror: then perform the incision according to how I describe it.
When you have confirmed at your place through what we said about significations that the ram is watery: then it is necessary that you make the patient sit on a raised seat.
Then cut the abscess in its lower part with a wide spatula and make the incision along the length of the body, and let the opening be ample until all water runs out.
Then put a pad on the wound and tie it, and let him go so that the remaining water runs out: then treat it until the wound heals.
If opposition is to the exit of water at the time when you cut it and there is nothing else but because a white cloth opposes at the wound's opening and hinders the exit of water: then it is necessary that you insert a feather into the wound, or an instrument with which water is drawn out from hydropic tumors, whose shape has been described before, or add a little to the operation on the wound.
And know that water returns and collects after six months, or approximately that time. When it has collected, then cut it again in the same way, and the patient will pass his days.
Chapter 63.
[On] The treatment of fleshly rams.
You should know that in cutting into this ram, there is danger leading to harm in many dispositions. Therefore, it seems to me that its release would be safer from it.
And I am remembering the operation on it and the appearance of the abscess.
I say therefore that many swellings occur in bodies from which the testicles are composed: and this happens due to many causes, namely.
Either from an acute or harsh fluid that flows towards the testicles. Or from injury.
And the color of the swelling is like the color of the body, and there is no pain found in it; and it is a severe swelling.
And perhaps it is a stone-like swelling, whose color is dark, and there is no sensation in it.
And sometimes it happens from a blockage of the arteries and their dilation; as has already been mentioned previously: or from dilation of veins.
That which occurs from them due to dilation of arteries is recognized by the arrangement of the swelling when you press it with your fingers. And do not touch it at all.
But that which occurs from dilation of veins does not disperse at all when pressed with fingers.
And now it is possible that on this appearance, incision and on the remaining flesh is done: and it is, as you separate the skin of the testicles. Then extend the testicle upwards and remove it with a white silk thread, and free the suspensiony ligament from its vessels, and ligate the vessels, and cut the suspensiony ligament after you have freed it from all parts of the testicle.
If however the testicle is adherent to those surrounding tissues, then it is necessary that you draw out the testicle and cut it.
And if there is adhesion between any part of the scrotum or what is between the vessels, then it is necessary that you free all that adhesion and cut it with a round incision.
But if there is growth of flesh in the place where adhesion occurs that is behind, then it is necessary that you cut all of it and remove the testicle as we have said.
When your operation is complete, then fill the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and wine, and then treat it with the usual treatment until it heals.
Chapter XLIV.
Concerning the treatment of a broken limb, which is accompanied by a dislocation.
An abscess has formed, wrapped around with a certain wrapping, similar to a funnel, along with the softening of the testicles; and it becomes difficult for the sick person to move and exercise, and to walk.
And in the treatment of this ailment, there is fear, because of the memory of what preceded it.
But it is necessary for us to say the operation performed on it according to what the first ones did.
I say therefore that you should make the sick person sit on an elevated seat. Then push the suspending ligament of the testicles downward. Then grasp the skin of the testicles with your fingers, near the vessels that are close to the penis; and let another assistant hold it and stretch it out forcefully.
Then make a wide, oblique incision with a sharp scalpel across the direct course of the vessels until they are exposed.
Then strip it from all sides, as we have told you regarding the extraction of arteries that are in the temples.
Then insert a needle into it with a doubled thread and tie it at the first points where the dislocations occur; and tie it again at the last of them. Then cut it straight across in the middle section along the length of the body, and remove what has accumulated from corrupted foul humors.
Then treat the wound with the usual treatment for other wounds, so that pus may be generated to allow the vessels that nourish one of the testicles to fall away. For there is no harm in this.
But if all the vessels are dislocated, then it is necessary to remove the testicles along with the vessels; lest on account of the incision, the egg be deprived of nourishment and waste away or shrivel up, and not have any benefit.
Chapter 65.
On the treatment of twisted intestine.
The cause of this twisting is from a rupture which occurs when the intestines are extended over the abdomen in the groin region due to intestinal obstruction.
Therefore, the intestines descend through that rupture into one of the scrotal sacs. And this rupture occurs either due to a sudden straining or an extension of it.
And these two types occur for many reasons. Or from a blow. Or from falling. Or from a sudden outcry. Or lifting of heavy objects and similar causes.
And the sign is when it occurs from the extension of the straining. It happens gradually over a long period and does not occur suddenly; it is an equal swelling towards the depth because the straining compresses the intestine.
And its sign when it occurs from a rupture of the straining is that it causes sudden severe pain at the outset; and it is an uneven swelling appearing near the skin; this is due to the exit of the intestine and its course towards the external opening.
And sometimes it comes out with a loop of intestine; this condition is called 'twisted intestine' and 'hernia'; and sometimes it occurs with wind.
And sometimes feces run into the intestine and become lodged there; this causes great harm to the patient, especially when it presses down.
And in treating these conditions with iron, caution must be exercised to avoid accidents.
The method of treatment is that you should instruct the patient to gently push the intestine back into its proper place with their hand if it can be reduced. Then, make the patient lie on their back between your hands and raise their legs.
Then stretch the skin that follows the skin upwards and cut all the skin of the testicles along its length. Then fix forceps on both lips of the incision according to your need to open it with them; and let the incision be made as far as possible so that an egg can pass through.
Then strip off the spermatic cord and what is under the skin of the testicles until when the white spermatic cord is completely revealed, hard on all sides. Then insert your index finger behind the testicle, between the white spermatic cord and the skin of the egg, and separate without any adhesion that is in the posterior part of the egg.
Then move the testicle with your right hand towards the interior of its skin and stretch the white spermatic cord upwards with your left hand. Then raise the egg with the spermatic cord towards the part of the incision, and tell your assistant to extend the egg upwards, and you dissolve the adhesion that is behind it completely.
And search with your fingers that there is nothing there of the intestine involved in the hard white spermatic cord. But if you find something from it, then push it down towards the abdomen below.
Then take a needle with a thick thread made of ten threads and insert it under the last spermatic cord, that is, the tunic which is under the skin following the incision. Then cut the ends that stick out from the thread until there are four threads remaining. Then arrange some of them over others in the shape of a cross and tie them together with the spermatic cord which we mentioned earlier, because it is under the skin of the testicles with a strong ligament, until no vessel that nourishes them can send anything to them so that no hot abscess occurs. And place again another ligature according to the first one, distant from it by two fingers less. And after these two ligatures leave a portion of the spermatic cord equal to the size of your finger under the skin of the testicles and cut the rest around its circumference and remove the egg.
Then make an incision in the lower part of the skin of the testicles from which blood and pus flow as we have described earlier.
Then apply a piece of linen soaked in oil to the wound and tie it with the ligature that was mentioned. And sometimes the spermatic cord that we have spoken of is cauterized after its incision is made to prevent bleeding. For this happens very often. Leave the ligature until it falls off by itself.
But if its falling off is delayed, then treat it with warm water and apply what was mentioned earlier until it falls off.
Then dress the wound with the usual dressing of wounds until it heals.
Chapter 66
On the Hollow Needle.
In this needle, I have not seen anyone dare to use iron for its cautery.
And the earliest authors said that it is done like we described for the needle with a vinegar coating. That is, tie the vessels after cutting them below with ease, then cut in the middle and treat with what opens it until the vessels fall off, and dress the wound according to what was said until it heals.
Chapter 67
On Disruption of the Groin.
When a rupture occurs in the groin as we have described and a swelling appears but nothing from the intestines descends to the testicles: and if something does descend it is small and returns at all times: truly if this condition persists for a long time, a fissure forms in the spermatic cord until the intestines descend into it.
And this occurs due to the stretching of the spermatic cord which is in the groin as we have said. That is, because the spermatic cord stretches, becomes softened and develops an abscess at the site of prominence.
And its treatment is with a cautery, as has been described above.
And sometimes it is treated with iron (i.e., surgery), in this manner: and it should be that the patient lies on their back between your hands. Then cut the location of the rupture, abscesses, and prominence with a section across its width, according to the measure of three fingers.
Then tie the spermatic cord tightly, squeezing what is beneath the skin until the white spermatic cord becomes apparent under the following skin.
Then take a cautery and place it on the prominent area of the spermatic cord, pressing it until it reaches the depth of the abdomen.
Then cauterize two points on either side of the prominence of the spermatic cord and connect them with a suture; then remove the end of the cautery.
And do not cut the spermatic cord entirely, nor stretch it out nor anything else, as I have taught you in treating prolapsed intestines.
Then treat it with wound treatment. Therefore, when cutting the threads, cleanse them and restore the wound until it is healed.
For the spermatic cord contracts and does not regain its prominence after rupture.
And a cautery is better in this location because it is closer to healing.
Chapter 68
On the softening of the skin covering the testes.
The skin covering the testes is often softened in some men, causing the flesh between them to become softened and making their appearance unsightly.
Therefore, it is necessary for the one who is asked to treat it that he makes the patient lie on their back and cuts all the softened skin on top of the natural skin.
Then bring together both lips with a suture: and if you wish, first remove the excess softened skin by three or four sutures, and then make a firm suture.
Then cut between the sutures.
Then treat it with wound treatment until it heals, and remove the stitches.
Chapter 69
Section on Castration
Know that castration is prohibited by our law. Therefore, it would be permissible for me not to mention it in this book of mine, as it is not necessary except for two methods.
One of these is so that the doctor may know about it when questioned concerning it; and also to know how to treat someone who has suffered from it.
And another reason is that we often need castration for certain animals, due to our sustenance through them, such as rams, roosters, cats and similar animals.
I say therefore that castration is performed in two ways. Or by friction. Or by cutting and tearing apart.
In the case of castration performed by friction, this is how the operation is carried out: Have the animal sit in warm water until its testicles soften and become lax, and hang down. Then strike them with your hand until they loosen and no longer appear to touch.
In castration by cutting and tearing apart, it is necessary that you hold the animal and press its scrotal skin with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory ligament and make one cut above both testicles until they protrude. Then cut them both after scraping each off, leaving nothing but a thin membrane over the blood vessels.
And this method of castration is better than that done by friction because friction might leave behind some part of the testicles, which causes the animal to desire mating.
Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter 70
Section on Treatment of Hermaphrodites in Two Methods
A hermaphrodite is of two kinds among males. One of them appears as a space between the two testes or on the scrotal skin between them, resembling a small vulva with hairs, from which urine sometimes flows.
In women there is one type, which is the vulva on top of the pubic region like small scrotal sacs, protruding externally; one resembles the penis of a man, and two resemble the testes.
The treatment for all three types — two from men and one from women — is the same: it is necessary to cut away the excess flesh until the indentation disappears. Then treat them with the usual care given to other wounds.
In the second type of men's condition, from which urine emerges, located on the scrotal skin, there is no operation nor any cure for it at all.
Chapter 71
