On the Incision of Hemorrhoids and Excess Flesh Protruding from Women's Vulvas
Hemorrhoids may additionally appear externally, causing disfigurement and ugliness until they become prominent like those of men, reaching even to the point of sexual intercourse.
Therefore, hold the excess hemorrhoids with your hand or hook and cut them; do not cut too deeply, especially at the root, to avoid bleeding.
Then treat it as you would any wound until it heals.
The flesh that grows is the flesh that develops at the mouth of the uterus until it fills it, and sometimes it protrudes externally resembling a tail. Some people therefore call this condition "tail-like. Therefore, we should cut it like we do hemorrhoids and treat it until it heals.
On the Treatment of Phimosis, Which is an Uncircumcised Vulva
Chapter 72
Phimosis is when a woman's vulva is not pierced or has a small opening. And it may be either natural or acquired.
An acquired one is from the sickness which preceded it; and indeed it occurs either from flesh born within, or from thin or thick growth, and it may occur either in the depths of the womb, or on its sides, or above or below it; and it hinders copulation and impregnation, and birth, and perhaps it hinders menstruation.
And knowledge of this is gained through the examination by an obstetrician if there is an apparent swelling nearby.
But if it is not apparent, then seek it out with your fingers or with a probe.
But if the swelling is from thin membrane and is near the two lips, then hasten and tear it apart.
And place on the two lips a little cotton wool.
Then place your index fingers of both hands together, and let the woman lie on her back with her legs spread apart. Then stretch the two lips with force until the thin membrane tears and the swelling opens.
Then take some wool, dip it in oil, and place it on the location; and let the woman copulate every day so that the location does not harden again.
But if the growth is thick and dense, then it must be cut away with a broad knife like the leaf of a fig tree.
But if the swelling is from flesh born within, then suspend it with hooks and cut into it; and have with you medicines that stop the flow of blood without biting, such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and boiled incense with egg white.
Then administer a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not heal quickly; hold it in place for several days; and then apply a linen dressing made from dry flax, after which treat the remaining wound until it heals.
And sometimes other flesh is born in the womb; therefore it should be cut according to this method: while there is no cancerous abscess, for a cancerous abscess that is in the womb must not be treated with iron at all.
Chapter 73
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, piles, and red boils in women's vaginas.
Hemorrhoids indeed are the opening of the veins' orifices until blood flows out from them abundantly and continuously.
When hemorrhoids are hardened, piles form; and I have already spoken of their kinds and signs in the division, and here I will say which ones receive treatment and which do not.
I say therefore that hemorrhoids which are at the mouth of the womb and are not visible to the senses then there is no treatment with iron for them.
But those that are at the opening of the womb upon which sensation falls are those that should be treated.
Therefore it is necessary to put the woman into a cold house Then extend the piles with forceps or rough cloth and cut them off from their roots.
Then powder them over at the flow of blood without cauterization, using powders such as acacia, dragon's blood, and frankincense, and similar ones.
Then make her press her feet against the wall for one hour. Afterwards put her into cold water.
But if the bleeding persists then make her sit in a decoction of sumac and pomegranate peel, and chicken feathers, and similar things.
Then take barley flour, mix it with honey and vinegar, and make a plaster with it for her back.
If the blood is cut off with it, that's good; but if not, make ventouses adhere to her back and breasts without incision.
Then take wool and immerse it in the juice of tartar or jujube fruit or hollyhock root, mixed with Pontic wine, and apply it to the place.
When the abscess has quieted down, then treat the area with ointments until it heals.
Albart is red in color, resembling the heads of crows, and appears rough to the touch.
Therefore, you should cut off what appears from it, as I said previously about the eyelid equally: and treat it until it heals.
Chapter 74
Chapter on Perforation of the Uterus which occurs in Childbirth.
Many kinds of swellings occur in the uterus, such as cancer, stone swelling, ulcers, adubelati, alekilati (herpes), aestimones, fistulas, hemorrhoids, ragades, eyelid swellings, and hot abscesses.
And indeed I have already spoken of all these diseases and their kinds, symptoms, and treatments in division: it is necessary that I speak in this treatise of the hot swelling which occurs in the uterus, when it is from abscesses which retain blood, and you wish to know how its opening is made with an iron instrument.
Therefore you should examine If the pain of the abscess at its onset is sharp with pulsation, and fever, and inflammation: and you see it becoming red: if sensation falls upon it, then do not rush to perform a perforation on it: but treat it with what causes maturation until when the sharpness of the occurrences has quieted.
Then you should make the woman sit on a chair that has two steps and lean back or lie on her back, and bring her feet close to the lower part of her belly: and let her hips be spread out, and let her put her arms under her knees, and tie both sides with a suitable bandage to it.
Then let the midwife sit on the right side, and assist with the instrument with which the opening of the uterus is made.
And it is necessary when you wish to administer this instrument that you should penetrate deep into the uterus of the woman with the speculum, so that what enters from the instrument may not exceed the depth of the uterus and injure the woman.
But if the instrument is longer than the depth of the uterus, then it is necessary that you place pads upon the cover of the depth of the uterus, so that the entrance of the instrument may be prevented from going entirely into the depth of the uterus. And it is necessary that she who turns the wheel should stand on the upper side, and that she should hold the instrument and cause the servant to turn the wheel until the depth of the uterus is opened.
When therefore the issue appears and is touched with the hand, and is soft and slack, then it is necessary that you should cut it off at its head with a wide-bladed knife.
When therefore the discharge is evacuated, then it is necessary that you should place upon the wound a linen cloth dipped in rose oil or green oil which has some astringency, and place the linen and draw out the portion into the depth of the uterus.
And place externally upon the uterus, and upon the pubis, clean wool dipped in water wherein mallows have been boiled.
Then let it be cared for on the second day with an ointment that is suitable to it until it heals.
And sometimes the uterus and wound are washed thus that a clyster is given with water and honey; then with water wherein the root of lily or long black cohosh has been boiled. Then it returns to the use of ointment.
But if the wound is hidden internally within the uterus, then it is necessary that you should refrain from treating it with iron, nay rather treat it with what we have said in division.
Chapter 70.
¶ The Doctrine of Midwifery: How live embryos are treated that do not come forth naturally according to their shape.
An obstetrician must first know the form of a natural delivery.
From these signs, it is that when you see a woman being pressed downward and she desires to smell the air and is relieved of what is upon her regarding childbirth, and she hastens the exit of the embryo: then know that this birth is according to its natural form, and its exit is on its head, and the afterbirth with it: either suspended or pendent by the umbilical cord.
Therefore, when you see these signs, then it is necessary to press down on her womb so that the embryo descends quickly.
For when it descends upon its head, the afterbirth comes with it and is cleansed of those superfluities by a good cleansing.
But one that is other than this birth is unnatural and reprehensible.
For sometimes the fetus comes out on its feet and its body comes forth before its head, with one foot or hand first, or both feet: or the head and hands come forth: or it emerges buttocks-first; and perhaps it turns upon its neck, and other such unseemly forms.
Therefore, it is necessary for the obstetrician to be prudent and skilled in all these matters, and let him guard against error and deception. And I shall show every form among them, and how they are managed therein, and what is signified thereby, and what should be done about it.
When the fetus comes forth on its head in a natural emergence and there is great pain upon the woman and her labor becomes difficult, and you see that her strength is already weakened: then have her sit upon a chair or let women hold her up; and apply to her vulva warm water decocted with fenugreek and moist oils.
Then the midwife should insert a small spatula between her fingers and use it to divide the afterbirth, cutting with her nail until the moisture runs out; then press down on the woman's womb until the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then the woman should be given an enema of fenugreek mucilage with sesame oil.
Then instruct her to lie on her back after the enema; cause her to sneeze with mustard powder, and hold her mouth and nose shut for one hour. For the fetus will come out immediately.
But if two hands of the fetus emerge first, they should be gently pushed back little by little. But if it does not obey at the entrance, then place the woman on a seat and raise her feet up; then shake the seat on the ground; and hold the woman so that she does not fall during the shaking.
If, however, the hands do not emerge and the fetus dies, then cut them and remove the remainder of the fetus; bind her hand with a cloth and gently pull it out.
¶ Exit of the embryo through the feet. When the fetus comes out feet first, then press them upwards; gradually turn the fetus; and then bring it forth.
Then take one of its feet and slowly stretch it out.
When they have emerged up to the hips, then instruct her to lie on her back and cause her to sneeze with mustard powder; for it will come out if God wills.
And if it does not come out as mentioned above, then gradually push the fetus back until you have placed it in its natural position. For it will then come out with ease.
But if it is still not coming out with all that has been mentioned, then take some psyllium mucilage and sesame oil and dissolved gum, grind them well together in a mortar; then apply this mixture to the woman's vulva and below her belly. Then have her sit in warm water until she feels embarrassed.
Therefore, when you see that her lower parts are heated up, then make for her a poultice of myrrh and apply it. Then, after holding the poultice for one hour, have her sit on a chair. Then make her sneeze; hold her mouth and nose closed; and press gently on the lower part of her belly. For the fetus will come out immediately.
The exit of the fetus is on knees and hands. When it comes out according to this method, then gradually insert your hands inside her. Then the woman should lie on her back and let her feet hang down towards the floor; she herself should be lying on a bed, and she should fold her hands together. Then press gently on her belly gradually. Then remove what is under her feet from the blankets until she is prepared below. But if the fetus does not come out, then take hold of her feet and move them violently. Then press gently on what is above her buttocks gradually until the fetus rises up. Then insert her hands and guide the fetus out gradually; and tell the woman to press down until the fetus comes out.
The exit of the fetus should hang downwards between the hands. Insert your hand gently inside her. But if she cannot reduce it herself, then lift up the woman and make her walk around.
But if she cannot walk, then have her lie on a bed; and shake her legs violently.
Then apply the liniment you have prepared from mucilages.
But if she is seized by labor pains, then make her sit on a chair. Then strive for the reduction of the hand and make the fetus follow its natural shape. And cause the woman to sneeze and instruct her to press herself until it emerges.
The exit of the fetus is upon its own neck, with hands spread out, and its face is towards the back of its mother. It is necessary for the midwife to hold the hand of the fetus and then gradually turn it and make it equal with ease.
When it has been made even, then place upon its vulva what we have mentioned earlier from oil and mucilage.
Then instruct her to move gradually. After you have done this, then make her sit on a chair and instruct her to lean towards the right side. Then instruct her to press herself downwards: and cause her to sneeze with some force.
The exit of the fetus is prepared on its side. When it descends to the opening of the womb in this manner along with the caul, then split the caul with your nail until it is emptied from moisture.
If however it hangs upon the face of the fetus and its neck, then cut it from the navel so that it may not remain there and cause death.
Then push the fetus inside until you place it properly and instruct the woman to press herself occasionally; for it will emerge easily.
The exit of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are often born and sometimes three or four are born at once. But this is rare and seldom happens.
But five is against nature and they do not live at all. And although there may be many of them, they are contained within one caul. And they are separated by the umbilical cord, each being distinguished from the other by its separate connection to it.
And sometimes they emerge like a single fetus through the natural and easy exit; but they also emerge in a way that is not praiseworthy, as we have described.
If some of them are difficult at birth and reach the condition we have described with unnatural shapes, then treat them as we have told you: use a fine thread and act gently and slowly, guiding it according to every shape with skill leading towards healing.
Reminder of what is formed in the womb from embryos and miscarriages.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, or even more than ten are formed in the womb; and it has been verified by me that a woman miscarried seven of them, and another fifteen, all of which were already formed.
Which was done with the measurement of the glorious wise one.
Therefore, all of this should be included in the science of obstetrics. For perhaps something similar may happen to her; therefore, she should oppose it with what is necessary.
Chapter 76
On the extraction of a dead fetus.
When you attempt to deliver a dead fetus using the methods I have mentioned in division and medication, but it does not emerge, and you are forced to use manual extraction, then you must consider [the following].
If the woman is of sound health and free from any accidents that threaten her with harm, then she should be laid on her back on the bed, with her head hanging downwards and her legs raised up. A servant should hold her at both sides or she should be tied to the bed so that her body does not move during the extraction of the fetus.
Then moisten the mouth of the womb with moistened oils containing gum tragacanth and fenugreek seeds, and flaxseed.
Then the midwife should anoint her hand with these oils and mucilages, and gently insert it into the womb. Then she should seek with her hand the place where hooks may be attached to the fetus, and suitable locations for that purpose.
Therefore, if the fetus is ready to be born feet-first, then attach hooks to its eyes, neck, mouth, palate, beard, collarbone, nearby ribs, or beneath the armpits.
But if it is being born feet-first, then attach hooks to its pubic bone and middle ribs.
And she must hold the hook with her right hand and place its loop between the fingers of her left hand. Then gently insert her hand and attach the hook to one of those places mentioned earlier until she reaches a vacant space.
Then attach another hook opposite it, or even a third if necessary, so that the traction is equal on both sides and does not lean towards one side.
Then traction should be applied evenly and not just in a straight line; rather, it should move in all directions to ease its emergence like the extraction of a tooth.
And the traction should be softened between them. If a part is stuck, then the midwife should anoint some of her fingers with oil and insert them from that side to twist what is holding it back.
If a part of the fetus comes out, then move the hooks to another higher location and continue this until the entire fetus is born.
If a hand emerges first and cannot be pushed back due to its constriction, then a cloth should be wrapped around it. Then pull it out until the entire hand emerges, cut it at the wrist or elbow joint, and do the same for the other hand and both feet.
If the fetus's head is large or has constriction at its exit or if there is accumulated water on its head, then insert a spiney spatula between its fingers and use it to open the head and drain the water. Alternatively, break it with an instrument called the Almisdac, whose shape will be shown in the next chapter.
Similarly, if the fetus is of a large head by nature, then you should perform a caesarean section or remove it with the Almisdac, as mentioned before. Then grasp the bones with forceps.
But if the head emerges but is obstructed at the fork [of the pelvis], then it should be opened until the fluid in the chest is released.
But if it does not turn and does not obey to emergence, then cut off the head piecemeal as much as you can.
If there is an abscess below the abdomen or hydrops (fluid accumulation), then pierce it until the fluid flows out.
But if the fetus is breech (feet-first), its extraction is easy and its direction towards the mother's outlet is straightforward.
But if it is obstructed at the abdomen or chest, then remove it with a cloth wrapped around your hand and open the abdomen or chest until what is inside runs out.
But if the rest of the limbs are removed and the head returns, then the midwife should insert her left hand. If the mother's outlet is open, then insert your hand deeply into the uterus and search for the head with it, grasping it with your fingers up to the mother's outlet. Then insert one hook or two hooks and pull it out with them.
But if the mother's outlet is obstructed due to an acute abscess, then do not exert force on it. Instead, administer hot humidifications and ointments and sit in waters that soften and moisten.
If the fetus is lying on its side, it can still be turned upright; then apply what we have said for live births.
But if that is not possible, then make an incision piece by piece and remove it.
And nothing of the afterbirth should be left inside entirely.
After completing your procedure, then apply what we have said for treating hot abscesses that occur in matrices.
If bleeding occurs, then let her sit in astringent waters and apply the rest of the treatment.
And indeed I have seen a woman whose womb was dead with a fetus; then she became pregnant again upon it, and afterwards another fetus died. Therefore, after a long time, an abscess occurred at her navel, which swelled until it broke open and began to discharge pus.
I was called to treat her, and I treated her for a long time; yet the wound did not heal.
So I applied to it certain plasters of strong drawing power, and the bone came out from its place. Then after some days, another bone came out. I wondered at this, for there is no bone in the womb.
So I concluded that they were from the bones of the dead fetus.
So I examined the wound and extracted many bones while she was in good condition; now she lives well after a long time, and only discharges a little pus from that place.
And I would not have mentioned this here except that it contains knowledge and assistance for that which the skilled physician administers with his hand in treatment.
Chapter 77
On the Shapes of Necessary Instruments for Extracting a Fetus
Shape of the Revolving Part by which the matrix is opened.
Here below is the shape of the Bookholder, with which books are made even, having two revolving parts at both ends of two pieces of wood. However, this Revolving Part ought to be more delicate than the Bookholder itself; and it should be made either from ebony or boxwood.
And let the width of each piece of wood be like two fingers; and the thickness of both shall be like one finger, and their length like a palm and half; and between the two pieces of wood there are two supports of the same kind of wood, which are already fixed on both sides, whose length is half a palm plus somewhat little; and the width of each is like two fingers, or somewhat more.
And these two supports are inserted into the mouth of the matrix so that it opens with them when you turn both Revolving Parts.
Another shape similar to this one, more delicate and lighter.
It should be made of ebony or boxwood, according to the likeness of forceps. However, at its end there should be two supports like you see above; and the length of each support is like a palm; and the width of both is like two fingers.
When you wish to open the matrix with it, then make the woman sit on a bed, with her feet hanging down, having opened what is between her legs. Then insert these two connected supports into the matrix; and you hold the lower end of the instrument between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument as you do with forceps, evenly according to the amount you wish from the opening of the mouth of the matrix, until the Midwife does what she wants.
Shape of another Revolving Part which the ancients called the Shape for Pushing, with which the fetus is pushed.
Shape of the Almisdac, used to crush a large head; it resembles forceps and has teeth at its end as you see.
And sometimes it is made oblong like forceps according to this shape, with teeth like saw teeth, used for cutting and crushing.
Form of the "Uncinus" having one spine
Form of the "Uncinus" having two spines
Form of the "Spatumalis" having two ends for cutting off the head
Shape of two "Spatumilium" wider apart.
All these instruments should be used and the more numerous their kinds and the better prepared they are by the craftsman, the swifter is their use and the greater their reputation among people.
Therefore do not disregard any of these instruments without having it prepared at hand. For their need is indispensable.
Chapter 78
Chapter on the Extraction of a Retained Placenta
When the placenta is retained at birth, then it is necessary to instruct the woman in labor to hold her breath. Next, make her sneeze with condis and place your hand on her lower abdomen, covering her nostrils. If it comes out this way, that is good.
If not, then take a pot and make a hole in its cover. Place in it herbs that open the mouth of the womb such as calamentum, rue, anethum, chamomile, sesame, and cassia wood, centaurium: take all these herbs or some of them, submerge them in water, and place them on a fire.
Then place a hollow reed over the hole of the pot's cover and the other end into the mouth of the womb. Hold it until steam reaches the cavity of the womb.
Then make her sneeze as we have described. For the placenta will come out easily this way.
If, however, after this treatment the placenta remains and does not come out, then instruct the midwife to immerse her hand in oil of sesame or dough made from flaxseed. Then she should insert it into the uterus and search for the secundines with it. When she has found it, then let her take hold of it and pull it out gradually.
But if it is attached deeply within the uterus, then she should insert her hand as we have described until she finds the secundines. Then she should pull them out gradually along their length so that the uterus does not collapse due to strong traction; rather, it should be moved gently towards the left and right sides alternately before adding more traction. Indeed, at this point it will yield, be preserved, and come out from its attachment.
But if the mouth of the uterus is contracted as we have already described, then treat it with sneezing and apply a poultice made from herbs in an earthenware pot.
But if it still does not come out despite all that has been described, then be careful not to tire yourself out with repeated attempts at extraction.
However, whatever emerges from the mouth of the uterus should be tied to the woman's thigh. Then perform an enema on her using the ointment known as "tetrapharmakon". For this will cause it to rot after a few days, dissolve, and come out.
But when it begins to putrefy, an unpleasant smell will rise from it to the stomach and head, causing harm to the woman's health. Therefore, suitable fumigations should be administered for this purpose.
Some of the ancients have already experimented with fumigation using watercress and dried figs.
The shape of the instrument used for fumigating a woman during retention of secundines and menstrual flow:
Make a vessel similar to an alembic or jar out of glass or pottery; place the wide end over the fire and put the fumigation on top of burning charcoal. Hold it until the fumigation is consumed, then repeat with another one.
Chapter 79
On the treatment of an unperforated anal fistula.
Many boys are born with their anuses not having been pierced, which has caused difficulty for physicians. Therefore, the midwife should pierce it with her finger; if not possible, she should use a sharp spatula and avoid touching the buttock.
Then place over it wool soaked in wine and oil. Then treat it with ointments until it heals.
But if you fear that it may not pierce, then place a lead catheter in the hole for many days, and remove it when the child wants to defecate.
And likewise, an unperforated anus can also occur due to the solidification of a wound or an abscess. Therefore, that solidification should be cut away, and then it should be treated with what was mentioned for treatment using lead.
Chapter 80
On the treatment of fistulas that occur in the lower part.
Fistulas that occur in the lower part are those with a knot or swelling near the anus externally or in the space of one part; these can be single or multiple.
When that knot becomes inflamed, it opens and a watery white fluid or thin pus flows from it.
And sometimes these fistulas penetrate deeply. Therefore, penetrating fistulas are recognized by the fact that something comes out during defecation and wind, when the patient uses their bowels; sometimes worms may also come out of them.
And there are fistulas which penetrate into other spaces, reaching as far as the bladder or the urethra. And they can also penetrate as far as the hip joint and even to the coccygeal bone.
However, to recognize if a fistula reaches the anus, even if it is not penetrating, you should insert your index finger into the anus and pass a thin probe made of silver or iron through the fistula, assuming there are no twists or turns.
If the fistula is twisted, then insert a thin lead probe or a horsehair filament into it until you feel it touch your finger.
If you do not feel the probe at all or notice no discharge from the opening, nor windiness, nor worm-like movements as mentioned earlier, then know that it is a non-penetrating fistula. Proceed with its treatment accordingly.
When a fistula reaches the bladder or urethra, its indication is urinary discharge from it, and its prohibition is to prevent closure of the site with medication.
But if the fistula penetrates to the hip joint or hipbone, its sign is that the probe reaches there without meeting any twists. There will also be pain around the area, constant pus discharge from it, and medication will not work nor can it be closed with an ointment.
In all penetrating fistulas like these, there is no cure at all; attempting to treat them is a futile effort and foolishness on the part of the physician who undertakes it.
However, in the case of a fistula that is not penetrating or very old, healing can be expected with use of a probe. I have tried and tested this myself: Have the patient lie on their back with their legs raised above the hips and the thighs inclined towards their belly. Then insert a probe made of lead or silver into the fistula until you know where it reaches.
If the patient feels the probe around the anus, then you must insert your index finger into the anus.
If you feel the probe has passed through itself and you can detect it between your finger and the tissue or flesh, then know for certain that the fistula is penetrating. Do not attempt to treat it as there is no cure for such cases, as we have already stated.
They claim that in some cases flesh can be cured and that a beneficial medication exists which involves using a fine hot cauterizing iron according to the size of the fistula, as described earlier, and inserting it into the fistula until it reaches near the anus. Then repeat this procedure twice or thrice until you know that all the fatty tissues resembling a bird's feather quill have been burned.
Then wrap it with linen soaked in butter until the burned tissues come out. Then treat it with ointments that promote healing and consolidation of tissue.
If it heals, then it is good.
And if not, then it will never heal with any other treatment.
And if the probe emerges but does not reach your finger inserted into the anus, and there is a thick layer of flesh or skin between the probe and your finger, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula. Continue cutting with the probe until you reach the point where the probe has reached its end, at which point the probe will be freed and fall out.
Then cleanse those fatty tissues resembling a bird's feather quill so that none remain.
But if bleeding hinders you and prevents you from cutting those tissues, then it is better and quicker to cauterize with fire or an acidic medication.
For a cautery with fire brings together good results: it burns the added flesh and cuts off blood vessels, and dries out moisture.
Then treat it with linen soaked in butter or crushed cabbage leaves until the wound discharges pus and the burned tissues come out in the pus.
Then treat the wound with consolidating ointments that produce hard flesh, which are medicines containing astringency and drying properties.
For once they have been restored, then it is healed; and do not fear repetition.
But if the fistula has reached to the depth of the anus and is distant from the outer skin: then insert your finger into the anus and examine with it.
If you feel the wall of the anus and between it and your finger there is a barrier made of skin or flesh, and it is close to penetrating: then there should be no operation on that part except by way of faith and hope.
That is to say, one of three methods should be used on it: either cauterize it as mentioned earlier; or cut it open until you reach its depth, so that you can insert linen and apply treatment from nearby. And do not proceed too far with the cutting so that you do not injure the muscle containing the anus and cause involuntary incontinence. Then treat it as we have said.
Perhaps it will heal; or you may extend that membrane and cleanse it after cutting it close to the anus as mentioned earlier, either with your finger or with another sharp-pointed instrument. Then cleanse those tissues exposed in the incision until you can examine them closely.
Then endeavor to restore the entire wound with its incision and upper opening where it is consolidated, leaving the fistula open internally at the anus for easier healing of the patient.
And sometimes the fistula is situated thus: when you insert a probe into it and find that it is on the side of the anus near the surface of the body with skin and margin of the anus. Then take a probe with a pierced end like a needle's eye, which is prepared from the form of leatherworkers' needles. And insert into it a thread wrapped around five filaments, and wind it around it. Then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches its cavity.
If it penetrates near the inner margin of the anus, then pull out the thread from that opening so that you can insert your finger into the anus and bring out the end of the thread. Then hold both ends together and tie them tightly, leaving it for one or two days. The more the thread cuts into the flesh, the tighter you should bind it until the tissues between the two ends of the thread are cut and fall away. After this, treat the wound until it heals and is solidified.
But if the opening of the fistula does not penetrate, then make it penetrate by whatever means you have available, unless it is very deep. For it is not permitted for you to cause injury on purpose so as not to wound it.
Then proceed with it as we have said until it heals.
The shape of the spiked spatula used to probe the fistula is very sharp at one end and blunt at the other, so as not to wound unnecessarily.
Chapter 81.
Concerning the suturing of hemorrhoids from which blood flows after their incision, and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids occur in two ways. Either they are inside the anus like charcoal embers or plums; or they seem to be grapes themselves. And some of them are small and others large, and blood flows from them constantly, either there is one or many. And they are also found outside the anus at its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus have few moistures, from which yellow water runs or little blood flows steadily; and they are of the same color as the body.
And its treatment which is inside the anus is that you should order the patient to sit and press, until it protrudes out of the anus, and then appears before you. Therefore also use hooks to suspend them or hold them with your fingernail. Then cut them at their roots.
But if the hooks do not hold due to their moisture and softness, then grasp them with a coarse cloth or with a wick made from doubled wool, and remove them with your fingers. Then cut them, and sprinkle on them after cutting some hot powders, so that they serve the purpose of cautery. Or cauterize them as was mentioned earlier in the chapter on cauterizing. Then treat them with the rest of the treatment until they heal.
But if the anus does not obey to expulsion, then give the patient a clyster with a little bite [of medicine] so that it may cleanse what is in the anus and quickly obey expulsion when the patient presses.
But if the piles are external to the anus, then their condition is mild and easy; it is necessary to grasp them with your fingernail or hook and cut them. Then treat them with what we have said until they heal.
And whoever recoils from cutting with a knife, then it is necessary to administer stitching according to this method. Take a thread wound around and insert it into the needle. Then draw out the piles upwards and make the needle penetrate its root from the other side; wind both ends of the thread around the lower part of the needle, so that they are crosswise; then tie the piles tightly with a strong knot. Then tie the knot and remove the needle.
Do this with all of them, leaving one alone; do not sew it up yourself, so that excess blood may flow out from it.
Then place upon the wound a linen cloth dipped in rose oil or carrot juice, and place it on the wound; and instruct the patient to rest. Then leave him until he falls asleep.
When the stitches fall out, treat them with ointments and other remedies until they are healed.
Paragraph. On the treatment of fissures. For often these occur due to dryness of the feces and constriction of nature.
When they are chronic and medicine is ineffective, then you should dilate them with pointed spatulas or your fingernail until they become moist, and remove the overlying skin that prevents their healing. Then treat them until they heal properly.
If they do not heal, then repeat the dilation with greater force than before until they bleed profusely and are then treated until they are healed.
Paragraph. On the treatment of inverted and non-inverted hemorrhoids, as well as dry stitches and ants.
Chapter 82.
Know that a hemorrhoid is nothing but a round swelling resembling the head of a nail in color; it occurs throughout the body, especially in the lower parts of the feet and toes, and causes pain during walking.
Therefore, it must be cut around the nail with forceps or a hook, and removed from its root.
Then treat it; if you wish, cauterize it according to what is prescribed in its chapter.
From dry stitches, one has a small, rough, porous, round elevation on the surface of the body. And they often occur in the hands of children.
Its treatment is to stretch out the stitch and cut it, or tie it with silk thread or hair until it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize it according to what has been mentioned either with fire or with caustic medicine. And now the inverted stitches will be eradicated, both inverted and not inverted ones using the instrument that I shall mention.
An ant is also an elevated small filtered sieve, thick, on the surface of the body, going deep into it. And when cold encounters it, one feels a sensation similar to an ant bite on it, and this occurs throughout the entire body, especially in the hands.
And its treatment is to take a cannula made from goose feather or strong eagle feather and place it over the ant mound until the circle of the cannula encloses all parts of it. Then revolve your hand with the cannula until it cuts off what is around the ant mound and place it deep into the flesh and eradicate the ant from its root.
And if you wish to do this with a bronze or iron cannula, use the same procedure as mentioned before.
Let there be attached above the cannula a deaf-mute wrapping to ease its grasp on the finger and its rotation.
And if you wish, cauterize the ant according to what has been mentioned in the cautery chapter regarding the cauterization of stitches.
Chapter 83.
On the shapes of instruments used for administering enemas in diseases of the anus, and for relieving the belly and treating colic.
Sometimes an enema is made of silver, or of brass, or of cast or hammered bronze.
And sometimes they are made small and large from these instruments according to those used for administering them.
Therefore, the one used in treating young children should be small, and those whose anuses are weak or painful should have similarly sized enema tubes.
Large Enema.
The tube of the enema, that is, its upper end where the bag is tied, should be wide in this manner. It should have a distinct division so that the bag can be tied as you see here, and its lower end which is inserted into the anus should be deaf, that is, not open, smooth, very fine-drawn, and on one side of it there should be two holes, and on the other side one hole.
The width of the hole should be according to the thickness of the fingers, and somewhat thicker. The bag in which medicines are placed should be made from an animal's bladder or from sheep's gut, shaped like a small sausage, with its root being one span wide.
It should be perforated with many holes as shown, and the space between each hole should be the thickness of a finger. Then insert into these holes a strong thread made from raw flax, which is composed of about ten threads or so, and tie the bag with it like a sausage. When the bag is placed inside, the head of the enema should be tied tightly between the two divisions at its top. Then proceed with the enema using it.
This is the shape of the bag and holes depicted here.
The quality of performing the enema with this is: Fill the bag or bladder with warm medicine according to the tolerance of the sick person. Make sure it is not too hot or too cold.
Then the patient should lie on their back and raise their legs up high. Place a cushion or cloth under them to lie upon.
Then grease the tip of the enema with oil, egg white, or slippery elm mucilage, or something similar. Then insert it into the anus gently. Then the assistant should take hold of the bag with both hands and squeeze it forcefully until all the contents of the enema have entered the intestine.
Then remove the enema and the patient should raise their legs up against the wall.
But if medicine compels him to an exit, then let him hold on as much as he can. And if he sleeps with it all night long, it is the most severe in healing.
But if you administer an enema to him in cases of bowel obstruction and his evacuation is hastened thereby, then repeat the enema until he is healed.
But if there are hemorrhoids or sores wounding the anus, then let the enema be very fine and gentle, like those used to administer to children.
And this is the form of a fine enema upon which the skin should be tightly stretched.
Chapter 84
I have already spoken in the division regarding the treatment of wounds as befits this book, and I will now speak here about their treatment by hand and with medicines according to what remains.
Therefore, wounds are differentiated according to the thing that causes them and according to the place where the wound falls.
The things by which wounds are caused are many, such as the throwing of a stone, or the cutting with a sword, knife, spear, wood, arrow, and similar other things that are numerous.
And wounds which occur according to the parts of the body are those that fall upon the head, neck, chest, belly, or hip, and similar other members.
And I will now describe the treatments for certain wounds so that I may establish a rationale and rule for the remaining wounds.
And I will begin with simple head wounds, since the discussion of compound fractures will come at the beginning of Chapter III.
Therefore, I say. Therefore, when a simple wound occurs on the head without bone fracture, consider if it was caused by a thrown stone or similar object and has only pierced the skin. If the wound is large and you fear that hot inflammation may result, then act quickly and perform phlebotomy at once; do not delay it. Draw out blood according to his strength, unless there is something preventing phlebotomy, especially if little blood flows from the wound. And let the phlebotomy be performed on the opposite side of the wounded part.
And let the phlebotomy be performed on the opposite side of the wounded part. The patient should refrain from overeating, wine, and heavy foods.
But if something prevents phlebotomy, then reduce food intake and avoid meat and wines.
And apply over the wound, if it develops a hot inflammation, cotton soaked in rose oil alone or with wine containing styptic properties.
If you are secure from hot inflammation, then apply over the fresh wound with its own blood and not yet exposed to air, the powder I will describe later. But if it has already been exposed to air, then apply over it some ointments until it heals.
If the wound is large or caused by a sword cut or similar, and its lips do not adhere with small strips, then join them together with stitches according to what I will describe regarding abdominal wounds under suturing.
But if the wound is already exposed to bone and hangs open without retention except for a small support, then cut into it. Then treat the wound with medicines and restore firm flesh instead of skin. Instruct the patient to consume foods that are easy to digest, such as broths, cooked wheat, and similar items until they can be digested.
But if you find decay in the skin of the wound and it does not adhere to the bone, then cut away all the decayed part and cast it out. Then treat the wound.
If there is an artery or vein from which blood flows copiously and it does not stop with medicines, then ask about the artery. Therefore if you find it uncut, then cut it with a scalpel or ligate it. But if necessity requires and the aforementioned method does not work, then cauterize it until bleeding stops.
But if these wounds are small and simple, their treatment is easy. Therefore sprinkle this powder upon them before they change in air.
Method of making the Red Powder. Take one part of olibanum (frankincense), two parts of dragon's blood, and three parts of unslaked lime. Grind all together well and sift it. Then fill the wound with this powder and apply a good dressing until it adheres well and coagulates with the blood.
Then bind it above with bandages and leave it alone without removing it until it is completely healed so that it does not break open again. For the medicine will not be removed from it until it is healed.
And sometimes lime alone does the job, when frankincense and dragon's blood are not available. It also performs the same function in many large wounds when it is proper to apply it and bind them, especially for fresh wounds with their own blood that have not been exposed to air.
But if the wound has already been altered by air, especially during summer time, then this powder is not intended for it. Therefore treat it with what we have said and apply some digestive ointments upon it, or apply poultices made of barley flour mixed with water and honey until they discharge pus. Then continue to treat them with the remaining treatment until they are healed.
If a fracture occurs with the wound and it is small, then extract it with forceps; I will discuss the treatment of skull fractures later.
On wounds of the neck.
But if a wound occurs on the neck, then there is no difference in treatment between it and a simple head wound.
But if the neck wound has already cut into a nerve or artery, then if it is a nerve, there is no treatment except to apply something that causes rapid coagulation such as zinc oxide and quicklime and similar substances. For they harm nerves and contract them.
And do not apply anything cold at all to the wound because the substance of nerves is cold and their continuity extends to the brain which is the most exalted organ; therefore, let what is used for treatment be made from gentle medicines such as chalk washed with sweet water repeatedly infused with oil or rose oil, and tutia, when it is also washed with sweet water: and other mineral stones in this manner, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then perform suturing; otherwise, close its edges with bandages. Then treat it until it heals.
But if the wound has depth or a pocket of pus forms at its lower part, then make an incision in a more depressed area of it.
If an artery has been cut and blood flow is profuse, then separate or cut it and tie it off; otherwise cauterize it if necessary.
But if the thyroid cartilage or part of it has been injured and the cricothyroid membrane is preserved, then approximate the skin edges with sutures over the larynx without touching the larynx itself, and reduce it to its normal position. Then support it with a suitable bandage for several days. Then treat it with ointments that have astringent and drying properties until it heals.
If the wound has already made an impression on the bone, then examine it. If there are any bone fragments in it, remove them all, or as many as you can; but leave what cannot be removed until the wound rots and separates itself from its opening and relieves the discharge.
But if the wound is fresh, then sprinkle upon it the powder that I have described. If however bone appears in the wound and a fistula forms, then treat this with what is mentioned in its chapter.
Chapter on Wounds in the Groin
If there is a wound from a spear or knife and you see its depth, then consider this. If breathlessness comes out of the wound when the patient sighs, know that the wound is fatal.
But if it is not deep and is fresh with its own blood, then do not put powder on it at first, nor bind it tightly so that the blood does not remain in its depth and return to the heart and kill the patient. Rather, apply an attractive ointment to it and loosen it twice a day.
But if the ointment is not available to you, then put a piece of old cotton at the mouth of the wound so that it may suck out whatever liquid comes from it. And make the patient sleep on the wound so that what collects in it may flow away.
But if three days or more have passed since the wound was inflicted and no convulsions, palpitations of the heart, nor shortness of breath has occurred in the patient, and you see that his other conditions are good, then know that the wound is safe. Therefore treat it with bandages and other appropriate treatment until it heals.
But if its healing is delayed and it continues to open up, know that a fistula has already formed. Thus treat it according to its own chapter.
If the wound is from a cut made by a sword or knife and is simple on the surface of the chest or back, then treat it with suturing if it is large or with dust if it is small.
But if the wound has already made an impression on the bone and has cut off pieces of it, then examine the wound and make haste to extract those fragments if they are separate. But if they are not separate, then leave them be until the wound putsrefies, for extraction will be easier then.
The judgment concerning the treatment of other wounds that occur in the remaining limbs is the same as what we have said.
On abdominal wounds, prolapse of the intestines, and their replacement.
The disruption that occurs to the abdomen is great, medium, or small.
Therefore, from a large disruption sometimes one intestine or several may protrude.
Hence their reintroduction becomes difficult and the suturing more urgent and challenging.
And even a small disruption can be difficult to handle due to its unique nature. Therefore, their replacement becomes more difficult if you do not promptly reintroduce them to their place immediately after the disruption, because they swell up, open wider, and thicken; hence, reintroduction becomes difficult.
Therefore, a medium-sized disruption is easier to manage because it does not present the same difficulty in reducing the intestines as do large or small ones.
Know that there are four ways of suturing an abdominal wound.
Two types of sutures are common to both abdominal and other bodily wound sutures, while two others are specifically used for other wounds. But these latter two methods are more suitable for abdominal wound suturing, and I will describe each one to you with its explanation and demonstration.
I say therefore that when the wound is small and some intestine protrudes from it, making it difficult to reduce, then this occurs due to one of two methods. Either because of the narrowness of the rupture as we have said, or because there is inflammation in the intestine due to exposure to cold air.
When this is the case, then you must warm it by immersing a sponge or damp cloth into hot water alone. Or boil in water purslane, cypress shoots, and spices together with the intestine until the inflammation is resolved.
Sometimes wine with astringent properties may be used for this purpose; it is better than water alone in resolving inflammation.
When you see that the inflammation has been resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which have been boiled irises or mallows. For through this, its entrance will be eased along with the small object.
But if its return is still prevented after this treatment, then tie off the narrow part of the rupture with an instrument used for cutting hemorrhoids.
And the first treatment is better when prepared than cutting; it should not be forced to cut after necessity and lack of instruments.
Let one part of it be sharp and twisted, the other not sharp, and its tip not too fine like a stylus, but somewhat blunt; this resembles a keyhole as you see.
When the wound is enlarged and the intestine enters it, then its reduction should be made according to its natural shape into its proper place if possible; for this is better.
But when the rupture is large in the lower part of the abdomen, then have the patient lie on their back and raise their legs higher than their head. And if it is in the upper part of the abdomen, then place their head and chest higher than their lower body.
Similarly, if there is a disruption in one of two parts of the abdomen, then place your purpose and attention always on this, that the part in which the wound is situated be higher than the other part.
And thus it is necessary to do so for large wounds and medium-sized wounds.
But in small wounds, place it according to what you prepare yourself.
Then have present between your hands a pious assistant who shall hold the entire disruption with his hand and bring together its edges. Next, remove from it for him who follows to suture some after some; and this is the common method of one stitch.
And it is that you should take a needle or several needles according to the size of the wound's width. Then let go from the end of the disruption a quantity equal to the width of a finger, that is, of the physician's finger. And insert one needle except where you introduce into it thread at both ends of the skin, with two ends of sinew being under the skin internally, until you make it penetrate from the other side, and now you have brought together two edges of the skin and two ends of sinew: and there are four rows made.
Then draw together with double thread around the needle several times from both sides simultaneously until the two edges of the wound are brought together appropriately.
Then let go the thickness of a finger also and insert another needle into them; then draw it straight with thread as you did with the first needle. Do not cease doing this with him until you complete the closure of the entire wound.
And let the needles be of medium size between thickness and fineness; for fine needles pierce flesh very quickly, and thick ones are difficult to enter the skin. Therefore they ought to be of medium size in fineness and thickness.
And it is not necessary to insert the needle into two edges of skin close together too much; so that flesh may not be cut quickly and the wound open before healing, nor stretched out with the suture, so that healing may not be prevented.
Then cut the point of needles so that they do not harm the patient while asleep; and put on them little pieces of cloth from all sides which hold the ends of needles; and let them be until you know that the wound is already healed.
And this indeed kind of suture with needles is more suitable for small wounds. For it suffices in the suture of them that one needle or two, or about those be used.
But the second suture is common thus: gather with a suture the four ends - two of skin and two of the silk thread in place of one, with a needle on which is wound an equal thread in fineness and thickness.
Then when you have pierced these four ends with the needle, bring back the needle from the side where you began, so that the thread falls on the upper part of the wound: so that it may be a suture like the stitching of clothes, and put between every suture an amount of thickness of a finger's breadth.
But men know this suture.
And with this suture I myself sewed up a wound which had happened to a man in his belly, who was wounded with a knife and the disruption of the wound was greater than one palm; and there came out from the intestines about two palms of middle intestine, and there was disruption in the middle of the belly.
Therefore I brought it back after his intestine had stood outside the wound for 24 hours.
Thus the wound was healed in about fifteen days; and I treated it, and he was cured: and he lived many years afterwards, being active in his occupations.
And physicians judged that it would not be cured.
And it was by miracle that I did not treat it with ointment. Because there was neither ointment nor any medicine available where I was located. Therefore, I applied an old cloth moistened with honey water to the wound twice a day and kept washing it constantly until it was healed.
I will now discuss two kinds of stitching according to my account.
And the first step in using an acute suture is to insert the needle from the outer part towards the inner part.
After the needle has pierced the skin and traveled along the length and breadth of the entire body part, release one end of the thread on the side where you inserted the needle, and make it pierce through from the inner part to the outer part at the other end.
Then make it pierce back from the outer part to the inner part at that same end, and release the thread on this side. Then insert the needle into the other end from the inner part to the outer part, and continue piercing until it reaches the other end. Repeat this process until the entire wound is closed.
Then begin again from this same side, and bring out the needle along with its thread from the skin that it pierced.
Then bring the needle back into that skin and its thread on the other side. Pull it out from the skin on this side again, repeating this process once more. Continue alternating sides in this manner until all edges are approximated according to this example.
The final step of this stitch is to tie the thread like butchers do when sewing hides, so that one end of the thread remains on each side of the skin wound, alternating sides once.
The second type of stitching, which Galen called "running stitch.
This method is described as being similar to the stitch used by some physicians, where they bring together all parts of a wound that are naturally fitting and apply one end of the thread to another end, while the other end is applied to the opposite side. And this is done in the manner that I have described for you.
You must insert the needle into the end of mirrah that is near you on the outer part and make it pierce inward until its point emerges inside. Then leave two ends of thread (sifac). Then turn the needle around and make it pierce from the outer part to the inner part on both ends of the thread, that is on each side.
Then turn both sides again and make them pierce from the inner to the outer part on the other two ends of mirrah that are on the opposite side.
And this method of stitching is better than common simple stitching, and it is the stitch that comes after remembering.
This is therefore also the discourse of Galen.
And some said The abdominal wound should be sewn with two needles. Which means that you should insert one thread into each needle and begin by inserting the needle on your side and make it pierce through to the other side, according to the stitch used by those who repair hides evenly.
And know that when a rupture is in the middle of the abdomen, its stitching is more difficult than that of other parts of the belly.
But the judgement concerning the treatment of wounds is the same for all wounds.
Which means that when you find a fresh wound with its own blood before it is exposed to air, and you reduce the intestine, tie it off, and adapt it, then lay over it powder for cauterization.
But if the wound has already been exposed to air, then apply some ointment that you have present until it makes pus appear and the threads fall out and become firm. Then treat it as you would treat other wounds until it heals.
But if you do not have the medications present, then immediately apply old cotton to it as soon as it begins to discharge pus. Change it twice a day, as I have taught you, until it heals.
For in many cases of simple wounds, no other medication is needed.
But if you fear that the wound may communicate with the main members and cause injury, then you should take some soft wool and soak it in tempered oil or rose oil. Apply it around the areas between the groin and under the armpits.
But if he feels pain or punctures or putrefaction in his bowels (for this often happens), then apply a warm, black, demulcent wine enema. Especially so if the putrefaction has reached the intestines and is causing a deep exitus.
And know that what comes from the large intestine is easier to heal, but what comes from the small intestine is more difficult.
But the intestine called the ileum does not receive healing from any wound that occurs in it at all. This is because of its multitude of veins and their size, the subtlety of its body, and its proximity to the nature of the nerves.
But if what comes out from the wound is stringy and fresh, then reduce it according to your method of reducing the intestines evenly.
But if some time has passed and it has turned green or black, then you should tie it with a thread above the spot that has been discolored by it to prevent bleeding. For there are veins and arteries in stringy matter.
Then cut what is beyond that tie and leave two ends of thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside it. This will relieve you of the attraction and extraction of the stringy matter and the discharge of pus from the wound.
Reminder of a wound that occurred in the intestine.
When there is a disruption in the intestine and it is small, then it may be possible for it to be restored in some people because I saw someone who was wounded in his belly with a lance blow and the wound was on the right side of the stomach and had healed: and a fistula formed, from which windy discharge escaped.
Therefore I applied treatment until I was confident of its healing and did not cease to monitor it until it healed and the area solidified.
After I saw that the location had healed solidly, I feared that some other illness might happen to him but it did not. And he remained in better condition than before, eating and drinking, copulating, taking baths, and exercising as usual.
And now some surgeons have said that when a wound occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it should be tied in this manner. And you must take ants with large heads. Then gather together the two edges of the wound and place one ant with its mouth open over them When it grabs onto them and closes its mouth, cut off its head for it adheres and will not let go.
Then place another ant near the first one and continue doing this with ants one after the other according to the length of the entire wound.
Then close it up and stitch the wound. For those heads remain attached in the intestine until it eats and heals, without causing any harm to the patient.
And now it is possible to tie the intestine even with a fine thread that has been removed from an animal's intestine and attached to it after being inserted into the point.
And you should take one end of this intestinal thread and cut off a lot of it. Then tie a fine linen thread wrapped around the end of it. Then insert this thread into the needle and draw the intestine along with it. Then reduce it back to its place.
And this method of stitching with ants and intestines is only a matter of trust and hope.
Know that wounds which are large or wide, especially if they involve one of the thin intestines, then there is no cure except for delicate stitching.
On the treatment of fistulas.
Know that any wound or abscess which becomes old and chronic, turns into an ulcer, does not heal up, discharges pus continually, and is not cut off, is called universally in whatever member it may be a fistula; and we call it zuchemen.
And truly a fistula is a hard, white knot through which fluid flows; there is no pain with it, and its cavity resembles the hollow of a bird's feather; and some people call it 'risati', that is, 'feather'.
And at certain times it is moist and discharges pus. Perhaps the moisture splits open at some times. And sometimes this fluid is much, and sometimes little; sometimes thick, sometimes thin.
And I have already mentioned in my division that any ulcer which does not heal and no flesh grows on it is due to one of nine causes.
One of these causes is either because of the scarcity of blood in the body; or because of its evil nature; or because there is hard flesh between it and around its edges hindering the growth of new flesh; or because of much dirtiness and filth; or because the ulcer itself is putrid, and the matter sent to it is of bad quality; or because unsuitable medicine was used in treating it; or because a corrupting fever has fallen in that region, as happened in Caesaraugusta, where digestion of illnesses is difficult and healing of abscesses is delayed by nature; or because there is one bone too many or too few.
And I have already shown all of these causes and signs of them, as well as their treatments with medicines, divided into sections. Now it is necessary for me to make you aware of the treatment of fistulas and goitres that occur through surgical operation by hand.
Therefore, know that a fistula can occur in all members of the body. For there are fistulas that reach the major veins and arteries, nerves, or tendons; or the intestines, bladder, or some of the ribs; or some of the dorsal vertebrae, or some of the joints composed by articulation; such as those in the hand and foot. And there are also those which approach the main member itself, and similar ones.
Therefore, among these fistulas, those that arise in this manner are difficult to heal and do not receive treatment; dealing with them is hard work and ignorance.
But if there is a fistula whose depth does not reach these places I mentioned and you hope for its healing and trust in its treatment, then take a probe made of bronze or iron (if the fistula runs straight) and explore it with that.
But if there is twisting in the fistula, then examine it with a thin lead probe. For lead, being malleable, yields and curves around the twists.
But if the fistula has many openings, it is not possible for you to locate it by use of a probe. Therefore choose one of its openings and perform a clyster through it.
For the liquid you use for the enema will flow towards the other openings and escape from them.
Then proceed to examine carefully according to what is possible for you to know whether there is a bone or nerve at that spot, and whether the fistula is deep or shallow; or whether it is one fistula with many openings. Stand on top of all this with your utmost strength, questioning the patient, discovering the pain upon compression with your hand over the area, and similar signs and causes occurring after an apostema.
And so having stood on all that with true steadfastness, then proceed to treat according to your confidence. And consider whether the fistula is close and apparent, or in a safe location far from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or any of the places I mentioned to you. Then cut the fistula as previously described in my account, and remove what is inside it by filtration, and the rotting flesh that grows at its opening from flesh or gland, and similar things: and treat it until it heals.
But if the fistula is deep and runs straight, then it is necessary to cut it deeply as much as possible. Then cleanse it of all its rotting flesh.
Then apply a linen bandage dipped in sharp medicaments and insert it into the depth of the fistula you have cut with the knife. Do this several times until the medicine burns away all that remains in the depths of the fistula from decay.
Then restore it with ointments that promote healthy flesh growth until it heals.
But if it does not heal by this method, then cauterize it as we have described.
If the cause of a fistula is bone and you have confirmed this, then examine it and cut it according to what I have told you; if nothing prevents you from doing so regarding any vein, nerve, or main member, as we said.
If bone is discovered and there is some corruption and blackness in it, then scrape it until all the corruption is removed. Then dress it with what will cause it to adhere until it heals.
But if it does not heal and continues to emit pus as before, then know that you have not reached the complete removal of the corruption.
Therefore scrape over it again, carefully examine its reason and cleansing with all your strength.
Then restore it. If it heals, that is good; but if not, then know that the corruption is in a deep and distant part of the body which you have not reached. Therefore, there is no cure for it except to leave it to nature.
But if the bone is small and it is possible for you, grasp it with fine forceps that fit its size.
But if there are many bones, then carefully grasp them all with your strength and do not leave any out.
But if something obstructs you due to humidity and there is no fear or error in cutting them, then cut those humidities and flesh, if they are present, and free the bones.
But if you are not prepared to remove the bones at that moment, then place something over the wound that will cause it to putrefy; and leave it until what is around those bones in flesh and humidity has putrefied.
And keep the wound from healing. And let the incision you made during your operation close up. Indeed, if you fear this, apply over it, soaked in oil mixed with ground sulfur; or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment, or any other ointment of bdellium.
For when those moistures decay and the bones separate from them, the removal of these becomes easier, and their attraction is facilitated.
But if it is a large bone, such as the thighbone or hipbone, and what has decayed in it is a large part, then shave it with the final razor until that blackness and corruption is removed. Then restore the wound.
But if what is decayed in it is a large part and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to saw it off and cut it all the way up to where the corruption has reached. Then treat it until it solidifies.
And I tell you this story that happened to a man regarding his leg so that you may set it as an example and help for your own operation.
There was a young man around thirty years old who had pain in his leg due to a cause that originated within his body, namely from the internal parts, until it reached the leg and formed an abscess with a large swelling. And there was no external cause.
Therefore, much time passed with errors of physicians until the abscess was opened and many materials accumulated around him. And treatment for it was delayed until the leg became fistulous and many openings formed on it which all emitted pus and bodily moistures.
Therefore many physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no wise hand in the art until I was called and saw his leg and the materials that were flowing from those openings with great force. And all of the man's body had already wasted away, and his complexion was yellowish.
I therefore inserted a probe into one of those openings, and it reached the bone.
Then I examined all the openings and found that they interconnected with each other from all parts of the leg.
I then searched and followed one of those openings until I found a bone part: and I found it rotten, already eaten away, blackened, decayed, and perforated to the marrow.
Therefore, I removed what was exposed and prepared a replacement from the rotten bone.
And I ensured that nothing remained in the bone except the corruption which I cut away and removed. And I had already eradicated it.
Then I applied a dressing to the wound with strengthening medicines over an extended period. And it did not heal.
Then I probed further and discovered another opening above the first one, and found continuous decay with the bone; and I removed what appeared to me from that decay as well.
Then I attempted its restoration. And it was not restored.
So I examined it again and did not cease cutting the bone piece by piece, trying to restore it; but it was not restored until I cut about a palm's length of bone and extracted it with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medicines and it was quickly consolidated and healed. And this procedure and incision would not have been necessary if not for the weakness of the patient's constitution, his lack of tolerance, and my fear for him due to the continuous evacuation of malodorous fluid from him at all hours.
Thus he was healed completely and a hard, good bone was formed in place of the missing bone. His body's condition improved and his strength returned to him, allowing him to walk without any hindrance whatsoever.
But if the protruding bone in the body is already broken, then it is necessary to cut it according to this method: take a ligament and tie it at the end of the protruding bone; have someone else pull it upwards; and place another ligament made of coarse wool over the first one. Then tie it above the flesh that is on the bone; extend its end so that you pull the flesh downward; and uncover the flesh over the place where you wish to cut, so that the knife does not wound the flesh when cutting.
Place wood or a board under the lower bone appropriately; for when you do this, it prevents the knife from cutting rotted flesh.
And it is necessary that the cut be made over the rotted place briefly, so that there is no concavity of bone corruption; and may not appear in its appearance to the senses, and force another cutting in its place.
But if the bone is rotted and not protruding, indeed continuous with its parts; and corruption is in the middle of it or in one of its parts; then uncover all the flesh from all sides completely. Then place wood underneath.
Afterwards, cut it from the first side where there is corruption until the cut continues to the other side; and let the cut be according to the length of the corruption briefly, as we have said.
But if the corruption is at the joint, cut the corrupted joint itself; and cut the bone where it continues from the other side.
But if the corruption is in the continuity of two joints, then there is no remedy for it except scraping off.
But if corruption is in the joint of the hand or foot, then the case is very difficult indeed. However, it is necessary to examine how the corruption appears to you. And scrape it away and clean it according to whatever arrangement is possible for you, and correct it with whatever means you have at your disposal; provided that the vein or nerve does not interfere.
And know that there are many types of knives and saws for cutting these bones according to the situation of the bones, their preparation, thickness, thinness, size, smallness, hardness, and rarity. Therefore, it is necessary that you prepare a suitable instrument for each type of operation to be performed.
And know that these operations require knowledge about the kind of instruments you will need, as prolonged study is necessary. And knowledge of the methods of this art, and an acquaintance with the various kinds of diseases: then you will be able to find for yourself what is suitable for each case from among the instruments adapted to every ailment.
And I will tell you at the end of this chapter the number of instruments which you should have as examples to cut by and for testing others.
Shape of Another Saw.
Form of Razor for Joints. Let the head of this razor be shaped like the head of a star-nosed hammer, and let its points be like those of an awl.
And it is not fitting unless when the heads of joints are rubbed with it when they are spoiled or when the bone is broad and large.
