On the Incision of Extremities and Sawing of Bones.
Extremities putrefy due to either external or internal causes.
Therefore, when you have treated that corruption with medicinal care and the treatment does not help, and you see that corruption spreading to the limb without any check, then it is necessary to cut off that limb until you reach the corruption so that the patient may be saved from death or from the worse malady which is a greater loss of the limb.
And the sign of this is that the limb becomes black until it appears rotten; which fire has burned or rotted after becoming black. Unless that corruption is cut out up to that which follows it, and spreads throughout the whole body.
Therefore, hasten with the incision of it, and similarly if the cause of corruption is from the bite of certain venomous creatures such as the sea scorpion, viper, and similar ones.
If therefore corruption or bite is on the extremity of a finger, then cut off the finger and do not give space for corruption to spread up to the remaining hand.
And similarly if it happens on the hand, then cut it off from its root at the wrist and do not give space for corruption to spread until it reaches the arm.
And if it invades further, then cut the arm at the elbow joint.
But if corruption goes beyond and you see it around the elbow, then death of the patient is imminent there. And to administer anything else besides that [i.e., amputation] as treatment is a waste of effort.
Similarly, if corruption affects the toe, then cut it at one of its joints; but if it has reached the sole of the foot, then amputate the whole foot. But if it reaches the knee, then cut off the leg at the joint of the knee.
But if corruption has already reached above the knee, there is no remedy for it except to leave the patient to die.
And the method of cutting off a limb or tying it is thus: tie the ligament below where you wish to cut and also tie another above that place. Let one assistant hold one ligament downwards, while another assistant holds another ligament upwards. And you separate the flesh that is between the two ligaments with a wide spatula until all the flesh is exposed. Then cut or tie it off.
And it is necessary to put a linen cloth on all parts and not to touch the healthy place with the saw; otherwise injury may be added and hot inflammation may occur.
But if bleeding occurs inside during the operation, then cauterize the place quickly; or put some powder that stops bleeding on it. Then return to your treatment until it is completed.
Then bind the wounded limb with an appropriate ligament and treat it until it heals.
And I tell you that what happened to a man's foot is the same as what I am telling you.
That is, because blackness occurred on his foot with burning that resembled fire-burning, and that corruption first began at his toe until it started to affect the whole foot.
So the man took matters into his own hands after he saw that corruption was rapidly spreading on his limb with its intense pain and burning. He cut it at the joint, and he healed.
And after a long time had passed, that same corruption occurred on his left index finger.
Therefore he came to me, and I attempted to draw off that swelling with what I had applied on his hand from among medicines after cleansing his body. But the swelling did not subside, and it began to spread to another finger until corruption reached his hand.
So he called upon me to cut off his hand. But I refused to do so, hoping that the swelling might still subside through my care; and also feared for his life during the operation on his hand. For the man's strength was greater than his misfortune.
And when he had despaired of me, he returned to his own land. And they told me that he hastened and cut off his whole hand, and was cured.
And I have not recorded this narrative except to serve as an aid in cases of this kind of disease, and so that there may be a sign by which it may be recognized, and according to which treatment should be undertaken.
On the Treatment of Hemicranias and the Quality of Enemas with Medicines.
When an abscess occurs in certain fleshy members and the extent of the abscess continues until it forms a collection of pus, then if it bursts or is incised and the matter within it comes out as pus, and the remaining space appears hollow like a vessel, and the skin over it is thin like a worn-out cloth, and it does not reach its ultimate stage of corruption nor spread to bone, nerve, or ligament, it deserves to be called a sinus; but if its extent continues until corruption spreads to any of these members, then it is called a fistula or zuqn.
And its treatment is to incision that skin entirely as it appears curved, especially when it has become thin and resembles cloth; and you may rely on the fact that it will not adhere to the place due to its corruption.
But if you expect that the skin will adhere and it does not result in that final corruption; and if there is thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with a clyster; and it is necessary to consider its sinus.
Then if it is large and the pus emitted from it has a foul odor; then clysterize it with Egyptian medicine which is made of equal parts of vinegar, oil, and niter, mixed together in a vessel and cooked over a fire until the medicine becomes thick like honey.
Then take from what you need of it; and dissolve it with water and honey, and clysterize the sinus with it; and close its opening, and leave the medicine in it for about two hours.
Then remove it with expression: do this for so many days until the sinus is cleansed and the foul odor is removed.
And sometimes it is clysterized with water of ashes when this medicine is not present; and you should take the ash of grapewood or oak wood and sprinkle it on that water. Then filter it, and clysterize with it until you are confident that the sinus is now cleansed.
But if the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or water of ashes, then clysterize with water and honey to which some powdered niter has been mixed. Or clysterize with honey and wine mixed together, because of the property of honey to cleanse and wash away impurities; and wine makes the sinus adhere especially when it has excess astringency and dryness.
After doing this repeatedly and being confident that the corruption of the sinus is now removed; then clysterize it with what causes flesh to grow there.
Take from the plaster a piece of diaphoretic (dysaphion): dissolve it with rose oil and set wine, or clysterize it with some medicines and other ointments, whose properties I have written about in my treatise on ointments.
But if the orifice of the sinus is narrow and cannot accommodate a clyster cannula; then dilate it slightly with iron, or place a linen cloth wrapped in Egyptian ointment or basilicon ointment until it dilates.
And likewise if the orifice is too wide; then join its lips with a suture and let out from it an amount sufficient for the clyster to enter without overflowing.
And similarly if the orifice from which the pus flows is elevated towards the superior parts; then cut it in a lower place where it is situated, so that the pus may flow out downward, because when pus becomes stagnant in the depths of the sinus, it prevents flesh from being generated there.
But if it is not possible for you to cut around the lower part of the sinus as desired; then try to prepare the limb in such a way that the pus flows out easily according to your preparation. Ensure that no pus remains inside at all; and take medicines for consolidation which are applied on the wound, suitable to what you desire. And spread them out on a linen cloth. Let the cloth be of such size that it covers the entire sinus. Then cut with forceps directly at the orifice of the sinus a hole larger than the sinus itself a little bit. Next spread out on another cloth some soft ointments according to the size of the hole, and place it over it at a distance from the orifice of the sinus so that the outflow of pus may be eased; and do not remove the large cloth except once every few days.
That one which is over the sinus' orifice you shall remove at every hour so that what accumulates therein from pus may flow out; and to know whether the discharge flowing from it is much or little, digested or undigested.
Moreover, consider the site of the sinus itself, whether its owner feels pain there or not, and whether there is an abscess or not.
Therefore, when your dressing is applied thus and you see the matter that flows over the sinus' orifice, know that the sinus is already adhering or approaching adhesion. Then dissolve it in the morning or on the third day; and when you dissolve the wound, then change to another cloth and renew the ointment.
But if thin pus flows from the sinus after a long time, do not despair of its adhesion, for healing is approaching; because among men there are some who delay the growth of flesh in their wounds, and others who promote it conversely.
But if undigested pus flows from the sinus after many days, then know that the sinus does not adhere.
Therefore if the adhesion of the sinus is delayed and prolonged, then increase the astringency of the dressings; let them be truly medicinal so that they are humid in substance and dry in power, like the ointment diafinicon when excess of zibet is added to it.
And lastly, when treating, take the ointment diafinicon and dissolve it with rose oil, and mix it with old wine equal in quantity.
Then mix it together and use it.
And similarly, when you do not have the ointment diafinicon present, use honey instead; cook it until it thickens and then use it. Or take myrrh, aloe, and frankincense: grind them all together and powderize them over honey while it is on the fire. Then take a linen cloth and bind it over the wound; then spread on it the honey which you have cooked until it thickens, and powderize over it spices, and place it on the linen cloth, and bind it in place.
And sometimes aristolochia longa and blue lily roots are administered, which is the lily; and barley flour and centaurea. Use them either separately or combined, grind them up and sift them, then powderize over the honey which I have mentioned, and use it.
But if the wound persists and is not healed by this treatment, know that there is corruption at its base. Either it has formed an ulcer on the bones or hard parts, or ligaments; then treat it according to the method of treating fistulas as previously mentioned.
Chapter: On the treatment of panaritium and nails crushed by injury, and on incising a finger added [i.e., a false growth] and cutting it off.
Panaritium is an abscess that forms under the nail of the thumb of the hand or foot, and it may also form on other fingers.
When this condition persists and is neglected until it becomes inflamed and the abscess becomes hot; or it decomposes and discharges pus until it reaches the root of the nail, and sometimes even decomposes the whole of it, and perhaps the corruption extends to the bone so that there is a foul odor, and the tip of the finger becomes swollen and turns black in color.
When you treat it with what we have said about division, and your treatment does not succeed, then it is necessary to cut off all the excess that remains from the nail with an iron blade. Then cauterize the wound afterwards. For a cautery is very helpful indeed.
But if the bone is sound and the nail also is sound, and there are empty spaces between the nails which press down upon the flesh inwardly, and begin to prick it and wound it: then you must place a fine probe under the angle of the nail that pricks the flesh, and push it upwards; and cut into the flesh easily, and lay upon that which remains of the flesh from burning medicines, corrosive ones, until all of it is removed. Then treat it with ointments until it heals.
But if the corruption has already affected the bone, then you must cut into that bone and extract it, for it will never heal while the corrupted bone remains.
But if you see corruption and decay advancing into a finger, then cut it at one of its joints, as has been previously mentioned. Then treat it until it heals.
But if a nail should become worn down or struck, and severe pain occurs in it, then you must first perform bloodletting on the afflicted person. Then cut off the nail with a sharp, pointed knife by making a transverse cut from above to below.
And be careful that the cut does not reach the flesh beneath the nail, for then you would cause severe pain to the sick person, and it would result in additional growth of flesh in that place.
Then treat the spot with something that alleviates pain.
Now the extra digit that grows on some people's hands may be entirely fleshy; or it may contain bones; or it may have a nail; and sometimes it grows at the root of the joint, or its growth occurs at certain joints of the finger. Therefore, that which grows on certain joints of the finger does not move; but that which grows at the root of the finger may possibly move.
Therefore, the cutting off of one that is entirely fleshy is easy. Which means, cut it at its root with a wide spatula.
But the treatment of that which has its origin at the junction's root is difficult. Therefore, refrain from cutting it.
But if one is born on the finger at the last joint, then it should be cut round first until the bone. Then saw off the bone with an appropriate saw blade. Then treat it until it heals.
Fractures that occur between fingers happen frequently either from birth in humans, or from fractures of wounds, burns, fire, and similar causes.
Therefore, it is necessary to remove that union until the fingers return to their natural shape. Then place between them bands or cloths soaked in rose oil to prevent rapid union, and separate them until the site heals as required. Alternatively, place a thin lead plate between them until it solidifies.
And similarly, if union occurs between certain fingers and the palm, then cut that union according to how it is prepared and fits the shape of the member.
On the Incision for a Tumor.
Veins that are twisted, thick, and full of melancholic superfluities occur in many parts of the body, but mostly in the legs, especially those of travelers, laborers, and porters.
Therefore, you should administer strong purging of the body with black bile purgatives several times.
Then perform a bloodletting on the patient using the basilica vein.
But their treatment with iron is according to two methods. One of these methods is to cut them open and extract the black blood. And another method is to elevate the vein and remove it entirely.
But its cutting off is according to this method. First, soak the leg in warm water until the thick, foul blood dissolves. Then bind the sick leg from above the hip to below the knee with a bandage. Next, cut the vein in one or two or three places with a wide incision.
Then draw out the blood from below the leg upwards and have the bloodletter first bleed the sick person. Next, cut the nail downwards until a sufficient amount of blood flows out that the strength of the sick person can tolerate.
Afterwards, bind it and tell them to abstain from foods that cause black bile. Repeat the evacuation and bloodletting when the veins fill up again, if it is harmful to the sick person.
The method of elevation is as follows. Shave the sick leg, especially if there are many hairs on it. Then place the patient in a bath or soak their leg in warm water until it reddens and the veins become prominent; alternatively, have them exercise vigorously if there is no bath available, until the limb warms up.
Then make an incision along the length of the vein, either at its end near the knee or at its lower part near the heel.
Then use hooks to open the skin and strip away the vein from all sides until it becomes visible; it will appear red with a tendency towards blackness. When it comes out of the skin, you will see that it is white like a cord.
Then insert a probe underneath it until it raises up and comes out of the skin; then gently suspend it with a hook.
Next, make another incision about three fingers away from the first incision. Then strip the skin from above the vein until it is visible. Then elevate it with probes as you did before and suspend it with another hook, just as you did initially.
Then make another incision or more incisions if necessary. Next, elevate the heel and make the final incision at the calcaneus.
Then remove it through the incision above it.
Do this until you have removed it completely through the highest incision; then cut it.
If it does not yield to you being pulled out and lifted up, then insert a needle with strong thread doubled, tie it around it, and pull it out. Or insert a probe beneath it and rotate your hand in every direction until it comes out. And be careful not to cut it. For if you cut it, it will be difficult to lift out completely and harm will come to the patient from it.
When you have removed it completely, then apply over the wound a piece of wool soaked in wine and rose oil or oil, and care for it until it heals.
But if it is contracted and twisted with coils into parts, and not straight as we have said, then you must split open all its twisted parts and places of coiling.
Then suspend it with hooks until you lift it out completely; and be careful not to cut a vein or wound it near your incision, for it will be difficult to lift out or remove. Therefore keep yourself cautious.
The Shape of the Incision by Which the Vein is Healed.
The Shape of the Blind Hook.
It does not have the curvature of other hooks nor is it sharp-pointed so as not to wound the vein. It is thick and rounded, gentle; for if it were thin it would cut into the vein with its thinness. Indeed, let it be thicker than necessary, as we have said.
On the Extraction of a Vein from the Leg
This vein is produced in legs in hot lands such as those of the Aliz Mughrebiyya and the land of the Arabs, and in dry, hot lands where grass nor trees grow.
And perhaps it may be found in other parts of the body besides the legs.
Its generation indeed occurs from the corruption that happens under the skin, such as those found within the bodies of snakes and worms, namely, the ascaris worm and other worms generated between skin and flesh.
And the sign of the onset of this vein's occurrence is that a fierce inflammation happens in the leg. Then it becomes hard (tumor). Next, the vein begins to exit from that hardened spot, like the root of a plant or an animal.
Therefore, when its end appears, then you should wrap around it a piece of lead weighing from one to two drachms and tie it tightly; let the lead hang down from the leg.
Whenever something comes out from it towards the outside, wrap that in lead and tie it. But if it extends far out, then cut off a portion of it and wrap up the remainder. Do not cut it completely until it has all come out. For if you cut it off entirely before it has finished coming out, it will contract and enter into the flesh, causing an abscess and corruption in that place, as well as a bad ulcer.
Therefore it is necessary that it be handled gently and drawn out slowly until it comes out entirely from its last point, so that nothing remains of it in the body. Indeed, in some people this vein can extend to five palms or ten fingers in length. And it has come to my knowledge that a man had one which was twenty palms long.
But if it is cut during your treatment session, then insert a probe into its opening and bore through it with a long needle, along its body, until all the material inside it has been emptied out. Then apply medicines to the site for several days to promote healing. Then treat it with the cure for boils.
And indeed this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the junction of the foot or on the foot itself; and it happens that it has many openings, with a branch issuing from every opening.
Therefore treat it as we have described in division (of the vein), along with what was said previously.
On the incision of swellings caused by worms under the skin, and called "bovine sickness.
This condition is called "bovine sickness" in certain regions among us because it often occurs in cattle and is caused by a small worm that is born between the skin and flesh.
And it travels throughout the body, ascending and descending, and its movement can be felt during walking from one limb to another until it bursts forth wherever it finds an opening in the skin.
And its origin is from the corruption of certain humors. Just as worms and serpents, that is, tapeworms and roundworms, occur in the belly.
And it is feared for its harmfulness. For when it moves through the body and rises up to the head, if it happens to burst forth there, it may emerge and destroy an eye. This occurs quite often.
Therefore, when you wish to treat and remove it, this can only be done during its movement and when it is felt through touch.
Hence, you should tie tightly above and below it with a strong binding cord. Then cut over it and extract it.
But if it is buried deep in the flesh and cannot be found, then apply a hot cautery to the spot until it dies.
And what is feared most is its corruption which infects the eye, as we have said.
But if you see that it has already reached the head near the eye, then tie a good ligature beneath it on the eyebrow. Then cut over it and extract it.
And it is necessary for the patient to cleanse his body with purgative medicines that dissolve bad corrupting humors.
And let him abstain from foods that cause putrefaction.
Chapter on the Incision for Nakir's Disorder
This disorder is called Nakir in our land, and it is a pain that occurs in certain limbs, then it moves from limb to limb.
And indeed I have seen this myself, as I am telling you now. I was summoned to a woman who was ill in some villages And she exposed her arm, and I saw a small swelling on the rope-like vein of the arm.
And after staying for one hour, I saw one swelling move along with a sort of pulsation, like the movement of a worm, ascending quickly to her shoulder, so that it appeared like living silver moving from place to place.
Therefore the pain withdrew from that spot and became fixed in the shoulder.
Then I sat for one hour, and it ran throughout her body until it reached another arm.
Then she told me that it was revolving throughout her whole body, as I had seen.
Therefore I was amazed at the speed of its movement from limb to limb. And truly, before this, I had never seen this disorder with my own eyes in such a manner. But nevertheless, I have seen many people who experience pain moving from limb to limb, and I did not see it with my own eyes as clearly as I saw it in this woman.
And I do not think that it is anything but what results from the fact that this woman was among those who are from dry-bodied villages with exposed veins. Therefore, it appears to be a kind of windy inflammation.
And it is necessary that these do not appear in those who are delicate and have moist bodies with hidden veins.
Therefore, when you wish to treat it and the patient feels pain: then if it appears before your eye, as we have said, then tie above and below it quickly; and cut above it until that windy inflammation comes out constrained; and cauterize the place.
But if you do not see it with your eyes: then treat it with rubbing of the body and with that which expels windiness, and scar it with foul-smelling pills and pills of senna and similar things.
On the Extraction of Arrows.
Arrows vary according to their types and the places on the body where they strike.
The varieties of arrows are those with large points and small ones, concave points and blunt points. And among them are those with three angles. And among them are those with tongues. And among them are those with stars.
But those that fall according to the members where they strike, there are two ways. Either these members are among the main concave members such as the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and similar ones. When an arrow falls into any of these members and the signs of death appear to you, which I will describe to you later, then you should avoid extracting that arrow from it, for death follows him who has it in many ways. And when these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow is not hidden deeply within the member, then extract it and treat the wound.
For the signs of the brain when an arrow falls into it and pierces the bone and exits through the fontanelle above are: sudden severe headache, blindness, vertigo, redness in the eyes, swelling, redness of the tongue, spasms, confusion of intellect, and vomiting bile. And perhaps blood flows from the nostrils or ears. And perhaps speech is cut off, and voice is destroyed. And there emerges from the wound site white fluid resembling milk. And it exits from it like watery flesh.
If these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment and remove the arrow if it does not exit.
The signs of an arrow having fallen into the heart are these: that it is near the left nipple; and one feels as if it were fixed in something hard, not something empty. And perhaps the arrow moves like the pulse; and black blood runs from the wound. And there follows coldness of the extremities, cold sweat, and syncope (fainting). Therefore know that death is certain.
The sign of an arrow having pierced the lung is the emission of frothy blood from the wound, and the swelling and change in colour of the vessels of the neck. And there is a great, high expiration; and he seeks to draw in cold air.
If the arrow falls upon the pericardium which is in the chest, then it is near the small ribs; and there is a great panting with severe pain accompanied by sighing, and all the members of the shoulders move.
And if the arrow falls into the stomach, then perhaps something undigested from food emerges from the wound.
And if the arrow falls into the belly and is fixed there: some feces, or a ringworm, or a disrupted intestine emerges from the wound. Therefore there is no hope for cure nor extraction of the arrow.
But if an arrow falls into the bladder and urine emerges, and something proceeds externally from it, and there is a severe pain upon the sick person: then know that it is mortal.
The other limbs, such as the face, neck, throat, shoulder, forearm, back, hip, thigh and similar parts of the body are treated according to various methods when the arrow does not strike an artery or nerve, nor is it poisoned.
And I narrate to you what I have seen regarding these arrows, so that you may indicate the same in your treatment.
That is, an arrow fell into the lacrimal duct of a man's eye, at the root of his nose. Therefore I pulled it out from him on the other side, under the fat of his ear: and he was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I pulled out another arrow from a Jew, which had fallen into the fat of his eye beneath the lower eyelid: and the arrow was already hidden, and I could not get hold of it except for a small part of its end with which it clung to its wood; and it was a large arrow among those of the Turkish bows, with a blunt iron tip, in which there were no ears.
Therefore the Jew was healed, and no evil befell his eye.
And I pulled out another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And it was an Arabian arrow, which has ears on it. So I seized upon it between the two glands (thyroidea): and by then it had already submerged itself in his throat. I gently pulled with it until I drew it out: and the Christian was healed.
And I pulled out another arrow from a man which had fallen into his belly: and we thought that he would die from it.
After thirty days had passed and there was no change in his condition, I seized upon the arrow, heated it, and drew it out: and he was healed, and no evil befell him.
I saw a man into whose back an arrow had fallen, and the wound around it had become inflamed. After seven years, the arrow emerged from the root of his thigh.
I saw a woman into whose abdomen an arrow had fallen, and the wound around it became inflamed; the arrow remained within her, but there was no change in her condition, and she suffered no harm from any of her natural functions.
I saw another man into whose face an arrow had fallen; the wound became inflamed and remained there, causing him little pain. Similar things happened to many others.
I extracted an arrow from a man among the king's princes, which had struck him in the middle of his nose and inclined slightly to the right side; the entire arrow was concealed.
Therefore I was called upon for his care three days after the accident. I found that the wound made by the arrow was very deep.
I probed it with a thin probe and did not feel the arrow; however, he himself felt a pinching pain beneath his ear on the right side. Therefore I hoped that that pricking sensation was caused by the tip of the arrow.
I applied a plaster to the spot where there was power to draw and digest, believing that it would draw the wound out and reveal a mark of the arrow for me to cut upon; but nothing happened to indicate that the arrow had followed the place.
I persisted with the plaster for many days, but nothing happened to indicate that the arrow had come out; meanwhile the wound became inflamed, and the patient remained sick about its removal for some time until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose. And he told me this.
And he told me this. So I applied a sharp, caustic medicine to the wound for many days until it opened up; then when I probed it, I felt the tip of the arrowhead which was stuck in the wood.
Afterwards I continued applying the sharp medicine to open the wound until we could see the tip of the arrow clearly; and about four months passed during this time with him.
Then after the wound had widened sufficiently that I could insert forceps into it and move it about to try to remove it, but without success; then I did not give up on refining my methods until one day with appropriately made forceps—I shall explain their form at the end of this chapter—I managed to extract it. Then I healed the wound.
And indeed physicians judge that cartilage of the nose does not regenerate. Therefore I healed it and the wound became solid; the patient was cured with full health, and nothing further harmed him at all.
And I tell you this story of the extraction of some arrows so that you may take it as an example and instruction for what I have not mentioned.
For the parts of this art and their distinctions are not fully contained within my narrative; nor does this book contain them all. The wise craftsman compares a little with much, and what is present with what is absent, thereby discovering new operations and instruments among those foreign to this art which happen occasionally.
I say therefore that arrows do not come out of the limbs where they are embedded except by two methods. Either by being drawn out from the place where it entered. Or by being pushed out through the opposite side of the limb.
And that which exits whence it entered is either a splinter projecting from a wound or it gets pulled out and exits.
But if it does not exit on its own after some time has passed since the injury occurred, then you must leave it alone for several days until the surrounding flesh rots and the tension around it is relieved so that it can be pulled out more easily.
And likewise if it is stuck in bone and does not yield to being pulled out, then leave it alone for several days as well, and repeat the attempt every day with gentle traction and movement until it comes out.
But if it still does not yield after several days, then you must bore around the tip of the splinter with a fine trepan on all sides until its passage is widened. Then pull it and remove it.
But if the splinter is hidden within the body and concealed from view and touch, then search for it using probes. If you find it, then pull it out with suitable instruments designed for that purpose.
But if you cannot reach it due to the narrowness of the wound and its depth, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein in the way, then probe for it until the wound is widened enough so that the splinter can be removed.
But if the ears are the ones holding it fast, then free the flesh attached to them from every side with all your skill and care, until the fracture of the ears and their twisting give way.
And when you perform the extraction of a splinter, wherever it may be, then use your hand's rotation with tweezers on all sides until you make it come out; and do so gently and carefully, lest the splinter break and become difficult for you to attract and extract.
But if you cannot remove it at once, then leave it alone for a few days until the flesh around it begins to decay. Then try again; for the tissue will have softened. But if there is profuse bleeding, then apply what we said under its appropriate chapter concerning treatment, and guard against cutting any vein, nerve, or tendon.
And use all possible means to remove the splinter gently and steadily, slowly and firmly, as I have instructed you.
And when you pull out the splinter, it should come out bent according to its original shape at the time of the injury. For that is more beneficial. But if you are unable to do this, then use whatever figures (methods) are possible for you.
But if the splinter comes out on the opposite side, or has already begun to emerge from it, or if you can feel a swelling at its tip beneath the skin near its exit point and see that it is raised, then cut over it, making the cut deep enough for tweezers to grasp it.
Then pull it out; for it will come out easily by itself.
But if it is retained within the bone, then twist your hand around its circumference until the arrowhead impr​ints itself upon the bone and expands; then pull it out.
And if not, let it be for a few days, then repeat it until it comes out.
But if the shaft of the arrow is inside it, then push it along with it.
But if the shaft has fallen off and you wish to administer a push, then introduce a hollow instrument into it so that its cavity enters the butt of the arrow; then push it along with it.
But if the arrow is hollow, then push it along with an instrument that enters into it. For through this (instrument), the arrow will be alleviated.
If the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary to remove all the flesh around it where there is venom, if this is possible. Then treat it along with what is suitable for that (wound).
If the arrow has fallen into the chest, belly, bladder, or flank, and is near what you are touching with your fingers, and it is possible for you to cut over it, then cut and take care not to injure a vein or nerve, and remove it.
Then examine the wound if there is one, and treat it until it heals.
And this is the form of forceps with which arrows are removed.
Let its ends be similar to a bird's beak, made like iron: when they grasp an arrow, they do not let it go.
And there are many types of them, large, small, and medium-sized, according to the quantity of the size of the arrow, its thinness, the width of the wound, and its tightness.
Form of the hollow pusher.
And form of the blunt pusher.
This is the form of a blunt end like a radius bone, so that its entrance into a hollow arrow may be eased, and it may be pushed along with it.
On Bloodletting from Veins.
There are thirty veins whose locations on the body have been determined for bloodletting.
Of these, there are sixteen in the head: two pulsating veins behind the ears, called the "two herbs"; and two arteries visible at the temples; and two veins in the two lachrymal glands of the two eyes, called the "watchful ones"; and a prepared vein in the middle of the forehead; and a vein at the tip of the nose. And two Gudgeons, which are in the neck. And two veins that are in the upper lip of the mouth And two veins that are in the lower lip. And these are the veins called Alghecherich And two veins which are beneath the tongue.
However, there are five veins in each arm that are used for bloodletting.
One of these is the Cephalic vein, and it is on the outer side; common people call it the vein of the head And the Dark one. And there is the Median vein, composed of a branch of the Basilica and a branch of the Cephalic; common people call it the vein of the body. And the Basilica, which lies on the inner side, is also called the Titillicaris Common people call it the vein of the belly. And the Cord of the arm, which is situated above the elbow; this is the vein that is used for bloodletting and it appears prominently above the thumb.
And Salvatella: it is a vein that lies between the Annular and Minimum veins, and it has two branches.
And there are three veins in the leg and foot. One of these is that which curves under the big toe on the inner side. And the second is the saphenous vein, and its location is at the heel from the domestic side. And the Nessus vein, and its location is at the heel on the outer side.
And there are three similar veins in the other leg.
On bloodletting of two veins behind the ears.
The bloodletting of two veins that are behind the ears is beneficial for treating chronic catarrh, headache, epilepsy, and old wounds on the head.
And the method of bloodletting it is as I shall describe. It is necessary to shave the head of the sick person and then rub their occupit where the two veins are located with a rough cloth for a good friction. Then let the patient tie his neck with a bandage until the two veins appear. And their locations are behind the ears in two depressed places on the head. Therefore seek them with your finger, and where you feel their pulsation under your finger, there mark it with a cautery.
Then take the scalpel for bloodletting, which is called "Incidens". Then insert it under the vein into the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone. Then lift your hand with the vein and skin upwards, and cut the vein with the skin using an incident cutting motion. And let the length of the incision be about two fingers' breadth or somewhat more, and make as much blood flow out as you wish.
Then bind it with lint and leave it until it heals.
And thus concludes the beginning of the book: the cutting and cauterizing of those veins.
The bloodletting of two arteries that are in two temple regions is beneficial for treating chronic headache, evil humors, and persistent eye inflammation, as well as the acute discharge of excess fluids to the eyes.
And the method of bloodletting these [arteries] is as I shall tell you. Let the patient tightly bind their neck until two veins become visibly apparent to sensation and their pulse is discernible under your finger. Then mark them with a cautery.
Next, elevate the skin above the vein using your index finger, insert the lancet beneath it, and elevate the vein upwards. And cut into it as you would with two other veins, allowing blood to flow according to your required quantity.
Then release the tourniquet from the patient and place your finger over the vein for one hour. Afterwards, apply cotton and lint to it, bind it tightly above with a bandage, and leave it until healed.
And thus ends the discussion of these veins, their incision, and their extraction at the beginning of this book.
The benefit of bloodletting the frontal vein after cephalic bloodletting is for treating ancient facial ailments, headache, redness, and foul ulcers.
And the method of bloodletting this [vein] is as I shall tell you. Let the patient bind their neck with a bandage until the vein appears. Then take an instrument called the "fossor.
Place the raised point at the top of the fossor on the vein and strike it with a pestle or another object in its manner. Then allow blood to flow according to your desired quantity. Then release the tourniquet from the patient and bind it until healed.
And sometimes it is opened with a wide-bladed lancet. But it is not necessary that the lancet should be sharp-pointed, as other lancets are, but rather broad-pointed. And make the incision lengthwise; because it is near to the bone: and perhaps the lancet will break if it is thin.
But the indication for bloodletting from both veins which are in the two lachrymals of two eyes, is for inflammation and redness of the eye, and discharge from the eye, and diseases of the face.
And the method of bloodletting these veins is that the patient should bind a cloth around their neck. Then make the incisions and you should stand over the head of the patient.
And make the incision lengthwise a little way with a small lancet slightly broad-pointed; for there is no flesh in that place. For if the lancet has a thin point, it may break.
Then let blood flow until you think necessary. And apply cotton to them and bind them up for one night, then loosen them.
But bloodletting from the veins of the nose is beneficial for acute fever and severe headache, and diseases of the face such as migraine and especially when there is redness which has been present for a long time.
And the method of bloodletting these veins is that the patient should bind their neck with a cloth. Then grasp the patient's nose with your left hand and take a sharp-pointed lancet that is long and insert it into the middle of the tip of the nose, between the two parts which divide the nose lengthwise. For there the vein does not appear to sight, and because blood begins to flow immediately.
And you must hold your hand with the lancet for a little while, until you have let out as much blood as you require. Then bind it that same night. For it recovers quickly.
Indeed, the phlebotomy of the veins of the foot is beneficial for constriction of breath, the onset of leprosy, and melancholic illnesses, and scabies, bad ulcers, and corrosive diseases, that is to say, herpes and hemorrhoids.
And the quality of this phlebotomy is such that a weakened patient should be bound tightly under both arms with a bandage, and an artisan should stand on the head of the sick person, who should sit upon a seat.
Then cut the vein lengthwise for a large phlebotomy briefly. Then draw out from the blood a measured quantity, or as much as you see is necessary according to the case at hand. Then do likewise with another vein.
Then untie the bandage and tie the two veins tightly in the middle to prevent the patient from fainting. And leave it like that until morning. For the wound will be healed.
On the phlebotomy of the veins of the arm. Indeed, the veins of the arm are beneficial for phlebotomy because they help with sore throat, gum disease, bad ulcers, cracks in the lips, and bad ulcers that occur around the nose.
And the quality of this phlebotomy is such that you should make the patient sit before you and tie a cloth around their neck. Then pull back the patient's lip and look for two veins, one on the right side of the lip and one on the left. The location of each is indicated by their darkness. And it is true that around them are small, dark veins. Therefore cut both of them with an incising cut.
But if there is doubt and you do not know which one to cut, then aim for the larger ones and make your incision on them.
And likewise do with the two veins that are in the upper lip, for it is most common to cut them together. And also cut the two veins that are in the lower lip.
About bloodletting of the vein under the tongue after cephalic bloodletting. The bloodletting of the two veins under the tongue is beneficial for treating ailments such as those affecting the throat, swelling of the uvula, and disorders of the mouth.
And the quality of bleeding these veins is as follows: let the patient sit between your hands facing the sun, stick out their tongue, and look underneath it to find one vein on one side and another vein on the other side. The color of both veins should incline towards blackness.
Therefore, bleed both veins, but be careful not to draw blood too far down your hand when bleeding them. For beneath them are arteries, and perhaps there may occur a flow of blood from those arteries.
The three veins that are bled in the elbow are ones whose bleeding is customary for all people.
And bloodletting of these veins is done according to two methods. Either by pricking with a wide myrtle lancet or cutting with an olive oil-washed blade that has been sharpened to a fine point. Or else by cutting with a scalpel-like bloodletter called Alnesil. And this is its shape.
And among them are wide and fine ones according to the size of veins and their narrowness. The shape of the myrtle lancet is wide, as you can see. And it is suitable for opening hollow veins, full veins, projecting veins, visible veins, thick veins, and those that contain thick and muddy blood.
This bloodletter is of lesser width and more refined in its points.
It is suitable for bloodletting from the small veins that contain thin yellow blood
For bloodletting, one of these three veins at the basilica is helpful. Because it draws out blood from illnesses that are under the neck and throat, below the chest, and the belly. It is convenient for a bloodletter to use with a bloodletter so as to be cautious, especially because an artery lies underneath it. If he errs and cuts too deeply in his puncturing of the bloodletter, he may hit that artery, causing a flow of blood. Therefore, it is necessary not to use a bloodletter with another bloodletter when puncturing; rather, one should use the Alnesil for cutting.
But if the basilica does not appear clearly, then one should seek out another vein and exchange it for that one, or find a branch of it. Otherwise, use a bloodletter on the arm in its place. This vein is visible, and you should cut it with the bloodletter called Anesil, as we have said.
If you wish to perform a bloodletting on this same vein, then before tying the arm, you should touch the spot until you find the pulse point. Next, mark the spot with a cautery. Then tie the arm and cut the vein using the concave cutting edge of the bloodletter called Anesil, as we have said. And be careful not to strike too far from the artery's location.
When you tie the ligature and see swelling at the spot you marked with the cautery, know that this swelling is the swelling of the artery; therefore avoid it.
If you see blood rising at the bloodletting like urine from a child, and it is thin, red blood, then know that it is coming from arterial blood. Therefore, quickly place your finger on top of the space at a distance, then remove your finger. If blood is cut off (for it is often cut off), then bind the arm and let it rest: and caution the patient against moving it and keep him in fear.
And do not move it for several days until it heals.
But if blood is not cut off and binds you tightly, and there is no medicine available at hand to you at that moment, then cut the artery if it appears visible to you. For its ends contract and the blood is cut off.
Or take half of a fig bark and wrap it: take one part of it and tie it over the place where the vein was tied with bandage and lint until the next day.
Then if blood is cut off, that is good. And if not, then treat it with the same method used for stopping blood flow from other wounds: and the cutting of its blood will be easy in many cases due to the small wound area and the possibility of tying off from the arm.
Indeed, the phlebotomy of the dark vein is beneficial in drawing blood from the upper part of the head and lower part of the body because it is composed of a branch of the basilica vein and a branch of the cephalic vein, as we have said.
And it is fitting for the phlebotomist to fear its phlebotomy because a nerve lies underneath it. Therefore, if during the bloodletting puncture he finds a nerve, it causes numbness and makes healing difficult, and perhaps it will not heal completely.
And this nerve often appears visibly. But if it is hidden in some people and is thin and not visible, then you should perform the bloodletting where it is usually done, making an incision with a scalpel, and avoid the nerve with all your strength.
If the vein lies between two nerves, then trace its length.
On the cephalic vein. The cephalic vein is beneficial for bloodletting because it draws blood from the head and helps alleviate eye disorders.
And it is fitting in this vein, if you wish, to prick it with an olive-oil phlebotome or a myrtle-phlebotome, for it is safer than all other veins because there is no artery nor nerve beneath it.
However, when performing bloodletting, you should avoid the head of the arm and seek a gentler spot. If no suitable location is found with the first strike, do not hesitate to repeat the procedure several times without causing harm.
Nevertheless, sometimes an abscess may form in some people when bloodletting is not performed at the first strike, but it does no harm in that place.
On the Benefits of Bloodletting and Precautions to Observe The quality of bloodletting and its consequences depend on its proper performance. You must consider this when correcting it.
Firstly, you should know that bloodletting is not performed except for the sake of preserving health and preventing diseases through diligence and caution.
And bloodletting should be performed on one of the three veins found in the elbow, namely the cephalic, the dark-colored, and the basilica. And bloodletting should be performed at the beginning of spring after fasting, during summer and autumn, and after meals in winter when signs of fullness appear.
Bloodletting should be done on the first or third day after noon has passed by three hours.
But bloodletting performed for illnesses does not have a fixed time; it should be done whenever the situation and necessity require it, day or night, at any hour, and at all times.
And children should not be bled until they reach fourteen years of age. Pregnant women should not be bled except from the fourth to the seventh month. Old people who have already passed sixty years of age should not be bled.
Therefore, when someone wishes to undergo bloodletting for any reason, it is necessary that they cleanse their bowels beforehand with a gentle enema if there is much retained feces, so that the veins do not draw foul, harmful impurities from the intestines during the bloodletting, which could harm the main organs.
The patient should not be bled when they are full or nauseated, nor when they are drunk or recovering from drunkenness until that condition has passed. Nor an invalid with dropsy (edema).
And beware of bloodletting again after a bout of colic pain, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive sexual activity, hard work or exercise, wakefulness, fasting, and any other thing that weakens the body's strength.
Then consider the fineness of the humors before doing it; if the blood is thick due to foods and drinks, and medications, proceed with caution if at all possible.
Then take a bath if there's no contraindication, or engage in some exercise to make the blood finer.
And perform the bloodletting on their chest around the third hour of the day, as we have said; and try to remove all harmful animal accidents from their chest that day, such as anxiety, anger, fear, and similar bodily excesses like hard work, punishment, and excessive sexual activity.
And let them have present the things with which people are accustomed to use for their comfort due to their fragrance, pleasant scent, and joyfulness, to the best of their ability.
Next, the phlebotomist should sit on a stool that is higher than the phlebotomy couch. Then, he should extend his arm and rub it with his hand twice or three times. Then tie a ligature with a bandage and wrap it around twice.
And let the ligature be moderate, for an unequal constriction hinders the flow of blood either by being too tight or too loose.
Then after the ligature has been tied, the person undergoing phlebotomy should rub their hands together until the veins swell and become visible.
Then the phlebotomist should cleanse the vein with a little old oil.
Then place his left index finger on the vein itself where he intends to perform the phlebotomy, lightly so as not to cause the vein to retreat and avoid bruising. For there are veins that slip away like a string under the pressure of phlebotomy, and others that are full of wind. When you place the phlebotomy needle on them, they contract underneath it, deceiving the phlebotomist so that he cannot open the vein or if he does, it is too narrow. Therefore, the phlebotomist must be firm and attentive in all these matters.
Then set down the phlebotomy needle. If the vein opens on the first attempt, that is good. And if not, then repeat it once or twice more at the same spot or above it with urgency, without damaging the site.
But if the site is damaged or the patient becomes frightened, then let him go for one or two days without tying the ligature. For otherwise a hot abscess may be drawn in. Do not enter a bath.
Then he repeated bloodletting, if he loved [it].
If the opening is small or if blood flows subtly, and you fear that not enough blood will flow out, then repeat bloodletting through the very same incision, following its straightness, and add a little to your procedure.
And do this quickly, before the location is closed up; for many people find that the wound closes during a small operation.
But if you see that it has already closed up, then do not repeat on it at all; for nothing will be gained by this. And place over it some oil and lint; for it relieves the flow of blood. And it is better than oil itself and other oils in this situation.
And similarly, use this mixture of oil in every bloodletting you perform, when blood vessels are let in order to stop the flow of blood; and many people add storax, aloeswood, or saffron to it when applying it on the wound.
For blood becomes thinner and is resolved when it is thick.
If a large abscess occurs at the site of bloodletting - which happens often, especially with someone who is bled only occasionally or has a small vein opening - then quickly apply a sponge soaked in warm water and salt to the area, binding it tightly for an hour; for this will dissolve it.
And you must do this after blood has stopped flowing from the vein itself, when its flow is complete, or from another vein.
But if some blackness or greenness remains at the site after a few days, this is not harmful; and if you wish, apply to it some aloe, or myrrh dissolved in wine, or juice of mint, that is, pennyroyal or something similar.
Indeed, many swellings and elevations occur at bloodletting sites from the basilica vein.
Therefore, place your hand over it; if you find softness when compressing it, then that swelling is bad. So beware to put upon it something of what we have said. For perhaps blood will flow from it from the artery.
But make a plaster for it with that which has astringency, so that the place may harden. Then care for it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
And it is necessary to withdraw from all men as much blood as its strength demands. And that which appears shining above the blood.
For if the blood is black, then let it flow out until it turns red; and similarly if you see it thickened; then let it flow out until it thins; and similarly if it is sharp, until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for one who is full, strong, and needs bloodletting once to make the bloodletting vein large and for the bloodletter to be wide; but for one who is weak, it should be done accordingly.
And it is necessary to withdraw it at intervals and for the opening to be narrow; and what is better administered in bloodletting of the vein is that it be a transverse cut, not a puncture.
And this method of bloodletting is safe from the flow of blood and from cutting the nerve; and it is more praiseworthy and safer than bloodletting through width and length.
And for one who is accustomed to fainting at bloodletting, it is necessary to feed him beforehand with a small piece of bread soaked in pomegranate water or dry ginger if it is warmed; and withdraw from him blood in three or four intervals.
And if he is of a cold temperament, then let him take beforehand bread soaked in pomegranate syrup or in honey-scented syrup with spices, or in scented syrup with fragrant herbs.
But if fainting occurs during bloodletting and the cause of it is much blood flowing out, then it is necessary for him to drink water from flesh and thin fragrant syrup; and let him use aromatics with aloe and let his chest be anointed with it; and let him use the rest which we have said in division in the chapter on fainting from evacuation.
He who wishes to evacuate his arm and release his blood a second time: it is necessary for the one whose bloodletting is done on account of much evacuation, and whose strength is weak, that he releases his blood gradually by the amount of virtue over continuous days.
But whoever wishes to evacuate his arm and release his blood a second time, and his body is strong, then let him do it after seven hours or nine from his first bloodletting.
But whoever wishes to draw blood from his body towards the opposite part to which it inclines, it is necessary for him to be evacuated on the second day or third.
But if in someone's body there is much blood already heated and corrupted which causes fever to occur, it is necessary for blood to flow out from him at once and a great quantity to be drawn out; and let the opening be enlarged until fainting occurs: afterwards, you being careful about all conditions of bloodletting, place your hand upon his pulse in the course of blood-flow so that death does not happen instead of fainting. For indeed it often happens thus when the bloodletter is ignorant and carelessness occurs.
And it is not necessary when you wish to release the arm and let blood a second time that if the vein's opening has already closed and exiting blood becomes difficult, then pressure should be applied forcefully upon it, and quickly straighten the curved [vein]. For indeed that is very harmful; nay, leave him alone until he can be bled a second time.
Or remove with care the coagulation which has formed from blood at the opening of the vein, and place upon it some salt dissolved in water. And put upon it some of theriac of Alfaruch or saffron, and apply gentle pressure until blood flows out.
But if the vein is already inflamed, then let it be, and do not cover it until the inflammation subsides.
But if there is necessity to let blood a second time and no excuse, then either do the phlebotomy on that same spot or on another vein of the other arm.

On Windpipe Surgery and Its Uses
Windpipes are made from horns, reeds, and glass.
And tracheotomy is performed in two ways. One way with scarification and bloodletting; the other way without scarification.
And this tracheotomy which is performed without scarification is done in two ways either with fire or without fire
And there are fourteen places on the body where tracheotomy is performed with scarification and bloodletting. Among these, one is called the "Ventosatio Alnocrati," located at the back of the head; another is called the "Alkehel," situated in the middle of the neck; two more are known as the "ventosae Alhagdahim," located on either side of the neck simultaneously; the "uentosae menti" is found beneath the jawline near the lower lip; the "uentosae duarum spatularum" affects both shoulders; the "uentosae Alachosos" is situated at the end of the tail; and finally, there are "uentosae duorum focilium," located in the middle of both arms, as well as the "uentosae duorum crurium" for both legs, and lastly, the "uentosae duarum cauillarum.
For tracheotomy does not draw blood from the small veins scattered throughout the flesh; therefore, its strength is not lost from them, as happens with phlebotomy. Nor should tracheotomy be performed with or without fire on any disease caused bypletion until the entire body has been emptied.
But if there is a need for tracheotomy due to illness or custom, it should be performed at any hour on the first day of the month, on the last day, and on the middle day, as well as whenever else may be necessary.
This is because there are some people whose blood multiplies within them to the point where they require its extraction through tracheotomy; upon such individuals, headache and shortness of breath occur.
And among these are those who find fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. And among them are those who experience itching on their face and forehead, darkness, and dimness of the eyes.
And among them are those who scratch the spot where the tracheotomy was performed. And among them is one whose laughter is multiplied (i.e., uncontrollable). And among them are those who find the taste of blood in their mouth; either the tongue becomes infected and spews forth blood. And among them is one whose sleep is multiplied (i.e., excessive). And among them are those who see blood and redness, slain men, wounds, and similar sights in their dreams.
Therefore, when we observe any of these symptoms, especially if it occurs on the third day of the month, we then command that a tracheotomy be performed after two or three days have passed.
The disadvantage of neck tracheotomy is this: because it causes heaviness in the head and the blood flows towards the eyes. But rather, it should be done after the evacuation of the entire body.
And this indeed is a tracheotomy instead of a cephalic phlebotomy.
It is not permitted to use it on those with cold constitutions or catarrh. For it brings great harm upon him.
And therefore, the elderly and those with cold head complaints should not use it.
But if one becomes accustomed to it, forgetfulness will ensue upon him.
Therefore, you must instruct the one performing the tracheotomy to temporarily withdraw his hand with the tube slightly towards the lower parts, fearing the generation of forgetfulness.
Tracheotomy or Alkehel is a substitute for dark-colored phlebotomy and church phlebotomy; therefore, it causes coughing, narrowing of breath, splitting of the breathing tool, coughing, and fullness.
And it is necessary that the Ventosa Alkehel be applied briefly, for if it is placed lower down, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
However, tracheotomy called Al-Hadzadin relieves pains occurring in the head, ophthalmia, hemicrania, perforation, and toothache; it is a substitute for church phlebotomy.
And it is necessary that you instruct the performer of tracheotomy not to press their hand with the incision, lest they strike an artery and cause bleeding.
Tracheotomy under the chin relieves bad breath in the mouth and gum disease, as well as similar conditions that affect the mouth; it serves as a substitute for phlebotomy at the two lips.
Tracheotomy with spatulas relieves alkahest (i.e., a mixture of herbs and minerals used medicinally), which is caused by congestion and heat.
Tracheotomy of the interior parts of both cheeks provides relief equivalent to phlebotomy of three main veins, basilica, fusca, and cephalica; for it attracts blood from all those tiny veins in the flesh, which then attract from other thicker veins until attraction reaches the three main veins.
And it is necessary that you instruct the performer of tracheotomy not to make the incision too deep, for the place is bare of flesh and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
A suction cup applied over haemorrhoids relieves piles (hemorrhoids) and lower ulcers.
And it is necessary that you instruct the performer to use a large glass suction cup and to make the incision large, because the location requires strong suction and delicate cups may break.
Large cupping glasses on the legs reduce pain markedly because they draw blood from the whole body and relieve ancient pains in the kidneys, womb, and bladder. They induce menstruation, expel bothersome humors, and serve as a substitute for phlebotomy of the saphenous veins and other vessels.
However, they greatly weaken the body and cause fainting in many people.
And cupping on the sides is akin to cupping on both legs.
The quality of cupping is that you should place the glass cup first and suck gently; do not prolong the duration of cupping, but apply them quickly and remove them quickly so that humors may come to their destination evenly. Repeat and continue this process until you see the area become reddened and swollen, and the blood begins to appear at the incision site. Then scarify and continue suction gradually. Then consider the dispositions of bodies.
For those who are of delicate flesh and sparse pores, it is necessary to scarify them only once, no more than that, so as not to cause an ulceration at the site.
And instruct the performer to make the incision wide and penetrate it slightly; let them control the suction gently, quickly, and smoothly.
If there is thickness in the blood, then it is necessary to scarify twice. The first incision opens a path for the refinement of blood and its purging, while the second enables careful examination and thorough extraction of thick blood.
If however the blood is very impure, then a third incision should be made to reach the final extraction.
And finally, when we wish to extract blood gradually or in small amounts, one incision will suffice us. If we wish to draw out much blood, we make many incisions.
And if we measure that the blood is thick, then it is necessary to make an incision deep; and the equal end of the incision is the depth of the skin.
And what should be administered from oils at the placing of Ventosae, and what it prevents when applied.
In that case where the skin is thick, hard, dark, and its pores are closed, it is necessary to anoint the places of Ventosa with oils that are open, soothing, and dissolvent: if it is in the season of summer, such as oil of alkanet, or oil of violets, or oil of sweet almonds, or oil of cucumber seed.
And if it is in the time of winter, then oil of narcissus, or oil of lily, or oil of chamomile, or turpentine: and similar things.
But if there is an abundance of thick, cold matter, then let the oil be myrrh, or balsam, or white oil, or castor oil: and similar things.
But if the one to be cupped has large pores and tender flesh, then it is necessary to forbid oil, and it is necessary that they wash the places of Ventosa with rose water, or cold water, or solatium water, or cucumber water, or purslane water: and similar things.
But he whose blood is of great moisture should wash the cups with vinegar, or with myrtle water, and sumac: and similar things.
But he whose excesses are thick should wash his cups with old wine, or sampsuch water, or decoction of castor oil, or chamomile: and similar things.
And it is necessary to avoid cupping in a bath, or on the trace of a bath; indeed, it is necessary that it be administered after exiting from the bath for an hour, or two hours.
And one should not sleep after cupping.
What is it that must be regulated before cupping: the ventosus (person subject to winds), or the phlebotomatus (bloodlet)?
And afterwards, one should first consider this: if the ventosus or phlebotomated person is choleric, and sharpness and inflammation prevail over their blood, then it is necessary for them to take cooling remedies, such as pomegranate juice; and also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and cumin, and iuleb, and similar things.
And let their food be young, and they should avoid eating lamb and cooked foods, and let them eat unseasoned foods with greens; and similar things.
And if their complexion is cold, it is necessary for them to take in drink syrup of honey, or almonds, or cumin made from seeds; and let them drink moderately scented wine that is between old and new; and they should be prescribed to use sparingly food. And let their food be young chickens, and hares; and sparrows, that is, small birds; and they should avoid eating young pigeons.
And it is necessary that on the day of cupping and bloodletting there be more drink than food. And it is necessary to give in drink to certain people in the hours of theriaca alfaroch, or medicine of musk, that is, muscat, or alesselithe before cupping, or after; so that the principal members may be strengthened, and the blood may be subtilized. And they should not take it in heated drink.
On Ventosus without scarification. But ventosas that are placed without scarification are those which are put over the liver, and spleen, and on gouty parts, and on the belly, navel, and locations of the kidneys, and on the hypochondria; because these members do not tolerate scarification upon them.
And they are sought only to draw out blood from one member to another; thus we place a ventosa on the breasts in the illness of bloody flux from the nose. And we use them to loosen from a member the cold windiness that has entered into it; thus we place a ventosa on the belly and navel. For it indeed rarefies the member, warms it, and removes pain thereby because it resolves that windiness.
And it is placed on the kidneys when there occurs in them retention of urine or a stone. For by its power of attraction perhaps it opens the retention, or rooting out the stone from its place.
And similarly it does when placed on the liver and spleen to draw off windiness from both of them.
And these ventosas are administered empty with only a small cut. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full with warm water in cases of pleurisy; and this is so that the ventosa may be filled with only hot water, or with water in which suitable herbs have been decocted.
Method of applying a ventosa with fire.
Let the width of its opening be two fingers' breadth open, as we have fashioned it; and let its depth be half a palm; and let there be a small hole in its side about midway, sufficient for a needle to enter; let it be made of thin, smooth, equal, and polished bronze or glass; so that it may not harm the member upon which it is placed; and let there be a transverse rod of iron or brass in its midst, where the candle with fire is placed.
And now make this ventosa large, larger than we have described, and somewhat smaller than that one, according to the illness and age of the person who uses it.
For the ventosas for children and those who are thin are different from those for men and those with large bodies.
But the quality of applying this ventosa with fire on a limb is to light a wick with fire made of clean linen or a small candle made of wax: and place it on top of the crossbar which is in the middle of the ventosa: so that the fire is burning around the lower part of the ventosa, so that the body of the sick person does not burn.
Then place it on the limb and keep your finger over the hole I mentioned until you feel the ventosa holding: when you need to release it, remove your finger and let the vapor come out through that hole, and the ventosa will be released immediately.
Then relight the wick according to its custom, and repeat this procedure if necessary.
But the ventosa used for the illness of pleurisy with water does not have a crossbar nor a hole and is only applied when it is filled with water and placed on the limb.
And this is its shape.
The larger this ventosa is, so that it can hold much water, the better it is.
On the Suspension of Bloodletters.
Bloodletting is performed on many parts where the application of ventosas is not possible due to their narrowness, such as the lips and gums, and similar areas. Or on a member denuded of flesh, such as fingers, nose, and similar parts.
And the quality of using them is to take the veins from bloodletters which are found in fresh waters, clean from corruption.
Then they should be kept for a day and night in fresh water until they are hungry and there is nothing left in their bellies. Then the body is first evacuated with bloodletting and ventosas. Then cleanse the limb of the sick until it reddens. Then place them upon it.
When they are filled and fall, if suction of the place with ventosas is possible, then it is more ultimate in healing. If not, then wash the place with vinegar. Then with much water, rub and squeeze out.
But if the flow of blood continues after removing the bloodsucker, and it is oozing: then dip a linen cloth in cold water and place it upon it until the oozing ceases.
But if blood multiplies, then sprinkle upon it powder of ground zedoary or gall nuts and similar things from astringents until the bleeding stops.
Or place upon the location halves of peeled broad beans and let them remain until the beans adhere to the location, for blood will stop.
And it is necessary if repetition of bloodsuckers is necessary when possible to change them.
But if bloodsuckers are prohibited by suspension then cleanse the location with fresh blood. Or stick needles into locations until something comes out from the blood. Then place them, for they themselves when they sense anything coming out from the blood adhere immediately.
When therefore you wish that they fall, sprinkle upon them some ground aloes or salt or ashes and they will fall immediately.
This chapter is also very necessary many times in the art of Medicine, and it is about the restoration of fractures and dislocations of bones that occur accidentally.
Know this, O sons, that many foolish doctors and commoners boast about this chapter, but they have never looked at the book of the ancients nor read a single letter from it.
And therefore, this method is private among our sciences. I have never found anything good in it at all, and I did not obtain from it what I have obtained except through the prolonged reading of ancient books and study on their understanding until I extracted this knowledge from them.
Then I clung to experimentation and study for the length of my life.
And now I have written down for you all that this chapter contains of my knowledge: and beyond what has been proven by experiment, after abbreviating it for you, and relieving it from the burden of lengthening, and abbreviating it to an end of abbreviation, and demonstrating it to an end of demonstration, I have formed many shapes out of the forms of instruments that are used therein, with a surplus of demonstration, just as I did in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter 2
It is necessary that we say in the prologue of this chapter the summary of speech and chapters, understanding of which the artisan is compelled above all to stand on their truth: namely so that you and those who are eager may know or learn this noble art from you.
I say therefore that when a fracture or dislocation or torsion or fall happens to someone, then it is necessary first of all to hurry to bleed him and resolve him, or both at the same time: if nothing prohibits it, such as weakness of power; or if he who has suffered something from them is a child or an elderly decrepit person; or if it is the time of extreme heat or extreme cold.
Then he should be satisfied with his food, eating cold vegetables and poultry and fish; and forbid wine, and coarse meats, and filling oneself with foods and all meals that fill the veins with blood. Until when you are secure from hot apostema (abscess) and do not fear the discharge of material to the member; then the sick person should return to his first diet with which he was accustomed.
Therefore, when the broken bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary for the sick person to eat foods that are highly nutritious and substantial, coarse, and heavy, in which there is viscosity; such as dishes made from cooked wheat with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and intestines of sheep, and eggs, and fresh fish: and thick wine, and similar things. For by this regimen the fracture is quickly and better coagulated.
And know that broken bones, when they are in the first stage of old age and decrepitude, it is not possible for them to be continuously united and consolidated according to their nature always, because of the dryness of their members. But those which are at the end of infancy are softened and united, like the bones of young children.
But nevertheless nature connects something similar to glue around the broken bone on all sides, which is thick and adheres to it and binds it until they are glued together and bound together until they come to the end of strength and firmness, as they were at first, unless something prevents them from operating in this way. And for this reason it is necessary that you provide nutritious food for the sick person, which has weight and viscosity and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse, according to the diversity of limbs: for a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the skull; and a fracture of the chest bone is different from a fracture of the back bone, and similarly all other limbs exist diversely among themselves. And I come with a remembrance of every type of fracture explained in its own chapter, distinct from another. And even within itself, the species of bone fracture are differentiated.
For a fracture occurs curved in it, besides the fact that stars [i.e., splinters] occur in it. And sometimes a fracture occurs along the length of the bone: and there are fractures of it which are jagged, that is, splinters, and additions proceeding like those in a reed when it is prepared for writing with a penknife. And not proceeding: and there is a fracture with wounding and dislocation at the head, and there is a fracture which is a small split.
And for every type of fracture there is an art or proper instrument for its restoration, according to the manner which will be remembered, distinguished in their respective places.
And from that by which a bone fracture is recognized, there is its torsion and its prominence, and its appearance to the senses, and its sound when it is squeezed in your hand.
Therefore, when there is no apparent torsion in its place, nor sound at your touch when you grasp the bone with movement, and the patient does not find great pain, then there is no fracture there, indeed it is possible that there may be a slight bend or small split. Therefore, it is not necessary to move it with your hand and compression altogether, but rather apply medicines over it, of which the memory will come, after the time suitable for the place: thereafter let it be bound with a fine bandage.
And know that when a place is bent and split into two beyond its usual state, nevertheless all parts will be removed from their comparison. Then it is necessary for you to begin immediately to straighten it, leveling it before an abscess forms.
But if an inflamed abscess should occur, then leave it alone for a few days until the inflammation subsides. Then level it according to whichever way is possible with gentleness and ingenuity.
And know that its restoration and leveling are easier than for bones where sprains occur.
Then bind it according to what you remember of its manner.
But if there are sprains in the bone, then it is necessary to extend this broken limb from both sides, whether it be a hand or foot: or with your own hand, if it is a small member: or with two ropes, or with one rope and your hand.
And let your position be such that you place the limb on an even surface according to its natural shape until the broken parts extend. Then try with all ingenuity and whichever way you can to bring back those additions to their proper places: and beware according to your strength, lest pain or injury occur upon the patient through your operation. And try with all your strength to apply one of the two bones together with its fellow, according to the better forms.
And at that moment, it is necessary for you to test and touch it with your hand. Therefore if you see something different there, correct it, and make it equal by the amount of your strength.
