And beware excessive stretching and strong compressing, as many foolish ones do. For often they cause by their operation an inflamed abscess or a contraction in the limb; as we have already seen happen many times on account of their operations.
Then, after equalizing and performing the operation properly, and binding that limb tightly, ensure rest and calmness for the patient. And instruct him to avoid moving it during his wakeful hours, sleep turns, and movements of any kind, using all his strength. And that he should beware of placing the limb in a position that causes pain. Which means that whenever he feels pain or injury due to the position of the limb, then it is necessary to change its position to one which does not cause pain.
And beware that his position remains equal and straight, so that no twisting occurs in the limb during recovery.
And the binding of a broken limb is as I have described.
Know that broken limbs differ in their fragility and size, and also in shape.
Therefore those which are small, such as the arm, finger, toe, and similar parts, should be wrapped with soft, fine cloth. But those which are large, such as the thigh and leg, require a wide, firm wrapping.
For a wide band connects the large limb and evenly squeezes it from all sides, so that no bruising occurs.
Then, after evacuating the limb and bringing it back into alignment, begin by applying an appropriate ointment on soft lint over the fracture site. Then begin wrapping the bandage around the fracture site three or four times, according to what is appropriate for the limb, and hold your hand lightly with the bandage.
Then go with him to the upper part of the fracture site, and bind it tighter than you bound the broken place itself. Then extend yourself with the wrapping from the fracture site a little bit, and gradually loosen the binding until you grasp something large from a healthy place.
Then take another bandage and wrap it again over the fractured area several times. Afterwards, go with the wrapping to the lower part from the fracture. And let your operation be in tightening and loosening of the wrapping, according to what we said concerning the first upper wrapping.
Then place between the wrappings soft wool or cloth, equal to the unevenness of the fracture if it is uneven; but if not, do not place anything in it.
Then wrap another bandage over it. Afterwards apply splints immediately if there is no swelling or hot inflammation at the site.
But if there is inflammation or abscess at the site: then place over it something that sits on it and reduces the inflammation; and leave it for several days. Then bind splints over it.
And let the splints be made from the midsections of broad, smooth reeds, or from wood used to make winnowing fans, which are made from pine or gerid (date palm branches), or calingi. The width of each splint should be according to this shape and figure.
However, the splint placed directly over the fracture should be slightly thicker and wider than the others; its length should be according to the size and thinness of the limb.
Then bind another bandage over the splints in the same manner as the first binding. Then bind with bands from above in the same manner as binding; and it should be that your binding over the fracture site is greater, and the further away from the fracture, the less binding there should be.
And it's necessary that the threads are of medium thickness and fineness, and made of soft linen.
For if the threads are thick, as I have seen done by foolish practitioners who use twisted linen cords, this is a great mistake; for the binding will slip out of place with them.
And fine threads are also not suitable: for you will not achieve your desired result through binding with them.
And it's not necessary that the space between each lace be less than a finger's width.
But if the patient is injured by the edges of the laces after binding in healthy places, then place soft cotton or dyed wool underneath them until no further injury occurs.
But when there is a fracture with a wound, and disruption of the skin occurs, I will deal with this separately in Chapter Two according to its specific nature.
And know that not every broken limb needs to be bound with laces from the very first day. That is, because when the limb is large, you should not apply laces to it until five days have passed, or seven, or more, according to your assurance that there will be no hot swelling.
And this is the method of poultices which the ancients used to apply over fractures and dislocations.
This is the general method of the poultice for healing fractures, suitable for many constitutions, especially women and children; because it does not cause excessive heat or cold.
And take some fine flour from the millstone's grindings; that is, the dough stuck to the walls of the mill near the moving stone. And prepare it thus, except sieve it with egg white; and make its consistency neither too thick nor too thin. Then use it.
Another method of plastering, for setting fractures, reducing dislocations, and treating sprains. Take of almonds, and laudanum, and acacia, and mallow, and mugwort: and juice of all of them equally, and myrrh 5 grains, and both kinds of aloes each 5 grains, and gum arabic 20 grains. Grind all these together and sift them, and mix them with the water of gum arabic or with the white of an egg if the patient is hot-tempered. Then apply this plaster. For it is a good mixture that quickly sets broken bones and benefits many people.
Another method of plastering which also benefits fractures and sprains. Take of mugwort, and pounded barley, and white mallow, of each 10 grains; myrrh 5 grains, and both kinds of aloes each 5 grains, acacia 6 grains, and bolus armeniacum 20 grains. Grind all these well and sift them, and make them into a plaster with water or egg white: and apply.
A method of plastering for unions and bones separated from their places; it also quiets the pain resulting from them, and prevents splitting of bone and fracture. Take fine wool and soak it in a mixture of vinegar and decocted oil: then place it on the affected area.
And indeed this plaster has no power to set bones, but is good for alleviating hot swellings and expelling pains specifically.
A method of plastering that sets broken bone. Take leaves of fig and leaves of wild poppy: grind them together and make a plaster of both.
A method of plastering for accelerating the setting of a fractured bone and when you wish to resolve remaining inflammation. Take of greater celandine root, chamomile flowers, violets flowers, and herb flour, of each an equal portion. Grind all together and mix with wine, unless the member is hot.
But if it is hot, then prepare it with water of fresh coriander or simply with water, and use it thus.
Here is another method for a stronger plaster to resolve hardness when an abscess occurs during the healing of a member. Take of greater celandine root, linseed, tragacanth gum, melilot seed, sampsoc (incense), violet flowers, and chamomile flowers, all in equal portion. Grind all together and mix with willow water or sweet water or wine. All of this according to the warmth of the member and the calmness of its heat.
Hippocrates himself did not say in his book that anything should be placed on a broken member during its healing except for a cataplasm made of wax and oil. He said nothing else, and he reported that it should be of medium consistency between thickness and thinness.
But Galen thought that things which dry heat should be placed on a broken member during its healing, such as myrrh, aloe, and frankincense, and similar substances from those we have mentioned.
Here is the method of a plaster that relieves wounding and pain. Take equal portions of tragacanth gum, chickpea flour, human hair cuttings or bird feathers, greater celandine root, and salt. Grind and sift them together and make a plaster from it.
The amount that should be used is such that the plaster remains on as a dressing, then is dissolved. Then consider whether any pain or itching has occurred, nor whether the broken bone moves from its place. If not, do not dissolve it for many days. But if severe itching or disturbing pain or swelling occurs in that place, then act quickly and dissolve the plaster in an hour, and remove it from him. Then take a soft cloth or soft marine sponge, wet it in warm water, and wipe the area with it until the itching stops and the pain subsides.
But if severe itching or disturbing pain or swelling occurs in that place, then act quickly and dissolve the plaster in an hour, and remove it from him. Then take a soft cloth or soft marine sponge, wet it in warm water, and wipe the area with it until the itching stops and the pain subsides. Then allow the limb to rest for one hour. Then place on it a piece of wool soaked in vinegar and oil or rose oil, and bind it there overnight until you are secure from the risk of hot discharge, and the swelling of the limb has subsided and its pain is relieved.
Then repeat the procedure for a tight binding and a small plaster; but do not tie too tightly at first, using fine bindings with it until it heals.
But if you see that the abscess and redness, pain, swelling, and all other symptoms are completely gone, and there is need for a plaster and binding, then repeat the procedure as you did initially, equally.
But if nothing happens to the limb from what we have said, then do not dissolve it unless after three, four, five, or seven days. And when it is removed after twenty days, according to what appears to you from the dissolution of the limb, as we have said, until when the fracture adheres and the coagulation of flesh comes close over it: then add also at that time more binding than your first binding throughout, and also increase the thickness of the sick person's food, according to what has been remembered.
But if you see the place of fracture already dried out and thinned more than it should be, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. Therefore moisten it with warm water wherever you dissolve it, and let it remain for three days to somewhat alleviate the binding.
Through this operation, nourishment runs to the limb and it is healed quickly.
But what foolish restorers do instead of another when a limb fracture is not first restored as it should be, and is restored crookedly, that is an error from their operation and great fear. And if it were right, the ancients would have said it in their books and worked with it.
And I did not see any trace of theirs at all in that, and it is better that it should not be done.
On the treatment of accidents to the head.
There are various kinds of fractures, and many shapes. Their causes are distinguished from one another.
For there is the fracture which occurs from being struck by a sword; and the blow removes bone completely until it reaches the sieve (siphac), which is under the bone, just as an axe does to wood: and on account of this, this kind of fracture is called axial.
Or a sword strikes something of the bone and cuts its surface but does not cut through to the end of it: and this kind of fracture is called Kalaan, that is, a simple cut.
And there are wounds of two kinds of fractures, either large or small.
And there is fracture which is bruising or grinding: and its cause is being struck by a stone, or falling onto one, or similar things.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which nearly reaches the periosteum under the bone, or is on the surface of the bone. It is a wound of these two kinds of fractures, whether small or large.
And there is fracture which is hidden in the bone like a hair's breadth: it is a small crack, and therefore this kind of fracture is called capillary.
And among these is the fracture which occurs from falling or being struck by a stone or similar things, which causes the bone's surface to enter inward and creates a hollow space, like when bronze kettles are struck and part of them enters inward.
Indeed this happens most often in the heads of moist bones, such as those of children.
And for all these kinds of fractures, splints are made separate from one another, and I shall describe the entire process in its proper place.
And we recognize all these kinds of fractures through inspection upon them and examination with threads, and the removal of decayed flesh which is above them.
The capillary type is recognized by this: it is detected on the bone and cleaned and polished with cauterization; for the fracture appears black.
But in treating a fracture, first consider the condition of the patient. Then if you see from these conditions signs indicating manifest fear, such as vomiting of bile, trembling, loss of consciousness, loss of speech, shortness of breath, acute fever, protrusion of eyes, redness of them, and similar things in many dispositions, by no means attempt to treat it. But if you see non-terrifying conditions and hope for recovery, then begin the treatment.
Which means: if a wounded person comes to you first when they are wounded, and this happens especially during the cold season, then it is necessary that you strive for the removal of bone before the fourteenth day according to every disposition.
And if it is during the hot season, then it is necessary that you hastily remove the bone before the seventh day, before decay sets in under the bone due to inflammation and those accidents which we have mentioned occur.
But if the fracture of a bone has reached the brain-pan and is accompanied by small fragments and attraction (i.e., tension), then it is necessary that you cut away the broken and crushed portion according to what I shall tell you.
And it is necessary that you shave the head of the wounded person and clear away from the bone as much as possible according to the shape of the wound and what is lightest for the patient.
But if during the exposure of the bone there occurs a flow of blood or a hot swelling, then apply to it what is appropriate. And it is necessary that you fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil, and let the wound rest until the swelling subsides, and be secure from the flow of blood. Then begin the treatment of the bone and its removal.
And this is done according to one of two methods.
One method is as follows: Incise the bone with an incising knife of sharp point: and this is its shape.
Then use another incising knife, wider but not too much so, in this manner: and this is its form.
Then also use another incising knife even wider than the second one.
And the second method is this: Have with you a number of incising knives of various widths, some wider than others and some shorter than others. They should be sharp-pointed at their extremities and made of steel or iron. Use them skillfully in percussion so that they do not strike the bone and harm it.
If the bone is hard and strong, then it is necessary to perforate around it with trepanes called "non-profounding trepanes," before using the incising knives. These are not named so-called because they do not penetrate beyond the thickness of the bone's flesh. This is due to the fact that the end of the trepan is round, similar to a margin or circle, which prevents it from sinking too deep and penetrating the bone's thickness.
And it is fitting that you take many of these trepanes, each suitable to the thickness of the bone, until you have enough for the entire skull. The sharp end of each trepan should be as long and short according to the thickness of the skull.
And this is the form of three kinds of trepans or borers.
The quality of boring through the circumference of a broken bone is such that you place the trepan on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been pierced. Then move the trepan to another location and place what is between every hole with the thickness of a thread or approximately that.
Then cut with scalpel what is between every hole from the bone, and make it finer than possible on top of its thinness, as we have said, until you remove the bone either by hand or with some other instrument prepared for that purpose such as tweezers and fine forceps.
And it is necessary to take every care that the trepan or scalpel does not touch anything of the patient's flesh.
Therefore, when you have circumcised the bone by boring and it has been separated from the flesh, if it is continuous with it and you have removed it, then shave and level its remaining end on the bone with another instrument similar to the scalpel. However, it should be thinner and finer than other scalpels.
But if there remain some small bones and splinters, then take them out with ease using the instruments you have at hand. Then take care of the wound afterwards with linen cloths and ointments which we have mentioned.
And there is another method of operation that is easy and entirely free from fear, which Galen has spoken about and praised highly, saying thus. It is necessary for you first to begin uncovering the part of the bone from the place where the fracture is more severe and obvious until when you have uncovered that part, you place under it the end of this lenticular scalpel whose shape is like this.
And the lenticular part is not sharp enough to cut anything, but its pointed part on both sides extends lengthwise like this so that the lenticular part rests on the flesh with its cutting edge pointing towards the bone.
Then strike with the cutting tool on one side using a small hammer until you cut through the entire bone easily, as it rolls around; and you should be confident that nothing will happen that you might fear.
And if the physician were not foolish among men, more ignorant of this [knowledge], and asleep, then if there remained anything attached to the bone from the flesh in certain places, he would cut it off with the pointed end of the lenticular part of the cutting tool and free it easily. For it is freed from it without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the flesh, and that which is cut off from the bone is its outer surface, and there remain on it roughnesses and fine spurs: then it is necessary to shave off that roughness with fine razors that you have prepared in various quantities so that it may be possible for you to administer those which are most suitable and straight according to what the operation requires, and the fractured bone, and its shape. And it is necessary that you use your judgment in this first shaving of the bone with the razors. Then afterwards use a finer one.
And it is necessary that you use your judgment in this first shaving of the bone with the razors. Then afterwards use a finer one. And do not cease doing this continuously until you reach the point of using the thinnest and finest ones of them all.
But in each small capillary crack or fine fracture that remains, you must give care according to what leads to its correction.
And this is something that cannot be concealed from anyone who has any interest in this art and who has stood on what we have written and demonstrated concerning great fractures.
But if the flesh is detected and remains when you cut the bone, then it is necessary to take a linen cloth according to the size of the wound, and dip it in rose oil, and place it on the opening of the wound. Then take another cloth doubled or tripled, and dip it in wine and rose oil, and place it on top of the first one. Do this as easily as possible so that it does not burden the patient.
Then apply a wide bandage on top of it. Do not bind it too tightly, but only enough to hold the wound. Leave it on for one day or two days until you are sure there is no risk of inflammation. Then remove it and apply powders afterwards to draw out excess moisture from the wound, such as lily roots, fern powder, frankincense powder, aristolochia, and similar substances. Make a powder from these medicines to sprinkle on the dry wound.
And generally apply any medicine that has the property of cleansing without biting. Also be careful, according to your skill, that the wound remains clean during its healing, without any dirt or filth from ointment.
Also be careful, according to your skill, that the wound remains clean during its healing, without any dirt or filth from ointment. And do not allow pus to gather there at all. For when pus collects on the brain membrane, it corrupts it and causes it to putrefy, leading to great harm to the patient afterwards. This is especially true for head wounds when the bone has been cleaned, particularly if its care is neglected.
Therefore, consider this: if the accidents we have mentioned occur to the patient, know that it is undoubtedly harmful.
And if blackness does not occur except from the medicine applied to it, and that medicine has the power to cause such blackness, then take one part honey, three parts rose oil, and mix them with linen cloth. Then place it on the siphac [a type of snake]; afterwards treat it with appropriate remedies until it heals.
Chapter On the Restoration of the Nose When It Is Broken
Know that the nose is not broken except in its upper two parts simultaneously or one of them; this is because it has two bones: since the lower part is cartilaginous, it does not break, and only friction, twisting, and bending occur to it.
If one of its two parts is broken, then you should insert a little finger into the nostril and gently press that fracture with your index finger from inside and thumb from outside until the nose returns to its natural shape. And this should be done skillfully with ease. And be careful lest by your operation you cause pain to the patient.
But if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and the finger does not reach it, then you should use the tip of a probe with a small thickness to align it.
But if there is a fracture in both parts, then make something similar [to the probe] and hasten to its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible. And if not, then on the seventh or tenth day when the swelling from the heat has subsided.
Then insert a linen wick into the tip of the nose if there is a fracture in one part, or two wicks if there are fractures in both parts. And let the thickness of the wick(s) be such that it fills the nostril.
And some ancient restorers said to imbue the wicks with butter and change them every day. But it seems better to me that the wick should be dipped in the white of an egg mixed with fine flour. Then let the splint sit until the bone sets and the cartilage hardens.
And sometimes a swan's feather is inserted into the nose instead of a splint, wrapped in soft cloth, to hold the fracture of the nose more firmly and allow the patient to breathe without hindrance.
This is not necessary. If you wish, do this; if you wish, do splints.
But if the nose is broken again while treating a hot boil, apply poultices made with wax, or cot (cotton) soaked in rose oil, or with a little dill.
But if there is no hot boil, then it is necessary to apply poultices made with sesame flour and powdered frankincense mixed with egg white. Then place over it soft wadding, and do not bind the nose with anything at all.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or crushed, then it is necessary to cut over them and remove them using appropriate instruments. Then close the wound, and treat it with flesh-forming poultices or ointments that suit it to promote healing.
But if a wound occurs between the nostrils, then you should treat it with splints and apply a lead tube until it heals.
On the restoration of the lower jawbone when it is broken
If the lower jawbone is broken without a wound, and the fracture extends only to its outer surface without being completely split into two pieces, then its diagnosis is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side of the jawbone, insert your left index finger into the mouth of the patient and push it inward to meet the convexity of the fracture from outside with your other hand placed correctly inside the bone until it is aligned. Do similarly if the fracture is on the left side using your right index finger.
If the jawbone fracture is already separated into two pieces, then you should apply traction from both sides according to its alignment until it can be realigned.
But if the teeth have already been displaced or shattered, then secure those that you hope will remain with gold wire, silver wire, or sinew. Then place on the fractured jawbone a hot compress. Next, place over it a doubled cloth, and on the cloth place either a well-made large plaster, or an equal piece of sole leather cut to the length of the jawbone.
Then bind it from above according to the preparation of its bandaging and the suitability of its application until it is healed. Instruct the patient to remain quiet and at rest, and provide them with soft foods for sustenance.
But if you judge that there is some alteration of its shape on the third day, then act quickly and remove it. Then correct what has been altered and apply a poultice made from ground grain dust with egg white or sesame flour after removing the hot compress. Place over it soft wool.
While that poultice remains applied to it and the position of the bone is not altered, leave it alone without removing it until it heals or else secure the fracture. For often this fracture becomes fixed in three weeks.
But if a hot swelling occurs meanwhile, then apply the remedies mentioned earlier to alleviate it until it subsides.
And if there is also a wound with the fracture, consider this: If a fragment of bone or splinter has already separated from the bone, then be careful to remove those splinters with appropriate instruments without causing further harm.
But if the wound opening is narrow, then dilate it using a dilator according to your need for bloodletting.
Then, after removing the splinters, if any remain, apply an appropriate plaster to widen the wound opening if it is narrow. If not, then apply a plaster that suits and adheres to it until it heals.
Chapter: On the repair of the furcula when broken.
The furcula is most often broken at its anterior part near the humerus, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways.
Either it breaks and separates into two pieces without any splinters, which makes restoration easier.
Or it happens that there are splinters in the bone, making restoration more difficult.
Or it occurs with a wound accompanying the fracture.
And when the furcula is fractured without a wound, perform as follows: Have two assistants present, one to hold the broken furcula in place and the other to extend the neck towards that part.
Then align the fracture with your fingers until it is properly shaped without any protrusion or concavity.
If you need further extension, then place a spherical pad made of cloth or wool under the patient's chin according to your needs. Then extend and elevate the furcula while compressing the sphere with your hand until the fracture is properly aligned.
If you cannot draw the end of the furcula outward because it has reached too deep, then have the patient lie on their back and place a folded cloth under their shoulder. Have an assistant press down on the patient's shoulder towards the lower part until the buried bone of the furcula is elevated upwards. Then align and straighten the fracture with your fingers.
If you feel a splinter from the furcula that moves, then search for it and remove it gently.
But if it is stuck in the bone, then use one of the prepared knives to make an incision over it. After placing the retractor below the furcula, which can be made of wood or iron, carefully extract the splinter with forceps. And this is its shape.
It resembles an un-hollowed snail shell.
Let its width be according to the size of the bone you need it for, and let it be thin enough not to cause harm. As for its length, make it as long as possible for the operation.
It should have two ends, one wide and one narrow, as you can see.
But if the wound you made while removing the bone splinter is large and there is risk of bleeding, then join the edges of the wound with stitches.
But if the disruption is small or there is no risk of bleeding, then fill the wound with linen and cotton according to its size.
If a hot inflammation occurs, then dip a cloth in rose oil, vinegar, and wine, and apply it to the affected area.
When setting a bone fracture without cutting or wounding, apply a plaster made of flour mixed with egg white over the bone. And place a soft cotton pad on top of it. Then place a splint under the affected limb if necessary. Next, take a roller, which is a long round tool used by the Saracens, with a width of about one palm. Then place doubled gauze over linen and the poultice. Next, take a star shape from thin wood, three fingers wide and similarly long. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap the cloth with the star over the location of the splint that falls upon the fracture and the affected area. Then place the splint over the fracture as I have described, and wrap it around its neck, under its healthy nostril, and under its sick nostril; bring it back over all parts, ensuring that the bandage holds the fracture securely in place without the star being visible or movable from above the broken bone. And therefore, you must examine the patient every day. Whenever the bandage becomes softened and you see that the star has shifted, then correct it and tighten the bandage.
Then place the patient's pillow on their back and put a small bolster under their nostril at night so that their helper can assist them from their side. Therefore, raise the broken collarbone by raising the arm.
Or bind the patient's arm to their neck; and do not remove the bandage unless something happens due to itching or gangrene up to the twelfth day.
Then renew the poultice if you see that its appearance has changed. And add to the bandage, and leave him until he is restored and healed.
And indeed, a broken collarbone is bound and strengthened most effectively within about twenty-eight days; but sometimes it happens more quickly.
On the restoration of fractures of the scapula.
The scapula is rarely broken, and only its edges are fractured.
When a fracture occurs in the middle of the scapula and cannot be seen except by touch, then prepare for its reduction according to the shape of the fracture. Reduce it back into its natural shape using all means at your disposal.
Then place over the site powder of millstone with egg white, and soft gum, and place over it a wadding made from doubled cloth. Then place on top a broad star-shaped piece of thin wood, either the size of the spatula or slightly larger.
But if there is a depression under the star-shaped piece of wood in any part of the spatula, then make it even with soft gum until the star-shaped piece sinks to an equal level.
Then bind it tightly from above with linen strips of appropriate length and strength so that the star-shaped piece does not move from its place.
And examine the bandage every day. Whenever the bandage becomes loose, tighten it again and make the star-shaped piece even if it has moved from its place: and have the patient lie on his healthy side.
And the scapula will be restored in twenty days or twenty-five days.
When this number is completed, then remove the bandage: and you shall be safe. For it (the scapula) belongs to those bones whose fracture is not feared nor their union.
But if a splinter of bone protrudes and punctures under the skin, then cut over it and remove it. And do as we have said for fractures of the fork (scapula) concerning the evacuation of a hot abscess if any should occur.
Concerning the restoration of fractures of the chest.
The chest sometimes breaks in the middle and rarely occurs. Its extremities indeed are most often broken and crushed by its accidents.
When the middle of it is broken and slopes downward, causing severe pain, difficulty breathing, coughing, perhaps vomiting blood, and the concavity in the broken bone is evident to touch, the restoration is as follows: have the patient lie on his back and place a pillow between his shoulders.
Then press on his shoulders and bring together the ribs from both sides with your hands, gently adjusting him until the bone returns to its proper shape as much as possible.
Then place over him a plaster and wadding, and place on top of it a thin plate made of willow or limewood, or similar lightweight material, having wrapped it first in cloth. Then bind the splint firmly over the broken bone so that it does not move, and wrap it around to the back several times with bandages, securing it with a suitable tie.
Then check the bandage at every hour and tighten it whenever it becomes loose. If necessary, remove it for any pruritus, corrosion, pain or abscess that may occur at the site, then quickly replace the plaster and proceed with treatment accordingly.
Then reapply the plaster if necessary, and continue binding it until healing is complete.
Section on the Reduction of Broken Ribs
Know that rib fractures occur only at the thick ends adjacent to the spine and at the front tips of the ribs. And they do not break except because they are made of cartilage. And the fracture is evident to touch when examined with fingers.
The reduction of a rib fracture is performed by gently manipulating the broken ends with fingers until they align properly in their natural position. Then apply plaster and splint the broken bone with a plate if necessary.
And if the rib fracture slopes inward, severe pain and sharp chest pain occurs, similar to that experienced by a person with pleurisy. This is because the bone pierces the pleura, causing difficulty breathing, coughing, vomiting blood, and making treatment difficult.
But the ancients have devised many methods for this, among which some suggest placing food that causes inflammation and wind in the patient's belly to make it swell up, thus pushing the broken bone outward.
We, however, reject this method, as it could quickly cause a hot abscess to form if not done correctly. But if it has already occurred, then you proceed accordingly.
And some of them said: place a cupping glass on the site and apply strong suction; this is similar to reasoning. Yet, it is feared that the cupping glass may draw superfluous humors onto the location due to the weakness of the patient's constitution.
And some of them said: the place should be covered with wool that has been immersed in hot oil, and powdered incense should be placed between the ribs until it fills up, so that when it is wrapped around evenly according to its roundness.
Then the patient should be treated for pleurisy with diet and medicine.
But if the patient is severely constrained by the pain that they cannot bear it, and the bone pierces the pleura with a harmful puncture, we then fear for the patient. Therefore, we must cut over the location and expose the broken rib. Then place upon it the instrument called the "siphon," as mentioned earlier; cut the bone easily with it, and also cut into it.
Then bring together the two lips of the wound if it is large enough to suture; treat it with dressings until it heals.
But if a hot abscess occurs during this time, then promptly take powdered incense mixed with rose oil and apply it to the location. Then treat the patient accordingly for an abscess.
Meanwhile he should also lie on his side on which he sleeps until he is healed.
Chapter: On the reduction of fractures of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
Indeed, fracture sometimes occurs in the bones of the neck, and rarely do they become worn down. The same applies to the vertebrae of the back. Therefore, when this happens to someone and you wish to know whether it will heal or not, then consider carefully. For if you see that his hands are already softened, paralyzed, and lifeless, and he cannot move them, extend them, or flex them; and when you compress them with your fingers and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it and there is no pain in them: then know that it will not heal, and in most cases it is fatal.
But if he can move them and feels the pressure with your fingers and the prick of the needle, then know that the cervical spine is intact, and that the patient will recover with proper treatment.
But if it happens similarly to the vertebrae of the back and you wish to know whether it will heal or not, then consider his feet. If they are softened and the same thing has happened to them as to the hands, then when he lies on his back wind passes out involuntarily and waste matter is discharged without his will; and when he lies on his belly waste matter is discharged without his will; and when he lies on his back and wants to urinate but cannot: then know that it is fatal. Therefore, do not trouble yourself with treating him.
But if none of these things have happened to him, the case is less serious.
And its treatment consists in sedating the hot abscess that has occurred. Thus you should apply rose oil alone or with beaten egg whites to the worn-down vertebra.
Place this upon it several times a day until the hot abscess is sedated. Then place one of the strengthening and drying poultices on the spot and bind it with bandage: And instruct the patient to use rest and quiet. And do not let him lie upon that part which is painful until it is healed.
But if the bone has worn away or something has separated from it, then you should make an incision through the skin and remove that. Then join together the two lips of the wound with stitches, if it is large. Then treat it with some flesh-colored substance from among ointments until it heals.
But if the coccyx bone breaks off last and the end of the tail is finished: Then you should insert your left hand's thumb into the anus and align the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible, with the second alignment.
Then place upon it and remove it: And treat the wound with the preceding treatment until it heals.
On the restoration of the ankle.
Chapter 11
The bones of the ankles are rarely broken. If they are broken, then the fracture is not complete unless it is comminuted at their ends. They split lengthwise and incline inward, and the patient feels pain and tenderness in that place. The leg becomes paralyzed where it is broken.
And its restoration is as follows: You should lay your hand upon it until you feel the fracture and know its shape.
If the fracture is only at its ends, then align it according to your preparation for alignment until it resembles its natural shape.
But if the fracture is lengthwise or already inclined inward, then make the patient lie on his back until you have prepared the restoration of that fracture. Therefore, when you have aligned it, place upon it an emplaster; then place upon it a splint of wood or leather; and bind it with bandages so that you may not fear the displacement of the fracture nor the removal of the splint.
And equalize the cavity of them with that which fills it until it accepts the binding according to its equality; and instruct the patient to sleep on his back or on his sound side.
But if a hot abscess occurs to him, then cease from extending it and its restoration until the hot abscess subsides; and place upon it what sits upon it according to what preceded, then return to its restoration: and bind it as is necessary.
But if there is a crack or fragmentation of the bone, remove it.
On the restoration of fractures of the hand.
The hand is that which is between the elbow and the head of the ulna.
If it breaks, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One method is: take a flat piece of wood of medium thickness in this shape which follows.
And two ligaments shall be attached at both ends.
Then it shall be suspended from a raised place, and the patient shall sit on a chair. Then let him throw his broken arm onto the wood until it reaches his elbow, where there is attached a curved piece of wood.
Then hang something heavy from above so that the attendant may extend it towards the lower part. Then let the physician align the fracture with his hands until the fracture returns to its proper position.
And another method is: let the patient lie on his back and hang his hand from his neck with a bandage. Then instruct two attendants to hold one of them above the fracture with his hands and for the other to hold it below and extend each towards their respective parts.
And if you wish for a stronger extension, then tie beneath the fracture with one bandage and above it with another; and let each of the two attendants extend them towards their respective two parts.
Therefore if the fracture is near the end of the arm bone, then one bandage must be placed beneath the armpit and another beneath the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary to place a bandage above that spot and above the elbow itself.
Then bring together the fracture easily without effort until when it becomes equal according to what is appropriate and adheres with a good setting. Then bind it if there is no hot inflammation.
But if there is a hot inflammation, then leave the binding until the seventh day. And place upon it cut wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil until when the inflammation quiets down: then bind it; and the way to bind it is by placing a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a bandage made of new cloth over the plaster. Then bring the arm onto its support and place his open hand upon his shoulder.
And place cloth over the arm support and bind it so that the arm remains in its proper position if nothing prevents you from doing so or if no bone fragment is displaced.
But if you fear that something may be displaced from the broken bone, then use splints; and it is necessary to place over the fracture itself the splint which is broader and stronger than the others. And let each splint be as wide as a finger's breadth; and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an extra three fingers' width on either side.
Then bind over the splints with the bandage that I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it should be such that your binding is tighter over the site of the fracture. And the more elongated the fracture, the looser the binding should be.
Therefore, if you see that the splints and binding are in place as we have said, when you reset the limb immediately, then do this: and if you fear a hot abscess, then loosen the binding and splints for seven days, as we have said.
Then examine the ligature on all three days to ensure that there is no itching, swelling or restriction of food reaching the limb due to the binding. Therefore correct this entire process according to what we have said.
But if you are confident that nothing will happen from it, then do not untie the ligature until after three days or more.
And let the patient lie on their back with their hands on their stomach, and place beneath them a pillow filled evenly with wool for support.
And observe at all times during the night or day that the figure of the broken limb does not become dislodged nor the ligature loosened. Therefore correct this entire process with your utmost effort.
And feed the patient according to the order that we have previously mentioned, so that their food is thin initially until the bone begins to unite, then you should make their food thicker.
For it is customary for the splint and leg to be bound for forty days. Therefore at that time you should loosen it, apply a warm bath, and treat it with ointments that will straighten it.
But if the fracture is foul and crushed, then do not loosen the ligature and splints from it until fifty days have passed, or even two months.
Chapter on the Reduction of a Fracture of the Arm
The arm is composed of two bones which are called the two elbow bones (radius and ulna).
Of these two one is short, it is that which follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is that which lies below the short one from beneath.
Therefore perhaps only the larger funnel breaks, or the smaller one; and perhaps both break together.
When therefore the small funnel breaks above, then its restoration is easy, and healing of it is easier.
And when the lower funnel breaks, its fracture is bad, and healing of it is difficult.
And worse still is the case when both bones break at once.
Therefore if the bone which breaks is the small funnel above, then it becomes necessary for the physician to apply a slight extension at its restoration with ease until it matches.
But if the large funnel is that which is broken then it becomes necessary for him to apply a strong strapping very firmly.
And it becomes necessary for him to place the figure of a hand at its restoration and to extend its extension over the pulvinar. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers above, and let its lower joint be below all the fingers or others, and let the invalid sit squarely upon himself, and let the pulvinar be before him, or in his direct line, at a height such that he does not strain himself.
Then let the assistant extend the arm from below with his hand or with a bandage. And another assistant extend it likewise from above.
Then let the physician match the bone until he reduces it into the best figure that he can.
But if there are splinters in the fracture of the bone, then try with all your strength to reduce each piece into its proper place.
But if there appears to be a separated splinter which pricks the skin and there is no hope of restoring it, then cut above it and remove it according to the method which we have mentioned previously.
But if the fracture is with a wound, I have already made provision for that in a separate chapter, therefore take its treatment from there.
But if by chance a hot swelling appears at the beginning of your restoration, then make a poultice with wax made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be of medium thickness. And bind the cloth over it with a thin strap until when the swelling subsides. Then remove the poultice and apply a plaster made from flour dust with egg-white. Then place bandages, and let the one placed over the actual fracture be wider for a little while, and stronger.
And know that the number of bandages for the arm is six in most cases: whether it be a fracture in one joint or in two joints at once.
Then place your bandage over the location of the fracture more tightly and vigorously, and somewhat more with a bandage above the fracture or below it: or place a softer bandage on its bandage for a short time, according to what was previously mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
And let the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture be soft, moistened ones; and not hard or rough. And let the thread with which it is bound be of linen, properly medium between thin and thick, as we have described.
And observe the limb and its bandage after some days. That if something should happen which requires correction, such as itching that happens to the limb: then it is necessary to embrocate the limb with warm water until that itching stops, and leave the limb unbound until it stops, then repeat the bandaging.
That if the bandage has already softened and the bone is now removed, and similar things have happened: then correct that whole thing with your strength again, and observe it once more.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excess binding, then it is necessary to soften it for a short time and leave it unbound for some days until nourishment reaches it again, then bind it.
But if none of the things we have mentioned happen to the patient, then it is not necessary to remove the bandage unless after twenty days or roughly that amount of time.
Then, the sick person's hand should be suspended at their neck. And let their neck be level and protected by its own strength from sudden movements. Make him lie on his back to sleep.
Know that this fracture is restored within thirty days or at most forty-two days; it may also be restored within sixty days. All of this depends on the dispositions of temperaments and the dispositions of strength.
Chapter: On the restoration of fractures of the hand
Know that fractures of the wrist and joints of the fingers rarely occur. Nor does it happen except after repeated rubbing.
When a fracture or rubbing occurs at the wrist joint: then it is necessary for the patient to sit on a square seat; and in front of him there should be a seat of equal height. Then place his hand on it, extended outwards.
Then the assistant should extend the broken bones and the physician should align them until they join together in good union.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and cotton if no warm swelling occurs. Then place a bandage over the top according to the size of the area; and you should already have wrapped it in linen cloth.
If the fracture is near the lower part towards the palm of the hand, then make a similar sphere out of cloth; and instruct the patient to press it against the broken palm with their foot; then bind it with a long piece of cloth.
And let the bandage be made of leather that is soft so that it may be applied to the body of the palm; and bind it as is necessary.
If the fracture is on the outer side, then you should place one bandage over it and another below it on the palm; and let the hand be held straight out. Then apply a splint around the hand in this way: and extend it between the fingers with a bandage.
But if any of the joints of the fingers are broken, then if it is the thumb, it should be aligned as necessary. Then secure it with a splint; and if it is dislocated, place a small straight splint on it so that the fracture is elevated and not moved.
But if the fracture is in any of the other fingers, such as the middle finger or the index finger, or the ring finger and little finger, then align it and bind it with the healthy finger that follows it; or they may all be bound together in continuity: which is preferable. Or place on it a small splint as mentioned for the thumb.
And consider also in your treatment, and after this arrangement, apply a warm poultice; and oppose to it whatever is necessary when something happens from it, according to what is repeated in its description.
On the Treatment of a Fracture of the Hip.
The hip bone is often broken and its fracture is recognized because it turns towards the inner and back parts.
And its treatment is done in this way: a bandage is applied above the fracture and another below the fracture. And the patient should lie on their face.
Then stretch all the ligaments of the affected side according to equality: and this is done when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if the fracture is near the root of the hip, then a bandage should be applied to the chest side so that the extension falls upwards; and another bandage below the fracture.
And similarly if the fracture is near the knee, then a bandage should be applied near the knee so that the extension falls downwards.
Then the physician should align the bone with both hands until it is reduced according to its natural shape; and the bone should be united with a good union.
Then it is necessary to apply a poultice and a splint if there is no inflammation in the limb. But if there is an inflammation in it, then leave it for a few days until the hot inflammation subsides, then return to your treatment.
Moreover, let its stricture be like this. You must wrap a hard bandage around the fracture, that is, a linen cloth, and fold it twice or thrice, leaving some excess. Then wrap the leg until you reach the heel at the root of the thigh.
And insert a long thread between the hip and the leg near the knee towards the bottom, so that the ends of the thread come out from above on both sides. Then wind around the leg and hip what remains of the bandage.
Next, place splints on the hip at the location of the fracture itself, and place one splint over the leg bone.
Then fill in the spaces between the leg and hip with soft cloths so that the structure is even.
Then begin with a strong bandage from the middle over the location of the fracture, making three or four turns. And the further you extend the bandage away from the location of the fracture, let your binding be looser and softer.
Then take up the two ends of the thread which you inserted between the hip and leg. Therefore bind with each end three of the splints that are on top. Then go down with both ends of the thread until you reach the ankle bone. And tie both ends together along with the other ends of the splints so that the binding does not move from its place.
Then leave the bandage on for as long as necessary so that no injury, inflammation, or other such condition occurs to the limb. But if anything should happen from it. Therefore, promptly attend to its treatment and correct whatever has occurred accordingly, as we have said many times.
But if there is a splinter of bone that is sticking out and causing pain, then it is necessary to remove it, if possible. And if you cannot do so, find the splinter and remove it, treating according to its nature until healing occurs.
And sometimes this fracture of the hip is bound with splints as we have described in our treatment and support.
However, after its restoration, the injured person does not become lame. And if it is restored alone, without the application of the other leg, then it is necessary that he who has it will always limp.
And know that the hip is bound for fifty days, or a little more or a little less, all of this depending on different constitutions and other dispositions.
¶ On the restoration of the fractured patella of the knee
Know that fracture of the patella of the knee is rare, but it often happens to it.
But if a fracture does occur, it will be either a crack or a hole in its parts. And this happens with a wound and also without a wound. And place the whole thing back together with sensation.
And its restoration is as follows: bring together the parts that have been disrupted with your fingers until they join and connect according to what is possible with ease and skill.
Then apply a plaster made from sesame flour or finely ground grains mixed with egg white; place a round splint on it if necessary, and bind it with an appropriate bandage.
Then consider all the dispositions that we have described for other fractures, such as a warm swelling and similar conditions, so that you may oppose any accident with what is appropriate to it until healing occurs.
¶ On the restoration of a fracture of the leg
Know that there are two bones in the leg, one thick called the tibia and the other thin called the fibula.
And both can sustain fractures like those that occur in the two bones of the arm; therefore, their restoration is done similarly and with the same operation.
If both bones break simultaneously, the leg turns towards all directions.
And if the thinner bone breaks, the leg turns towards the front.
And if the larger bone breaks, you will find it below; and it is one of those that are not hidden from you.
Then apply extension, alignment, and star-shaped bandaging equally to both.
But if the fracture of the leg is a bad one with many displacements, then the extension should be less and more gentle in alleviating it.
And in the case of the leg, there is something special about its operation compared to that of the arm. Thus, when you have aligned the stars and completed all your operations, then take two splints of pinewood used for making roof beams, which are placed under boards; choose ones with a moderate thickness, not too thin or too thick, and their length should be equal to the length of the leg from the knee downwards.
Then wrap each splint twice with a bandage according to its length. And place one part of the leg on one side and the other part on the other side, from the knee down to the foot.
Then tie the two splints at three points: both ends and the middle. For when restrained, the leg inclines to the right and left, and remains in a good position.
And now a cradle made of wood according to the length of the leg should be applied, and the leg placed in it to keep it from moving.
But especially important is that you do this in cases where there is a wound accompanying the fracture.
Then observe the leg every day, and pay particular attention to it regarding any swelling, inflammation, or other signs of complications. If anything like this occurs, then apply the appropriate treatment until healing takes place, God willing.
On fractures of the foot and toes.
Indeed, the calcaneus does not break completely.
However, fractures do happen to the bones of the foot and toes, though they are rare; it is more common for them to be dislocated.
If a fracture occurs in the bones of the foot and you see that the bones are elevated above each other, then the patient should place their foot on the ground as if walking.
Then stand up and place your foot on the part that is elevated among those bones; afterwards press or step on it until it returns to its proper position.
Then apply a poultice and bandage it with linen strips; afterwards place a small board under the sole of the foot, which has two flat heads.
Then bind it firmly with the foot using an appropriate bandage, after having wrapped it with cloths and other necessary materials.
When three or four days have passed, then untie the bandage. For you will find that the bones are set in place, whether they were broken or dislocated. Therefore, the operation for them is as follows:
But if some toes are broken, then restore them and set them in place according to what I have told you regarding the restoration of fingers. Then place your finger on top of the toe in line with its length, and somewhat broader than it; next, place this board that I have told you about under the foot, and bind it firmly with an appropriate bandage.
But if two or more bones are broken, then place a splint of appropriate size on each toe, wrapped in linen cloth; bind the board under the sole of the foot and let it stand on one head of the board outside the sole of the foot to provide good support.
And remember that you should consider all the things I have told you regarding other fractures and apply appropriate treatment for each case.
Regarding the fracture of a woman's pubic bone, sternum, and male penis.
When the pubic bone of a woman is broken, then make her sit on a square cushion. Then tilt her slightly towards her back and hold her from behind.
Then let the midwife fill the woman's vagina with cotton until it is full, so that it forms a spherical shape within the vagina.
Then strike the woman gently and gradually lift her back upwards. For then the cotton will come out until it forms a spherical shape before the vaginal opening, and the broken bone will return to its proper place.
Then place a pillow on her back. When she wants to urinate, remove the cotton gently, then let her urinate. Afterwards, replace it according to how you inserted it initially, and then return to her original pillow.
She should do this for about seven days, as the bone itself regains its former shape.
And if you wish, take a bladder of a sheep and tie over the opening a reed tube, then insert the entire bladder into her vagina. Then blow air through the reed tube with force until the bladder inflates within her vagina, for this will heal the fracture.
Then fill it with cotton and let it stay for several days as we have described, until she is healed.
And when the sternum of a man or woman is broken, then treat it for restoration and alignment using the method we described for healing the bone at the ankle.
And it should not be forgotten that in these nearby fractures which occur rarely, the rectum is particularly affected, especially if there is some swelling or infection present.
And understand my book well, for the fracture itself indicates to you in many ways the correct path towards its restoration and healing. So understand it well.
When the virga (a stalk of a plant) of a man is broken, take the gullet of a goose and insert it into him. Then place over it a wrapping made of cloth, wrap it around, and let it remain for three days or thereabouts. It will be healed if God wills it.
On the restoration of bone fractures when accompanied by a wound
It is fitting for one who has suffered a fracture accompanied by a wound, especially if it is large, such as the thighbone or the arm bone, to act promptly and perform phlebotomy on him immediately: if you find the conditions suitable for bloodletting, as we have previously discussed.
But if the wound is flowing with blood, then you should act promptly to cauterize it, using powdered myrrh mixed with oil if nothing else is available.
Then begin to set the fracture on the same day itself; and do not delay it unless an abscess forms.
But if a hot abscess forms, then defer setting the fracture until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides. And do not approach him on the third and fourth days at all, for you would thereby cause him harm.
But if the broken bone is protruding through the skin and exposed, then you should attempt to reduce it manually with gentleness and slight extension.
If it cannot be prepared for reduction or made even by your hands, then use this instrument to reduce it. The instrument is made of iron and should be seven or eight digits in length, and its width should match the size of the wound. And it is necessary for the physician to take three or four pieces of this instrument according to the size needed for treating any kind of fracture.
And let it be round; and let its thickness be small so that it does not double over itself during compression at the time of operation; and let it have a sharp edge with a margin at both ends. And let its upper part gradually decrease in thickness towards its lower end.
And this is its shape.
It is necessary in this operation that the sharp curved end of this instrument be placed on the prominent end of the bone and push it with it continuously until the bone returns to its proper place and becomes aligned.
Therefore strive to align the ends of the fracture together.
But if the ends of the fracture are too thin and do not fit properly into the instrument, then it is necessary to cut off the end of that bone until the instrument can grasp it.
But if you cannot reduce them with the instrument we have described at all, then cut it with one of the knives that we mentioned or saw it off with any suitable saw.
Then shave what remains on the bone from its roughness and small scales.
Therefore, if after reducing the bone the patient feels severe pain afterwards, know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Do this. For you shall give a large splint to the patient when the restoration of the bone that you are performing is complete.
Then soak a cloth in black stypitic wine. And especially if it is summer time. Do not apply hot poultices or anything containing oil to the wound lest it cause putrefaction and decay therein.
Then administer bandages at the hour of completion of your restoration of the bone. And leave the wound open; thus, cut through the stitches with forceps according to the size of the wound.
And beware of all precautions not to close the wound in case of a fracture. For many times foolish physicians have done this and caused either death or gangrene (corrosion) or pus (zuchem), that is, corrupted blood. And let your suture be soft, gentle, diverse from the sutures of other fractures.
If the wound is bad or large and you fear some harmful accidents that have been mentioned, and if there is pain in the area, then do not apply cauterization (astellas). And make them compressions with hard cloths at the site of cauterization and tie it with them.
But when after one or two days you see that the wound is beginning to produce pus, then remove the cloth you placed on it with wine. Then apply linen bandages and ointments according to our custom for treating wounds, such as the ointment called Tetrapharmacon, and similar ones.
And it is appropriate for you to loosen the suture. And examine the wound every morning and evening until it heals.
It is necessary that the limb be prepared in such a way that pus flows easily downwards towards the lower parts.
But if many days pass and the wound does not heal nor is the pus discharged from it, then know that there are fragments of bone stuck in it. Therefore, you must explore the wound with probes to find out. Remove those fragments that are loose and extract them. And those which are not loose but stick into the limb and cause pain, try to cut them and remove them in any way you can.
But if gangrene or inflammation (ekileten), that is, fistulas or other signs of corruption and putrefaction appear on the wound, then apply to each of them what is suitable according to its treatment, whose explanation has preceded in its chapter.
And concerning that which it is necessary for my speech to dwell upon and to which you should give your full attention when a bone breaks and protrudes above the limb, such as the thighbone or elbow bone and similar bones of the major limbs, it is not advisable to attempt to reduce (attractionem eius) it too often, for many deaths have resulted from this. Rather, leave it alone until it becomes putrefied.
For perhaps it will fall back into place on its own after twenty or thirty days.
Therefore, if the wound appears inflamed to you, treat it; otherwise, leave it alone.
Chapter on the treatment of nonunion at the site of a bone fracture.
Nonunion often occurs at the site of a healed bone fracture. Especially those that occur near joints and cause deformity of the limb. And perhaps prevents the limb from functioning properly in its natural way.
Therefore, if the nonunion is recent, apply medicines that have astringent properties, such as aloe, frankincense (olibanum), myrrh (myrrha), tragacanth (sarcocolla), and acacia, and similar substances.
So that you take some or all of these and mix them with astrigent wine, egg white, or vinegar, and apply them on a wick to the nonunion, binding it securely.
And leave the dressing in place for several days without changing it. Then remove it and repeat with another dressing until the nonunion is resolved.
Or apply a lead plate appropriately shaped to fit over it. For lead has the property of drawing out all that is harmful from the limbs.
But if the nonunion has already become ossified and hardened, and urgent necessity demands its removal, then make an incision over it from above to expose the prominent bone. Then shave it off with suitable razors until it is removed, and treat the wound until it heals.
On Treatment: When it restores, but the limb remains afterwards thin, not according to its natural state.
When a fracture of the bone restores, but the limb remains afterwards thin and weak, this does not occur except on account of many causes. One cause of this is the excessive slackening and tightening of ligaments not according to what is proper; or because of an excess of binding of ligaments so that nourishment cannot reach the limb; or because of an excess of applications at a time when they are not appropriate; or because of a deficiency of blood in the body of the sick person, and its weakness.
And the treatment for this is to feed the sick person and make their body soft until there is an increase of blood in them. And administer a bath. And bring joy and pleasantness upon him.
Then apply pitch to the limb so that it may draw much nourishment towards itself. And persist with applications of warm water on it until nourishment flows and it returns to its natural shape.
On the Treatment of Fractures When They Heal Crookedly
When a limb has healed crookedly after the fracture, with prominence of the bone or with nonunion, although this disfigures the form of the limb, yet if it does not hinder its natural movement, then do not listen to anyone who advises that the limb should be broken again.
And many foolish physicians and bone-setters have done this in our land.
This operation is indeed very blameworthy, leading to death or great fear, so that the patient may die.
But if the limp and nonunion are recent, then it is necessary to apply warm water containing softening herbs such as hollyhock leaves and root, mallow, and others of that kind. And a poultice should be made for him with a softening plaster, like well-made tragacanth.
Or the mucilage of hollyhock root may be taken and beaten with chicken fat or sesame oil, and applied as a poultice.
Or figs may be taken and ground with pigeon dung and other substances that are known to reduce union.
And sometimes nonunion is dissolved by repeated gentle friction with moist hands. Movement of the affected limb should be encouraged towards all directions at all times.
But if the deformity has already occurred and become strong, and has turned into bone, and the need for surgical intervention with iron has arisen: then it is necessary to cut above it, dissolve the bony union, and cut away what is superfluous from the nonunion or bone using fine cutters, and ensure ease of movement therewith careful attention.
On the subject of dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of some articulation from its proper place. Therefore it is prevented from movement and the figure of the limb is deformed. And it causes severe pain and serious injuries to fall upon the weak.
When therefore dislocation occurs in someone, then it is necessary to hasten its restoration without delay.
For if delayed, the place becomes inflamed and perhaps the reduction of the dislocation will become difficult.
Therefore it is not necessary to move or stretch it during the hour of inflammation. For often it causes spasm and painful injuries.
But when this happens, then you must hurry to perform bloodletting on the patient. Then let him be left alone until the inflammation subsides.
Then apply warm water and oil to the limb. Then reduce it gently.
And treat all members with that for which there will be a reminder in its proper place.
I have already arranged the chapters on dislocations, also according to what preceded in fractures, from the upper part of the body down to its lower part.
Chapter: On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two jaws are rarely dislocated except seldom.
And dislocation of both occurs in one of two ways. Either they are moved slightly from their usual positions and kept there for a short time; Or they are completely dislocated until they become limp towards the chest area, so that the sick person cannot hold them and cannot close their mouth, and their tongue is involved with speech.
But when the dislocation is slight, it returns spontaneously in many cases with few complications.
But if the dislocation is complete and entire, then reduction must be administered quickly and not delayed at all. And it is necessary for an assistant to hold the patient's head while the physician inserts the thumb of one hand into the root of the dislocated jawbone between its teeth on one side; Or inserts both thumbs simultaneously if the dislocation is on both sides. And let the other fingers of his hands be outside where they may be helpful;
And instruct the patient to clean their teeth and open their mouth wide on all sides. Then the physician should gently push the jaw until it returns to its proper place;
But if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated simultaneously, then apply a warm vapor with water and oil until reduction becomes easy. Do not delay reducing them at all, as we have said.
When they return and are positioned correctly, and the patient's mouth is closed without limpness, then place over them small pads of linen with cerate made from wax and rose oil.
Then bind it gently with soft bandages.
And let the patient lie on their back with their head supported between two pillows so that they do not move it to the right or left and neither attempt nor are forced to chew anything; rather, they should be given a soft diet until the pain subsides and the jaw is bound again. Then they may eat what they wish, administered gently.
They should not exert themselves in eating, drinking, and yawning until the jaw is bound and healed.
But if the reduction of the two mandibles proves difficult when they are dislocated at some time and do not return to their proper places, then frequent feverish attacks may occur, along with continuous thirst, and perhaps the patient's abdomen will loosen and they may vomit bile.
Therefore, if you observe this [situation], then know that it is a sign of certain death, and often the person to whom it happens dies within ten days.
[On the Reduction of Dislocations of the Shoulder Joint and Arm Bone]
The shoulder blade does not become dislocated from its inner side due to its continuity with the chest. It may sometimes become dislocated outward and this is clearly apparent by sensation.
Its reduction can be achieved by having the patient lie on their back with arms extended. Then press firmly on the spot with your palm; it will return to its proper place.
Then apply a plaster and dressings, and bind it tightly.
The parts of the arm that follow after the elbow, namely the wrist and hand, rarely become dislocated unless there is some special cause for this. If it becomes dislocated on any given day, then it should be reduced and set right according to what has been said, with the necessary preparations made by you.
Then place upon it a plaster, bandages, and a dressing. And instruct the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until they are healed.
And with this same treatment, the head of the humerus is reduced when it becomes dislocated from its place as well.
Paragraph: On the reduction of dislocation of the arm.
Know that the humerus does not dislocate except in three ways.
One of which is for it to dislocate towards the armpit and downwards. The second is for it to dislocate towards the chest. And perhaps it may also dislocate at the upper part of the humerus, which happens rarely.
It does not dislocate backwards because of the shoulder blade. And it does not dislocate forwards due to the nerves.
Most commonly, however, it dislocates and exits downwards towards the armpit, especially in those whose flesh is sparse. For it goes out quickly and enters quickly in them.
In those whose flesh is abundant, however, it is the opposite: it goes out with difficulty and enters with difficulty.
And sometimes it happens to certain people that they are struck or fall, and the humerus becomes inflamed with heat, causing them to believe that it is already dislocated.
Therefore, you should examine it until you are certain of its condition, and then proceed with its treatment.
And dislocation is recognized when it occurs downwards towards the armpit; distinguish between a dislocated arm and one that is sound. For you will find a distinct difference between them: on the head of the humerus there is a depression, and beneath the armpit upon touch the head of the humerus feels as if it were an egg. The patient cannot reduce their hand to their ear nor move it in all ways.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest or upwards, then you will find a distinct touch, and it is not concealed.
Indeed, reduction of this dislocation is easy when it is recent or if the patient is a child.
And its reduction is such that the assistant raises their hand upwards. Then place your thumbs beneath the armpit of the patient and elevate the joint with force upwards to its proper place. And the assistant raises their hand and extends it upwards. Then let him place it below; for he returns quickly.
But if it does not return by what we have said, and many days have passed since the dislocation occurred at first: then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water, and an embrocation which softens and soothes be administered, such as if the roots of althea, and fennel, and melilot are cooked in water, and are administered.
Then the patient is made to lie on their back: and beneath the armpit of the patient there is placed a sphere and wool which is equal between softness and tension.
Then let the physician place his heel upon the sphere and elevate the head of the humerus with force, and draw the hand of the patient downwards.
And if you wish, reduce it in this manner: and do so by making the man who is present longer than the patient, and let him stand on the side of the patient's ribs and insert his humerus beneath the armpit of the patient; then elevate the armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in air. And another assistant should pull the patient's hand towards their lower abdomen. But if the patient is light in weight, then it is necessary to suspend with him something else to weigh him down, because dislocation occurs immediately.
And it is also reduced in another manner: and thus a long piece of wood should be placed on the ground, the head of which is round in shape like the eye of a millstone, neither too thick nor too thin. Then place it beneath the patient after placing soft cloths on top of the rounded head of the wood; and let the patient stand along the length of the wood.
Then extend his hand towards the ground on the other side and stretch out his body with force, because the joint will return to its place quickly.
But if the reduction of this is difficult through all that we have said, then use this treatment: and thus take a piece of wood whose length is two cubits (about 3 feet), and its width four fingers, and its thickness two fingers; and let its head be round in shape so as to ease entry into the depth of the armpit, according to this form.
Then soft cloths should be bound over the rounded head of the wood so that it does not harm the patient.
Then place it beneath the armpit of the patient so that his hand or arm is extended over the wood towards the ground; and let the wood be bound to the support, the arm, and the end of the hand.
Then place the arm over the step of a ladder in width and extend the hand towards the ground, letting the rest of the body hang suspended on the other side. For the joint enters immediately.
When therefore its entry is completed in whatever way possible, then you should place beneath the armpit a sphere of equal size made of wool. Next, apply an ointment prepared from flour dust with frankincense and egg white over the entire shoulder as it curves upwards.
Then tie beneath the armpit with a suitable binding; wrap the bandage over the ointment and attach his hand to his neck, allowing it to remain undisturbed for seven days.
And food should be given sparingly until the member is strengthened, as this will facilitate quicker healing.
Then after seven days or five, a larger amount of food should be given and exercise begun with movement. If it then becomes firm and not soft, he is now healed.
But if the joint is dislocated repeatedly due to moisture that has occurred or another cause, then a cautery with three holes should be applied there according to what was described earlier in the chapter on cauteries.
But if all this is done and the bandage is removed after seven days, and the joint remains unhealed and cannot be raised upwards, then know that his nerves leading to the head of the shoulder have either been cut or stretched or softened, and know that the joint will never again return to its proper place.
For dislocation towards the chest and nipple or reduction to the back, this occurs with force and extension using hands until it returns, and then the remaining binding and care should be applied until healing is complete.
