But if heaviness and slowness of movement occur in the limb after healing, then the patient should frequently use warm baths until that heaviness is eased and it returns to its original nature.
On the Treatment of Elbow Dislocation.
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty and also returns to its position with difficulty. It can be dislocated in all directions but most often towards the front or back.
And its dislocation will not escape your notice because it is evident both to sight and touch, regardless of what shape the dislocation takes. And when you reduce a dislocated elbow joint, it becomes plainly apparent. For the joint is hollowed out and cannot bend the arm nor touch its own humerus.
And you should hurry to reduce the dislocation immediately before a hot inflammation occurs.
For if a hot inflammation occurs, its reduction becomes difficult and perhaps it will not heal at all, especially when the dislocation is towards the back. It is worse than all other kinds of dislocations, causing intense pain and often leading to death.
And its reduction, when possible, is done by extending the patient's hand with both hands of the surgeon, keeping the arm expanded. The surgeon's hands should be placed above and below the elbow joint, and they should push the joint back into place using their palms or fingers until it returns to its proper position.
Or if the dislocation is towards the front, then reduction is achieved by bending the hand towards the palm until it strikes the dislocated elbow with the root of the surgeon's palm.
But if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then apply strong and forceful traction. Two assistants should extend the arm while two others hold it steady during traction.
Then rotate the arm in all directions after wrapping a long doubled cloth or broad bandage around the hand to prevent injury.
And when the surgeon touches the joint with his hand, he should anoint it with oil to make the reduction smoother and easier. Then push the joint forcefully until it returns to its place.
After reducing it, apply a plaster with absorbent properties containing egg white and tighten it with an appropriate bandage. And let the arm be suspended from the neck of the sick person; and leave it thus for some days.
Then, untie it. If however the joint remains firm in its place, then remove the bandage from it and leave it alone.
But if you see that the joint is not tightly bound, then repeat the plaster and bandage, and leave it thus for some days until it is properly bound. Then remove it.
But if heaviness occurs after the reduction of the joint and there is inflammation with movement: then apply moistening in bathing, gentle rubbing, and compression until it becomes straight, or apply over the hip joint, that is, fresh sheep's fat. Then bind it, and leave it thus day and night. Then remove it, and immerse the patient in a bath. When he begins to sweat, then rub the joint with a gentle rubbing motion. Then repeat over it an application of warm water once, twice, and thrice with each entry into the bath until it becomes softened.
And if you wish, apply over it the feces of a vaccuum that has been moistened and warmed with butter, and bind it tightly.
Do this several times. Thus it will become softened, and return to its natural state.
Prologue: On the treatment of dislocation at Al-Mahassen
Al-Mahassen hand is frequently dislocated, that is, the joint between the arm and the hand.
And its reduction is easy. Unlike other joints. However, speed is necessary in reducing the dislocation of this joint at the time it occurs, before the site becomes inflamed, otherwise a hot inflammation may occur there.
And the reduction of this dislocation is accomplished by placing the hand of the injured person on a board and extending its fingers by an assistant. The physician then places his palm over the prominence of the joint and presses it until it returns to its normal position.
However, it is necessary to consider whether the palm has already moved towards the belly of the hand. If so, the patient should place the back of their hand on the board during extension and reduction. And if the dislocation proceeds towards the back of the hand, then the position of the hand should be placed internally on the board, that is, the inner part of the hand should be placed on it, so that the doctor's hand falls upon the prominence of the joint.
If it returns immediately, then all is well; but if not, then bind it with a poultice to reduce the swelling and leave it alone without repeating the procedure.
For if it does not tolerate the reduction and you cannot effect it even after a number of days have passed, and yet the joint remains in its twisted state without causing harm to the patient except that the hand becomes softened and unable to grasp anything, then know that the nerves have either been cut or frayed.
Therefore, it is not advisable to do anything further but to burn it close to its site with a cautery. And perhaps this will be of no benefit.
But if Al-Mahassen does not return to its proper place, then apply over it the poultice that has been mentioned above. Then bind it and leave it for five days; afterwards untie it and allow free movement of the hand for exercise.
But if it lacks movement and becomes stiff, then soften it with warm water and rubbing until it becomes supple.
On the treatment of dislocation of fingers.
When any of the fingers is dislocated towards any direction, either towards the back or palm of the hand, then extend the finger and press the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns to its normal position.
Then support the head of the finger and suspend it towards the part where it was dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then suspend him and extend him until he is equal to the straight one on that same day.
Therefore, when it is night, bind him according to the description itself; and do not cease to loosen him during the day; and exercise him with movement; and bind him at night.
Do this for several days until he becomes strong.
And do likewise with him if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand; and bind him to that part, and perform your first operation on him until he heals.
And do likewise with him when it is dislocated onto other parts.
On the treatment of dislocations of the vertebrae of the spine.
When it happens to someone that the vertebrae of the spine or neck are completely dislocated, or many vertebrae are displaced from their places, then there is no treatment for it, since death approaches the sick person.
And the sign of this is that the discharge of the sick person goes out without his will and he cannot hold it in. And sometimes some of his limbs become soft, either both legs or both arms, or one of them, or a vertebra is displaced from its place.
For indeed, displacement often occurs because there are four parts involved in such dislocations.
Therefore, that which is displaced towards the back is called Kyphosis (Gibbositas).
But its treatment is that if kyphosis occurs from childhood, then there is no cure for it, nor is there any hope of recovery.
But in that which occurs from an accident or blow or similar causes, the ancients have already described the methods of treatment at length. Mostly it does not return without damage.
But I have abbreviated it from that which is lengthy because of its proximity to me and in accordance with my intent and explanation, and I have formed an instrument for it in a different way from what they showed and explained.
I say therefore that in the hump which occurs on the anterior part, there is no cure nor healing from it.
And similarly in that which happens on both sides.
And only those which occur on the back are cured by that which I tell: and it is thus, that the sick person should lie face down on a flat surface near the wall, with a wet cloth underneath to prevent harm to their chest.
Then a straight piece of wood should be placed in a trough on the ground at the head end of the bed and another at the foot end, but not fixed into the trough. And an assistant should hold one of the pieces of wood. And another assistant should hold the other.
Then wrap around the head of the sick person and under their armpits a soft, strong rope. Extend one end of the rope to the piece of wood at their head and tie it there.
Then tighten another rope above their ankles, above their knees, and around their calves. Then gather all the ropes together and tie them to the piece of wood at their feet.
Then all assistants should pull on the ropes towards their respective pieces of wood. The two pieces of wood should not be moved from their places but also should not be fixed as we have said.
And the physician should place his palms on the sacrum with force until it returns to its proper position or he should place a board on it. Then he should press his feet against the board until it returns.
But if it does not return by this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three cubits. And hollow out in the wall which we said is near the sick person a place into which the end of the board can fit. Then place the middle of the board upon the "Spondyle. And let the physician place his feet on the other end. And tie it firmly until the "Spondyle" tightens, and then return to its place.
Then if you wish, operate with the lathe which is wrapped around your hand. And it is like this: fix two pieces of wood into the ground at the head of the patient's foot, each one being a forearm in length; and likewise at his head.
And let the distance between each piece of wood be a palm's length: and now all holes have been made, through which the lathe runs; and let every piece of wood be firmly fixed into the ground so that it does not move at all.
And insert the round wood, and let the lathe upon which the bandage is rolled be placed into the two holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at its end is a hole into which the wooden stick with the length of a palm plus what is rolled upon it is fixed; and likewise on two other pieces of wood.
Then tie the bandages which you have tied on the chest of the patient with the lathe that is at his head; and tie the bandages which you have tied on his legs with the lathe that is at his feet.
Then let there be a helper at each lathe who turns his hand with the linen bandage around which the lathe revolves; and let the physician adjust the "Spondyle" according to what has been said.
This is the form of the lathe which follows.
Then after the "Spondyle" returns and the location is adjusted, then it is necessary to place an astringent plaster with egg white. Then pitch. Afterwards place on top of the plaster a star from a board, whose width is three fingers or about that length. And its length should be equal to the size that covers the swelling and also over the healthy part of the "Spondyle.
Then it shall be tied with the ligament we mentioned. And the patient should use a soft diet until they are healed.
But if there remains some prominence at the site even after healing, then one must apply softening and soothing medicines along with the application of the board we mentioned for a prolonged period. And now lead sheet is applied to that area.
Sometimes there appears a prominence at the last "Spondyle" of the back, and it is believed to be due to dislocation; this is the bone that is now prominent. Therefore, one should not attempt this treatment without careful consideration, for it may result in death.
On the Treatment of Dislocations of the Ankle.
Know that dislocations of the ankle and elbow do not occur except in cases of complete dislocation; they are not affected by slight displacement or concavity as other joints are.
And indeed the ankle joint is dislocated according to four parts. That is, because it can be dislocated either forwards or backwards.
Most often it is dislocated inwards; less commonly it is dislocated outwards or backwards.
And the treatment for inward dislocation of the ankle is as follows: when you align the healthy leg with the injured one, the injured leg will be longer; the knee will be higher than that of the healthy leg, and the injured person will not be able to bend their foot at the hip; there will be a swelling below the groin due to the head of the femur having reached that area.
The sign of outward dislocation of the ankle is that it has symptoms opposite to those mentioned above.
And the sign of forward dislocation of the ankle is that the healthy leg is extended but not bent without pain at the knee; if the person attempts to walk, they cannot do so with their foot protruding forwards; urine retention occurs, and there is swelling at the groin; during walking, they tend to land on their heel.
And the sign of backward dislocation of the hip is that the knee cannot be extended until the groin is bent; the affected leg appears shorter than the other; the groin feels soft and the head of the femur is located at the buttock area.
But the reduction of this dislocation may be achieved by considering whether it is an old dislocation that has already become fixed. Then if you attempt to reduce it and it remains dislocated, there is no cure for it at all. Therefore, do not attempt this procedure rashly.
However, in cases where the dislocation is recent and one of four possible types, then quickly attempt to achieve reduction by moving it both internally and externally, and left and right; perhaps it will reduce without needing further treatment.
But if this does not succeed, then prepare a strong assistant to extend the patient's leg from below, either with their hands or using a bandage tied above the knee; meanwhile another assistant should extend their hand from above so as to insert their hands beneath the buttocks. Then bind with a gentle bandage at the root of the hip and hold both ends tightly.
And let the extension be either from the front of the groin to the fork region or from behind to the dorsal side.
All this should happen simultaneously until the patient is lifted bodily off the ground and remains suspended.
This method of traction is common to all four types of dislocation.
If the dislocation reduces with this method, then that is good; but if not, then it is necessary to apply specific treatment for each type.
When reducing it specifically, if the dislocation is towards the inside, it is necessary that the patient be laid on their healthy side. Then place a bandage over the root of the hip, where the head of the hip bone is and the area under the groin. Then extend the bandage upwards from the groin towards the higher part of the body, to the fork of the rib cage.
Then another assistant takes both arms of the patient and pushes the prominence of the hip outward with great force. For it has now returned to its proper place.
And this method is easier than other methods of treatment for reducing this member.
But if the patient resists you and refuses to cooperate with this method of treatment completely, then it is necessary to bind both feet together with a strong, soft bandage over the two heels and both knees, so that each foot is four fingers' length apart, and the injured leg should be extended one finger's breadth longer than the other.
Then hang the patient over the top of a beam that is in the house, with their feet off the ground by two arm lengths.
Then order a strong young man to protect the head of the hip and another youth to hang from it with the patient. Another youth should push the hip joint back into place with force, for it will quickly return to its proper position.
But when the dislocation is towards the outside, it is necessary that the patient lie on a platform as described for those with humpbacks. And bind the bandage tightly around their injured leg and across their chest.
Then place one beam at his feet and another at his head. Then place another beam securely in the middle of the platform, over which wet cloths are wrapped to avoid hurting the patient. Place the beam between their thighs so that it does not pull them downwards during extension.
Then each assistant should extend towards their respective parts, and the physician should evenly reduce with his hand.
If they obey to the return, it will be good.
And if not, place on top of it another board and press it evenly as we said over the convexity. Nevertheless, the patient should lie on their healthy side.
And when the dislocation is towards the front, then the patient's leg should be extended quickly: this is done on the platform and the physician places the palm of their right hand over the patient's groin. Then grasp it with the other hand and with that grasp extend it downwards towards the knee joint.
And if the dislocation is towards the back: then it is not necessary to extend the patient downwards, rather they should be raised up from the ground. Instead, place them on a hard surface like when dislocating their ankle outwards, according to what we said about placing them between two chairs and facing upwards with extended ligaments.
And compression should be administered with a board over the place where the joint exits when reduction is completed as it should be.
The sign of the joint's return is not hidden from you: when you extend both of the patient's legs and see them equal, and the patient relaxes their leg and extends it without resistance, then know that the limb has been reduced properly. Then join the two thighs together and apply a plaster, securing it with a bandage made of cloth or a roller so that the ankle does not move to any part. Let the patient rest quietly for three or four days.
Then loosen the bandage and plaster and compare the leg with the other. If you find them equal in appearance, then know that the dislocation is now fixed. Let the patient walk without assistance.
And if you observe any softening: then return and apply a plaster to it, and bind it according to your first binding. Leave it like that for three days.
Then loosen it and make him walk slowly on both feet for several more days until he is well strengthened.
Chapter on Dislocation of the Knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It can be dislocated to the outside, inside, and downwards, that is, backwards.
And it does not dislocate forwards.
The sign of its dislocation is that when you tell the patient to apply their leg to their hip If it does not adhere to the hip, then know that the knee is dislocated.
And the reduction of all modes of its dislocation is that you make the patient sit upright so that they can already extend their leg if possible on top of it. Place your back against the patient's face and put their foot between yours.
Then press your knees with your palms and stretch them straight between your fingers on the knee of the patient Then press both sides of the knee with force using your palm And another assistant should extend the foot until the knee returns to its place
The sign of reduction is when the leg adheres to the hip gently, not with difficulty.
Then apply a plaster and make sure the leg adheres to the hip. Then bind it together with bandages for three or four days. Then loosen it.
And the patient should not walk for several days until they are healed.
If reduction is difficult for you, then perform strong traction with bandages, as previously described in the treatment of dislocations of the ankle, until it returns to its place.
Chapter on Treatment of Dislocation of the Heel.
The heel is sometimes moved by a slight displacement, and sometimes dislocated according to its completion. And its dislocation occurs either inward or outward.
The sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen and protruding towards the part to which it has been dislocated.
However, the treatment of its displacement is easy reduction: and it is that with ease you stretch it out with your hands, and make it even until it returns.
But in its treatment when it is dislocated according to completion, you should make the sick person sit upright: and have a strong servant hold him firmly from behind, in the middle of his back.
Then, take hold with your right hand of his foot from above it, and with your left hand below the ankle.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand. Then with the left [hand] using both fingers.
Then extend it with your left [hand], and press the front of the foot with your right towards the thigh without effort.
Do this twice as we have described.
Then press the front of the foot against the thigh for a third time, and you pull the heel.
If however it returns on the first or second attempt according to this method, and you see the foot even, that is good. But if not, then repeat the procedure upon it: for indeed it will return.
But if its reduction is prevented by what we have described, then make the sick person lie on his back upon the ground, and fix a peg firmly in the ground so that it falls between his thighs. And you wrap around him a cloth, so that it does not harm the sick.
Then let one servant take his thigh: afterwards another servant stretches out the other foot, either with his hands or with a bandage that he ties around the ankle. Then all servants stretch out against each other's stretching: and the peg stands between both thighs of the sick, holding him so that his body is not drawn downwards during the stretching.
Then let the physician reduce the dislocated [joint] with his hands, and another servant hold the healthy leg downward. For reduction occurs with speed.
When therefore the dislocation has been reduced, and you know that its reduction is sound: then apply a plaster and wick, and bind it firmly with bandages, and tie the foot to the leg.
And you must be careful not to cause injury or harm to the nerves which are above the back of the heel, lest a bandage be pressed upon them and wound them.
Then leave him for two days, or three. But if the bandage becomes too tight, loosen it: then remove it on the third or fourth day: and forbid the sick person from walking for forty days. But if he attempts to walk before that period, there is no assurance that the dislocation will not dissolve again, and be ruined, so that it will not recover afterwards.
But if an inflamed abscess should occur to him: then you must apply for its suppression that which preceded this account in another place concerning treatment, and poultices until it is removed.
On Dislocation of the Toes of the Foot, and Its Treatment.
It is necessary to reduce what has been dislocated among them with little effort through extension: and indeed this is not difficult, rather it is easy.
But if the dislocation is in certain joints of the back of the foot: then let the sick person sit with his foot on the ground, or on a board, and stand as if walking.
Then you stand and place your foot upon that which projects from those joints. And then press it with your foot with force until it returns and you see that it is reduced, and no prominence appears in its place.
Then place under the sole of its foot a board which receives the whole foot, having two heads. Then bind it with a proper bandage for three days. Then release it and cause him to rest from walking many days until it is bound up and secured against reduction.
On kinds of dislocations with wound or fracture.
When something happens to it and you attempt its cure and restoration, many times death follows; therefore, it is not appropriate for anyone to presume upon its care unless one who is experienced in the art with long use, merciful, pious, slow, neither rash nor presumptuous: and let him use at the beginning medicines that calm hot apostemes only: and let him leave the sick to divine judgment. Unless you see or hope for salvation from destruction with gentleness of sickness, and where there appears some hope. Therefore, strive to reduce it immediately at the beginning of the matter before a hot aposteme occurs.
If therefore the member returns as we desire, then administer a regimen that calms the aposteme: and cure the wound with what is suitable for it among drying ointments.
But if dislocation is with fracture, and splintered pieces occur in the bone, then strive for their removal, and do in it what we have said in simple sicknesses about that which preceded in the places of those.
And beware according to your power, and keep yourself from entering into the path of fear: according to what has preceded my preaching to you.
For that is better for you before your Creator, and more lasting for your glory, and safer for your body among them in your time.
